bridge is therefore no longer merely ``open''; it is open in a way that
already suggests where the nonlinear modeling mismatch accumulates.
The tangent metric is stricter than in earlier notes because the benchmark now
compares the same condensed section tangent
\(\partial M_y/\partial \kappa_y\vert_{N=\mathrm{const}}\) on both sides,
instead of mixing that quantity with a raw diagonal constitutive entry.

An even stronger control now exists on the exact same geometric specification:
the \emph{elasticized structural parity slice}.  There, both codes use the same
fiber cloud, the same station cloud, the same displacement-based beam family,
and the same protocol, but the concrete and steel ingredients are replaced by
linear-elastic uniaxial laws.  Under that control, the global and distributed
structural observables close very tightly for both OpenSees beam-column
comparators.  With the displacement-based comparator,
\[
\max \varepsilon_{V} \approx 5.17\times10^{-3},\qquad
\max \varepsilon_{M}^{\text{base}} \approx 5.17\times10^{-3},\qquad
\max \varepsilon_{N}^{\text{sec,path}} \approx 2.43\times10^{-15},
\]
while the force-based sensitivity path closes even tighter in the global
observable,
\[
\max \varepsilon_{V} \approx 1.32\times10^{-4},\qquad
\max \varepsilon_{M}^{\text{base}} \approx 1.32\times10^{-4},\qquad
\max \varepsilon_{N}^{\text{sec,path}} \approx 2.06\times10^{-11},
\]
and in both cases the section-tangent drift stays at about
\(1.65\times10^{-9}\).
This is a strong falsification result: once constitutive nonlinearity is
removed, the structural bridge closes tightly not only globally but also
station by station.  The remaining nonlinear gap is therefore no longer
credible evidence of spatial mismatch, beam-family mismatch, or observable
extraction error.  It is a genuinely nonlinear distributed-response mismatch.

That conclusion is now frozen through a dedicated structural formulation audit
bundle in
\texttt{data/output/cyclic\_validation/reboot\_structural\_formulation\_audit\_summary}.
The audit compares the four structurally relevant comparator slices
\[
  \{\texttt{elasticized},\texttt{nonlinear}\}
  \times
  \{\texttt{dispBeamColumn},\texttt{forceBeamColumn}\},
\]
all on the same cantilever geometry, the same \(92\)-fiber cloud, the same
three Gauss--Legendre stations, and the same guarded displacement protocol.
This audit makes three points explicit:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item the current \texttt{fall\_n} reduced structural slice is
    \texttt{TimoshenkoBeamN} with mixed interpolation and
    \texttt{DirectAssembly}; no element-level static condensation is active in
    this benchmark;
  \item the OpenSees structural comparators are
    \texttt{dispBeamColumn}/\texttt{forceBeamColumn} plus
    \texttt{SectionAggregator(Vy,Vz)}, so they are useful comparators but not
    literal clones of the \texttt{fall\_n} Timoshenko beam and not the
    explicit \texttt{elasticTimoshenkoBeam} family;
  \item under nonlinear materials, \texttt{forceBeamColumn} improves the
    station-wise axial-force consistency dramatically, but does \emph{not}
    close the global hysteresis mismatch.
\end{enumerate}

The elasticized formulation control is especially informative.  With
\texttt{dispBeamColumn}, the maximum global base-shear mismatch drops to about
\(5.17\times10^{-3}\); with \texttt{forceBeamColumn}, it drops further to
about \(1.32\times10^{-4}\).  In both elasticized cases, the section tangent
error remains at about \(1.65\times10^{-9}\), the section axial-force error is
at or below \(10^{-11}\), and the imposed-control observables remain essentially
exact.  Once constitutive nonlinearity is restored, the picture changes
qualitatively: the \texttt{forceBeamColumn} sensitivity path reduces the
station-wise axial-force drift from about \(7.47\times10^{-2}\) to about
\(7.56\times10^{-7}\) and reduces the section-tangent drift from about
\(1.47\) to about \(8.02\times10^{-1}\), but the global base-shear mismatch
actually worsens from about \(2.18\times10^{-1}\) to about
\(2.81\times10^{-1}\).  This is exactly the kind of result the reboot needs:
it says the axial-load path and the beam-assembly route are no longer the main
unknowns, and it points the remaining frontier back to nonlinear preload plus
reversal response, consistent with the tracked extremal \texttt{cover\_top}
concrete fiber.

That same comparison also changes how the station-wise structural section
bundle should be interpreted. For the declared nonlinear parity anchor
\texttt{dispBeamColumn + LinearCrdTransf}, pointwise section-force and
condensed-tangent traces are no longer treated as unconditional acceptance
gates. They remain valuable \emph{diagnostic localizers} of distributed
nonlinear drift, but they must be read under a family-specific equilibrium
contract: the displacement-based OpenSees comparator is weak-equilibrium at
station level, whereas \texttt{forceBeamColumn} provides an equilibrium-richer
sensitivity path. This distinction matters scientifically because it prevents
the benchmark from misclassifying a formulation-dependent local trace as if it
were already a family-independent physical observable.

This same audit also clarifies what OpenSees does and does not already offer.
OpenSees does provide an explicit \texttt{ElasticTimoshenkoBeam} element for
elastic shear-flexible controls, and it also provides the
\texttt{LinearCrdTransf} and \texttt{Corotational} transformation layers for
beam-column elements \cite{OpenSeesElasticTimoshenkoBeamWiki,OpenSeesDispBeamColumnDocs,OpenSeesForceBeamColumnDocs,OpenSeesCorotationalCrdTransfDocs}.
However, the current nonlinear reduced-column bridge still has to pass through
\texttt{dispBeamColumn}/\texttt{forceBeamColumn}, because those are the audited
OpenSees nonlinear beam-column routes tied to fiber sections and beam
integration. The consequence is methodological rather than polemical:
\texttt{ElasticTimoshenkoBeam} is a valuable additional elastic control, but it
is not a drop-in nonlinear parity anchor for the present RC-column benchmark.
Once that control is calibrated against the exported elastic section stiffness
of \texttt{fall\_n}, however, it closes the structural elastic benchmark almost
exactly: the global base-shear and base-moment drifts both drop to about
\(1.32\times10^{-4}\), the section tangent and raw \(K_{00}\) drifts collapse
to zero at machine precision, and the tip-drift control trace closes exactly.
This is a useful scientific result because it shows the Timoshenko beam theory
itself is not what is blocking the nonlinear benchmark.

That distinction lets the thesis start stating some \texttt{fall\_n}
advantages in a verifiable way instead of as a slogan. On the present audited
slice, \texttt{fall\_n} already offers a compile-time typed
mixed-interpolation Timoshenko path, an explicit quadrature family as a model
axis, direct export of raw and condensed section tangent blocks
\((K_{00},K_{0y},K_{yy})\), and protocol/reversal/fiber diagnostics inside the
same code base. Its runtime is already competitive, but not uniformly better:
on the elasticized displacement-based parity slice, \texttt{fall\_n} reports a
total wall time of about \(0.180\,\mathrm{s}\) versus
\(0.216\,\mathrm{s}\) for OpenSees; on the stiffness-equivalent
\texttt{ElasticTimoshenkoBeam} control it reports about
\(0.161\,\mathrm{s}\) versus \(0.184\,\mathrm{s}\); whereas on the nonlinear
displacement-based slice OpenSees is slightly faster
(\(0.228\,\mathrm{s}\) versus \(0.269\,\mathrm{s}\)). The honest read is
therefore stronger and narrower at the same time: \texttt{fall\_n} is already
competitive in cost while exposing a richer and more auditable formulation
surface.

This also sharpens the next architectural step. Two beam-family extensions are
now worth treating explicitly as deferred growth paths after the reference
reduced column is closed: a \emph{force-based Timoshenko} family for nonlinear
distributed plasticity under shear-flexible kinematics, and a
\emph{geometrically exact} beam family of Simo--Reissner type for large
rotations and objective beam kinematics
\cite{Spacone1996_FibreBeamColumnModel,Li2013_FiberSectionTimoshenkoElementOpenSees,Meier2019_GeometricallyExactFiniteElementFormulations}.
The important methodological point is that neither family is now hidden behind
the current benchmark; both are declared growth paths with their own evidence
burden.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.88\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_structural_formulation_audit_errors.pdf}
  \caption{Canonical structural formulation audit for the reduced RC-column bridge.  The elasticized slices close tightly for the two OpenSees beam-column comparators and for the stiffness-equivalent \texttt{ElasticTimoshenkoBeam} control, while the nonlinear slices remain open.  The key asymmetry is informative: \texttt{forceBeamColumn} reduces the section axial-force and tangent mismatch, but does not close the global hysteresis response.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-opensees-structural-formulation-audit}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.76\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_external_structural_hysteresis.pdf}
  \caption{Preliminary structural hysteresis overlay for the reduced RC-column benchmark under the declared \texttt{cyclic-diagnostic} bridge after correcting the base-shear sign convention and moving the external structural reference to the displacement-based parity anchor \texttt{dispBeamColumn + LinearCrdTransf}. The bundle remains open in amplitude, but the residual gap is no longer attributable to sign convention, fiber-cloud mismatch, or section-station mismatch.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-opensees-structural-hysteresis}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.82\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_external_structural_section_path_by_station.pdf}
  \caption{Station-by-station structural section-path overlay for the same reduced-column benchmark.  The mismatch is no longer spatially diffuse: the loaded-end station carries the largest moment/curvature drift, while the base station carries the strongest tangent discrepancy.  This figure makes explicit that the residual structural gap is already visible within the distributed nonlinear section history.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-opensees-structural-section-path}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.82\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_external_structural_control_trace.pdf}
  \caption{Boundary-control audit for the same reduced-column benchmark.  The imposed lateral displacement path and the maintained base axial reaction close essentially exactly between \texttt{fall\_n} and OpenSees, while the top axial shortening already diverges at the preload-equilibrated state.  This makes the current residual gap much harder to misread: the dominant discrepancy is in the nonlinear preload/section response, not in the Dirichlet enforcement itself.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-opensees-structural-control-trace}
\end{figure}

\paragraph{Section-level external bridge.}
The structural benchmark above still mixes three effects that should not be
confounded: constitutive mapping, fiber-section discretization, and beam-level
distributed response. To isolate the first two, the reboot now also carries a
section-only external bridge based on the same OpenSeesPy script in
\texttt{--model-kind section} mode plus the new orchestrator
\texttt{scripts/run\_reduced\_rc\_column\_section\_external\_benchmark.py}.
On the \texttt{fall\_n} side, the matching internal runner is
\texttt{fall\_n\_reduced\_rc\_column\_section\_reference\_benchmark}. Both
paths drive the same reduced RC section under constant axial compression plus
monotonic curvature control, and both now report compute time explicitly.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=bash,caption={Canonical section-level \texttt{fall\_n} vs OpenSeesPy benchmark for the reduced RC reboot.},label={lst:validation-reboot-section-external-benchmark-run}]
python scripts/run_reduced_rc_column_section_external_benchmark.py \
  --output-dir data/output/cyclic_validation/reboot_external_section_benchmark_policy_smoke \
  --mapping-policy monotonic-reference \
  --axial-compression-mn 0.02 \
  --max-curvature-y 0.03 \
  --section-steps 40
\end{lstlisting}

This section bridge immediately exposed a real implementation bug: for
\texttt{zeroLengthSection}, OpenSees returns \emph{nodal end forces} in
\texttt{eleResponse(..., "force")}, not the condensed section-force vector.
Reading that response as if it were \([P,M_z,M_y,\dots]\) produced an apparent
axial-force error of \(100\%\). After correcting the mapping to the base-end
nodal force/moment components induced by the chosen orientation, the section
benchmark became much more informative.

On the current smoke slice
\((N_{\kappa}=40,\ 0.02\,\text{MN axial compression},\
\kappa_y^{\max}=3.0\times10^{-2})\), the section-level comparison now shows
\[
\max \varepsilon_{N} \approx 3.9\times10^{-7},\qquad
\operatorname{rms}\varepsilon_{N} \approx 8.7\times10^{-8},
\]
which is essentially exact axial-force closure between the two codes at this
level. The flexural comparison is not yet closed, but it is materially better
diagnosed than before:
\[
\max \varepsilon_{M} \approx 5.53\times10^{-1},\qquad
\operatorname{rms}\varepsilon_{M} \approx 1.04\times10^{-1},\qquad
\max \varepsilon_{K_t} \approx 6.62\times10^{-1},\qquad
\operatorname{rms}\varepsilon_{K_t} \approx 1.44\times10^{-1}.
\]
This matters scientifically. The reduced-column \texttt{fall\_n} vs OpenSees
gap is no longer dominated by an ambiguous axial inconsistency at section
level. The remaining frontier is now sharper: early-branch flexural response
and tangent mismatch in the external constitutive/section bridge, followed only
then by beam-level structural effects. The timing observable is also already
useful: under the declared \texttt{monotonic-reference} policy,
\texttt{fall\_n} reports about \(6.93\times10^{-2}\,\text{s}\) total wall
time, whereas OpenSeesPy reports about \(1.67\times10^{-1}\,\text{s}\).

The same bridge now also supports a short cyclic section bundle:
\begin{lstlisting}[language=bash,caption={Short cyclic section-level \texttt{fall\_n} vs OpenSeesPy benchmark used to separate constitutive mismatch from beam-level continuation effects.},label={lst:validation-reboot-section-external-benchmark-cyclic-run}]
python scripts/run_reduced_rc_column_section_external_benchmark.py \
  --analysis cyclic \
  --output-dir data/output/cyclic_validation/reboot_external_section_benchmark_cyclic_spatial_parity \
  --mapping-policy cyclic-diagnostic \
  --axial-compression-mn 0.02 \
  --max-curvature-y 0.03 \
  --section-amplitudes-curvature-y 0.01 \
  --steps-per-segment 4 \
  --max-bisections 4
\end{lstlisting}

That short cyclic section bundle is already scientifically useful.  On the
current audited slice,
\[
\max \varepsilon_{M}^{\mathrm{sec,cyc}} \approx 5.91\times10^{-2},\qquad
\operatorname{rms}\varepsilon_{M}^{\mathrm{sec,cyc}} \approx 3.59\times10^{-2},\qquad
\max \varepsilon_{K_t}^{\mathrm{sec,cyc}} \approx 7.31\times10^{-1},\qquad
\operatorname{rms}\varepsilon_{K_t}^{\mathrm{sec,cyc}} \approx 1.85\times10^{-1},
\]
while the axial-force closure remains essentially exact,
\[
\max \varepsilon_{N}^{\mathrm{sec,cyc}} \approx 1.10\times10^{-7}.
\]
The large tangent maximum is no longer a convention artifact.  Once both
sides are forced to report the same condensed tangent
\(\partial M_y/\partial \kappa_y\vert_{N=\mathrm{const}}\), the remaining
peak localizes at the preload state and, more sharply, at the exact return to
zero curvature after reversal.  Away from those two singular states, the
nonzero-curvature branch tangents are much closer.
This can now be stated quantitatively instead of visually only.  If the same
bundle is split into nonzero-curvature branch points and zero-curvature anchor
states, the branch-only tangent mismatch drops to
\[
\max \varepsilon_{K_t,\mathrm{branch}}^{\mathrm{sec,cyc}}
\approx 6.72\times10^{-2},\qquad
\operatorname{rms}\varepsilon_{K_t,\mathrm{branch}}^{\mathrm{sec,cyc}}
\approx 3.12\times10^{-2},
\]
while the zero-curvature anchors retain the large peak.  The newly exported
diagnostic CSVs
\texttt{fall\_n/section\_tangent\_diagnostics.csv} and
\texttt{opensees/section\_tangent\_diagnostics.csv} also show that the left and
right numerical slopes around the reversal-return state remain positive on
both sides.  In other words, the remaining anomaly is no longer best read as a
sign inversion.  It is a localized discrepancy in the axial-flexural
condensation at the reversal anchor: OpenSees reduces
\(K_{yy}\approx 4.31\) down to an effective tangent of about \(1.68\) because
\(K_{0y}\approx K_{y0}\approx 50.6\) and \(K_{00}\approx 975\), while
\texttt{fall\_n} reduces \(K_{yy}\approx 7.45\) only to about \(6.27\) with a
milder coupling \(K_{0y}\approx K_{y0}\approx 44.8\) and
\(K_{00}\approx 1699\).
At that point the investigation was pushed one layer deeper, to the
fiber-history level.  That audit found a second convention issue hidden below
the section-level \(M\)-\(\kappa\) overlay: under the raw OpenSees fiber
readout, positive reported \(\kappa_y\) was compressing the opposite cover
face from the one implied by the \texttt{fall\_n} plane-section convention
\(\varepsilon_x = \varepsilon_0 - z\,\kappa_y\).  The comparison layer now
therefore projects the OpenSees fiber history into the declared
\texttt{fall\_n} curvature/cover convention before matching fibers spatially.
Once that convention mismatch is removed, the apparent fiber-level gap drops
substantially:
\[
\max \varepsilon_{\sigma}^{\mathrm{fiber}} \approx 2.59\times10^{-1},\qquad
\operatorname{rms}\varepsilon_{\sigma}^{\mathrm{fiber}} \approx 9.72\times10^{-2},
\]
\[
\max \varepsilon_{E_t}^{\mathrm{fiber}} \approx 1.00,\qquad
\operatorname{rms}\varepsilon_{E_t}^{\mathrm{fiber}} \approx 1.00\times10^{-1},
\]
with the zero-curvature anchors still carrying the dominant residual mismatch.
This changes the scientific interpretation again: the remaining section-level
gap is no longer best read as a global orientation error, but as a localized
cyclic residual-state mismatch that survives after the convention fix, mainly
through the cover-concrete contribution at the reversal-return anchor.
The same bundle now also makes the control schedule itself explicit through
\texttt{section\_control\_trace.csv} on both sides.  This closes a second
methodological doubt with direct evidence.  The declared benchmark path is now
matched essentially exactly in the variables that actually matter physically:
target curvature, achieved curvature, curvature increment, and benchmark
pseudo-time.  On the audited short cyclic section slice, the bundle reports
\[
\max \varepsilon_{\kappa,\mathrm{target}}
\approx 2.31\times10^{-16},\qquad
\max \varepsilon_{\kappa,\mathrm{actual}}
\approx 2.31\times10^{-16},\qquad
\max \varepsilon_{\Delta\kappa}
\approx 8.67\times10^{-16},
\]
with zero relative error in the pseudo-time trace.

The guarded continuation policy is therefore no longer an unresolved suspect
for this slice.  Both routes accept the same physical substep schedule, with
\[
\max |\Delta n_s| = 0,\qquad
\max |\Delta b| = 0,
\]
that is, one accepted increment per non-initial state and zero bisections
throughout the audited bundle.  What does remain different is the iterative
effort \emph{within} that shared schedule: the Newton iteration counts differ
on \(15/17\) common states, with
\[
\max |\Delta i_{\mathrm{Newton}}| = 4,\qquad
\operatorname{rms}|\Delta i_{\mathrm{Newton}}| \approx 2.03.
\]
That is an important scientific refinement.  The remaining tangent/anchor gap
is now occurring under the same physical benchmark path, but the two codes are
still traversing that path with different local nonlinear work.

The OpenSees domain time should therefore not be interpreted as a physically
comparable benchmark variable here.  Under static
\texttt{DisplacementControl}, OpenSees advances an internal pseudo-time/load
state tied to the active reference-load patterns
\cite{OpenSeesWebsite,OpenSeesPyDocs}.  On the same audited short cyclic slice,
its reported \texttt{domainTime} becomes large and non-monotone even while the
benchmark pseudo-time remains exactly aligned:
\[
t_{\mathrm{domain}}^{\mathrm{final}} \approx 3.52\times10^{1},\qquad
\max |t_{\mathrm{domain}}-p| \approx 3.85\times10^{4},\qquad
\max |\Delta t_{\mathrm{domain}}-\Delta p| \approx 1.08\times10^{4}.
\]
The correct reading is therefore narrow and useful: the compared
\emph{physical} step schedule is already closed, while OpenSees domain time is
only a diagnostic of the internal static integrator state.
The control-trace contract now makes that recommendation operational instead
of only rhetorical. Both routes export
\texttt{target\_increment\_direction},
\texttt{actual\_increment\_direction},
\texttt{protocol\_branch\_id},
\texttt{reversal\_index},
\texttt{branch\_step\_index}, and
\texttt{newton\_iterations\_per\_substep} together with pseudo-time and
accepted-substep counts. On the audited short cyclic section slice, these
protocol semantics close exactly between \texttt{fall\_n} and OpenSees. The
remaining solver-effort difference therefore cannot be explained by a hidden
non-monotone branch agenda.
The timing side is also now part of the benchmark bundle:
\texttt{fall\_n} reports about $2.04\times10^{-2}\,\mathrm{s}$ total wall
time, while OpenSeesPy reports about $1.77\times10^{-1}\,\mathrm{s}$, so the
reported-total ratio is about $1.15\times10^{-1}$. This changes the
interpretation sharply: the structural cyclic gap can no longer be attributed
only to beam continuation, section-station placement, or
drift-to-curvature extraction. The nonlinear section bridge is itself already
open under cyclic reversal, but once the section layout and station parity are
audited explicitly the mismatch becomes clearly moderate rather than
catastrophic at section level, with the strongest tangent drift concentrated at
the reversal return state rather than spread uniformly over the whole loop.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_external_section_monotonic_moment_curvature.pdf}
  \end{minipage}\hfill
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_external_section_cyclic_moment_curvature.pdf}
  \end{minipage}
  \caption{Preliminary section-level overlays for the reduced RC benchmark. Left: monotonic \texttt{monotonic-reference} slice. Right: short cyclic \texttt{cyclic-diagnostic} slice. The contrast is informative: axial closure remains essentially exact in both, while the cyclic flexural branch opens well before beam-level distributed-response effects are reintroduced.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-opensees-section-overlays}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.78\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_external_section_cyclic_tangent_consistency.pdf}
  \caption{Direct-versus-numerical tangent audit for the short cyclic section benchmark.  The high tangent error is now seen to be strongly localized at the zero-curvature anchors; along the nonzero-curvature branches, the direct and numerical tangents stay close on both sides.  The reversal-return point is therefore better interpreted as a localized axial-flexural condensation discrepancy than as a global sign inversion.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-opensees-section-tangent-consistency}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.80\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_external_section_cyclic_control_trace_fiber_anchor_time_audit.pdf}
  \caption{Control-trace audit for the short cyclic section benchmark.  The target and achieved curvature schedules close essentially exactly between \texttt{fall\_n} and OpenSees, and the accepted substep/bisection schedule is identical.  The iterative work is still different, however, as seen in the Newton-iteration trace.  The OpenSees domain time is intentionally shown only as an internal diagnostic: it is not a physically comparable benchmark time under static displacement control.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-opensees-section-control-trace}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.82\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_external_section_cyclic_anchor_zone_contributions_fiber_anchor_audit.pdf}
  \caption{Zone-wise contribution audit at the worst zero-curvature anchor of the cyclic section benchmark, after projecting the OpenSees fiber history into the declared \texttt{fall\_n} curvature/cover convention.  The large apparent top-versus-bottom discrepancy seen in the raw fiber readout collapses under that convention fix, and the remaining gap becomes much more specific: a localized residual-state mismatch dominated by cover-concrete contributions rather than a global sign error over the whole section.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-opensees-section-anchor-zone-contributions}
\end{figure}

To keep that diagnosis honest, the same bridge now also carries an
\emph{elasticized parity slice} in which both codes retain the same fiber
layout but replace the nonlinear concrete/steel ingredients by linear-elastic
uniaxial laws.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=bash,caption={Elasticized parity control for the section-level \texttt{fall\_n} vs OpenSeesPy benchmark.},label={lst:validation-reboot-section-external-benchmark-elastic-run}]
python scripts/run_reduced_rc_column_section_external_benchmark.py \
  --output-dir data/output/cyclic_validation/reboot_external_section_benchmark_elastic_policy_smoke \
  --material-mode elasticized \
  --mapping-policy elasticized-parity \
  --axial-compression-mn 0.02 \
  --max-curvature-y 0.03 \
  --section-steps 40
\end{lstlisting}

That control closes essentially at machine precision on the current audited
runtime:
\[
\max \varepsilon_{M} \approx 4.0\times10^{-9},\qquad
\max \varepsilon_{K_t} \approx 1.65\times10^{-9},\qquad
\max \varepsilon_{N} \approx 5.2\times10^{-14}.
\]
This is a strong result. It means that the remaining nonlinear section gap is
no longer plausibly attributable to section geometry, fiber placement, axis
mapping or observable extraction. Those surfaces are now effectively frozen by
the elasticized parity slice. The scientific frontier contracts accordingly to
the nonlinear constitutive bridge itself, especially the mapping from
\texttt{KentParkConcrete}/\texttt{MenegottoPintoSteel} to the external
\texttt{Concrete02}/\texttt{Steel02} family and the way that mapping shapes the
early flexural branch and tangent response.

That constitutive frontier is now isolated explicitly through a uniaxial
material bridge built from \texttt{fall\_n\_reduced\_rc\_material\_reference\_benchmark},
\texttt{scripts/opensees\_reduced\_rc\_material\_reference.py}, and the
orchestrator \texttt{scripts/run\_reduced\_rc\_material\_external\_benchmark.py}.
Unlike the section benchmark, this bridge does not reintroduce any beam or
fiber-geometry effect on the OpenSees side: it uses the OpenSees material
testing environment directly through \texttt{testUniaxialMaterial}.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=bash,caption={Canonical uniaxial-material \texttt{fall\_n} vs OpenSeesPy benchmark bundle.},label={lst:validation-reboot-material-external-benchmark-run}]
python scripts/run_reduced_rc_material_external_benchmark.py \
  --output-dir data/output/cyclic_validation/reboot_external_material_benchmark_smoke \
  --python-launcher "py -3.12" \
  --steps-per-branch 24
\end{lstlisting}

The current audited bundle already separates what closes from what remains
open. On the monotonic steel path, the bridge closes essentially exactly:
\[
\max \varepsilon_{\sigma}^{\text{steel,mono}} \approx 1.54\times10^{-6},\qquad
\max \varepsilon_{K_t}^{\text{steel,mono}} \approx 3.24\times10^{-5},\qquad
\max \varepsilon_{\Psi}^{\text{steel,mono}} \approx 4.39\times10^{-7}.
\]
By contrast, the guarded two-level cyclic steel protocol remains sharply open:
\[
\max \varepsilon_{\sigma}^{\text{steel,cyc}} \approx 1.29\times10^{1},\qquad
\max \varepsilon_{K_t}^{\text{steel,cyc}} \approx 9.90\times10^{-1},\qquad
\max \varepsilon_{\Psi}^{\text{steel,cyc}} \approx 9.93\times10^{1}.
\]
The concrete bridge is also still materially different under the current
external mapping:
\[
\max \varepsilon_{\sigma}^{\text{conc,mono}} \approx 1.19,\qquad
\max \varepsilon_{K_t}^{\text{conc,mono}} \approx 3.28\times10^{-1},
\]
\[
\max \varepsilon_{\sigma}^{\text{conc,cyc}} \approx 3.26,\qquad
\max \varepsilon_{K_t}^{\text{conc,cyc}} \approx 1.10\times10^{1},\qquad
\max \varepsilon_{\Psi}^{\text{conc,cyc}} \approx 3.04\times10^{-1}.
\]

This is the key scientific interpretation of the current benchmark ladder:
monotonic steel already aligns essentially exactly, whereas the unresolved
section-level nonlinear gap must be attributed primarily to cyclic steel
history and to the concrete mapping, not to fiber geometry or observable
extraction. The operational policy therefore stays conservative: the canonical
cyclic steel benchmark is frozen at a guarded two-level protocol because the
more aggressive multi-level Steel02 material-testing path becomes non-finite on
the audited OpenSeesPy runtime and is therefore not treated as a valid closure
reference.

The current operational policy is deliberately conservative.
\begin{enumerate}
  \item Continuous integration runs the script only in \texttt{--dry-run} mode,
    so the manifest/CSV contract is kept under regression without making
    OpenSeesPy a hard dependency of the core build.
  \item The real execution path is audited separately.  In the present local
    audit dated April 16, 2026, a monotonic smoke run and a short cyclic smoke
    run both completed successfully under Windows with Python 3.12 and
    OpenSeesPy 3.8.x.  The monotonic smoke bundle emitted \(3\) hysteresis
    points and \(9\) section records; the short cyclic smoke bundle emitted
    \(7\) hysteresis points and \(21\) section records.
  \item The scientific meaning of that success is still limited: it proves that
    the external computational bridge is executable and observable-compatible,
    not that the reduced-column benchmark is already physically validated.
\end{enumerate}

This distinction matters.  The bridge is strong enough to detect implementation
drift, protocol mismatch, or gross section-response inconsistency across two
independent code bases.  It is \emph{not} strong enough, by itself, to close the
experimental benchmark gate.  In the reboot logic, it is therefore a
methodological bridge between internal closure and later physical/literature
closure, not a shortcut around that final obligation.

That same constitutive bridge is now followed by an explicit
\emph{mapping-audit} layer.  The new runner
\texttt{scripts/run\_reduced\_rc\_material\_mapping\_audit.py} keeps the
comparison at uniaxial level and asks a sharper question: is the remaining gap
mainly a parameter-selection issue inside a reasonable OpenSees material
family, or a genuine family-level constitutive mismatch?

\begin{lstlisting}[language=bash,caption={Constitutive mapping audit over external OpenSees material families for the reduced RC reboot.},label={lst:validation-reboot-material-mapping-audit-run}]
python scripts/run_reduced_rc_material_mapping_audit.py \
  --output-dir data/output/cyclic_validation/reboot_material_mapping_audit_smoke \
  --python-launcher "py -3.12" \
  --steps-per-branch 24
\end{lstlisting}

The current audited bundle already yields a useful decision. For cyclic steel,
a moderate \texttt{Steel02} grid search over \((R_0,cR_1,cR_2)\) improves the
bridge but does not close it. The best audited profile,
\((30,\ 8,\ 0.3)\), reduces the RMS errors to approximately
\[
\operatorname{rms}\varepsilon_{\sigma}^{\text{steel,cyc}} \approx 1.67,\qquad
\operatorname{rms}\varepsilon_{K_t}^{\text{steel,cyc}} \approx 3.65\times10^{-1},\qquad
\operatorname{rms}\varepsilon_{\Psi}^{\text{steel,cyc}} \approx 4.75,
\]
which is materially better than the default \texttt{Steel02} bridge, yet still
far from a credible closure reference. The conclusion is therefore precise:
the cyclic steel gap is not merely a bad choice of transition parameters inside
the current external family.

For concrete, the reading is different. Among the currently audited external
profiles, the default \texttt{Concrete02} mapping remains the best monotonic
bridge, while a reduced-tension \texttt{Concrete02} profile currently provides
the best cyclic diagnostic bridge:
\[
\operatorname{rms}\varepsilon_{\sigma}^{\text{conc,mono}} \approx 4.53\times10^{-1},\qquad
\operatorname{rms}\varepsilon_{K_t}^{\text{conc,mono}} \approx 2.46\times10^{-1},
\]
\[
\operatorname{rms}\varepsilon_{\sigma}^{\text{conc,cyc}} \approx 7.05\times10^{-1},\qquad
\operatorname{rms}\varepsilon_{K_t}^{\text{conc,cyc}} \approx 3.80\times10^{-1},\qquad
\operatorname{rms}\varepsilon_{\Psi}^{\text{conc,cyc}} \approx 1.36\times10^{-1}.
\]
This does not close the concrete bridge either, but it does refine the next
section-level policy:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item retain the no-tension \texttt{Concrete02} plus baseline
    \texttt{Steel02} pair as the declared
    \texttt{monotonic-reference} bridge;
  \item retain the reduced-tension \texttt{Concrete02} plus tuned
    \texttt{Steel02} pair as the current \texttt{cyclic-diagnostic} bridge;
  \item do \emph{not} treat tuned \texttt{Steel02} as sufficient cyclic-steel
    closure yet.
\end{enumerate}

Scientifically, this is an important narrowing of the problem. The next
section-level \texttt{fall\_n} vs OpenSees comparison no longer needs to ask
blindly whether ``the whole section matches''; it can ask, much more honestly,
whether the section remains consistent \emph{once the external constitutive
mapping policy has been declared explicitly from the uniaxial audit}.

That localization is now tighter still through
\texttt{scripts/run\_reduced\_rc\_problematic\_fiber\_replay\_audit.py},
which replays the exact strain history of the most problematic audited section
fiber through both uniaxial bridges.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=bash,caption={Exact replay of the most problematic audited section fiber through the \texttt{fall\_n} and OpenSees uniaxial bridges.},label={lst:validation-reboot-problematic-fiber-replay-run}]
python scripts/run_reduced_rc_problematic_fiber_replay_audit.py \
  --output-dir data/output/cyclic_validation/reboot_problematic_fiber_replay_audit
\end{lstlisting}

The current audited replay bundle makes the remaining gap much more specific.
The selected fiber sits at the reversal-return anchor (\(step=8\)) in the
\texttt{cover\_top} unconfined-concrete zone. After projecting the OpenSees
fiber cloud into the declared \texttt{fall\_n} convention, the two section
codes differ only mildly in that fiber strain history, with
\[
\max |\Delta \varepsilon_{\mathrm{fiber}}| \approx 5.02\times10^{-5}.
\]
Yet the uniaxial responses do not collapse to the same state under that same
near-zero compressive strain. On the exact \texttt{fall\_n} replay history,
\texttt{fall\_n} returns approximately
\(\sigma \approx 0\ \mathrm{MPa}\) and
\(E_t \approx 3.0\times10^{-2}\ \mathrm{MPa}\),
whereas OpenSees \texttt{Concrete02} returns approximately
\(\sigma \approx 4.36\times10^{-1}\ \mathrm{MPa}\) and
\(E_t \approx -6.0\times10^{4}\ \mathrm{MPa}\).
The same pattern repeats on the OpenSees strain history. This is a strong
result: the active compressive branch is no longer the main mystery; the
dominant disagreement is localized to the crack-closure / reversal-return
regime of the unconfined concrete cover.

The replay now also shows that this constitutive mismatch occurs under the
same benchmark branch semantics. At the problematic anchor, both routes sit on
\texttt{protocol\_branch\_id}=2 and \texttt{reversal\_index}=1, with negative
increment direction and one accepted substep. OpenSees still resolves that
same anchor in four Newton iterations, whereas \texttt{fall\_n} uses five.
This is exactly the kind of positive solver trait worth importing from
OpenSees; it is not evidence that the benchmark should adopt a non-monotone
\texttt{domainTime} as its physical control variable.

That same replay also sharpens the architectural read on OpenSees-like
``time''. Importing OpenSees \texttt{domainTime} semantics into
\texttt{fall\_n} would be high-cost and low-return: the benchmark pseudo-time
already closes exactly as a physical control trace, whereas \texttt{domainTime}
remains an internal static-integrator diagnostic. The positive part worth
adopting from OpenSees is narrower and more useful: richer protocol metadata,
explicit reversal/branch traces, stronger continuation diagnostics, and
adaptive increment policies, all while keeping the benchmark pseudo-time
monotone and physically declared.

\paragraph{Amplitude-frontier audit.}
With the local constitutive frontier now localized, the reboot can ask a more
disciplined larger-amplitude question: how far does the current external
benchmark remain executable and scientifically interpretable before a new
continuation benchmark or a new constitutive bridge is required?
That question is now frozen explicitly in
\texttt{scripts/run\_reduced\_rc\_amplitude\_escalation\_audit.py}.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=bash,caption={Canonical cyclic amplitude-escalation audit for the reduced RC-column external benchmark.},label={lst:validation-reboot-amplitude-escalation-audit-run}]
py -3.11 scripts/run_reduced_rc_amplitude_escalation_audit.py \
  --output-dir data/output/cyclic_validation/reboot_external_amplitude_escalation_solver_policy_shared \
  --mode both \
  --print-progress
\end{lstlisting}

One infrastructure bug had to be removed before trusting this audit again.
The amplitude-escalation orchestrator was not propagating its declared
\texttt{--python-launcher} into the nested section and structural runners, so a
headless rerun could silently fall back to a broken ambient
\texttt{py -3.12} launcher.  The same script also assumed an interactive
matplotlib backend.  Both issues are now corrected in
\texttt{scripts/run\_reduced\_rc\_amplitude\_escalation\_audit.py}: the nested
benchmark invocations inherit the explicit launcher, and figure generation
defaults to a non-interactive \texttt{Agg} backend.  This is not a cosmetic
cleanup.  It restores the methodological meaning of the audit by separating a
real OpenSees frontier from a wrapper failure.

The audit separates two coupled frontiers instead of collapsing them into one:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item a section-level external cyclic frontier over
    \(\kappa_y \in \{0.010,\,0.015,\,0.020,\,0.025,\,0.030\}\,\mathrm{m}^{-1}\),
    where the nonlinear constitutive gap is already known to be active;
  \item a structural external cyclic frontier over tip amplitudes
    \(\{1.25,\,2.50,\,5.00,\,7.50,\,10.00,\,15.00\}\,\mathrm{mm}\)
    on the declared reference slice
    \((N=4,\ \text{Gauss--Legendre},\ \texttt{dispBeamColumn parity anchor})\),
    now complemented by a family audit over
    \(\{ \texttt{dispBeamColumn}, \texttt{forceBeamColumn} \}\) and an
    explicit \texttt{arc-length} retry probe.
\end{enumerate}

The resulting read is now sharper and more asymmetric than the earlier one:
\begin{itemize}
  \item the nonlinear section bridge completes through
    \(\kappa_y = 0.015\,\mathrm{m}^{-1}\);
  \item the first section-level external failure occurs at
    \(\kappa_y = 0.020\,\mathrm{m}^{-1}\), and it occurs on the OpenSees
    \texttt{zeroLengthSection} side under the declared
    \texttt{cyclic-diagnostic} constitutive policy;
  \item a more aggressive retry with smaller curvature segments and more
    bisections does not rescue that external section slice, so the failure is
    no longer attributable merely to a loose wrapper default;
  \item on the updated combined structural external benchmark, the declared
    reference slice is no longer limited by the old single-profile OpenSees
    solve; under the audited convergence-profile cascade plus
    \texttt{max\_bisections = 8}, the combined structural bundle now completes
    at \(20,35,50,100\,\mathrm{mm}\);
  \item the first combined failure still appears at \(75\,\mathrm{mm}\), and
    it is still on the OpenSees side rather than on the \texttt{fall\_n}
    side;
  \item the current external structural frontier is therefore
    \emph{non-monotone}: \(75\,\mathrm{mm}\) remains a pathological OpenSees
    bridge case, \(100\,\mathrm{mm}\) is reopened, and
    \(125/150\,\mathrm{mm}\) fail again on the OpenSees side;
  \item the reopened larger-amplitude probes also show that the internal
    \texttt{fall\_n} slice remains controlled beyond that OpenSees bridge
    frontier: the canonical \(125\,\mathrm{mm}\) and \(150\,\mathrm{mm}\)
    bundles complete on the \texttt{fall\_n} side in about
    \(0.412\,\mathrm{s}\) and \(0.457\,\mathrm{s}\) reported total wall time,
    while the OpenSees bridge fails before reaching protocol point
    \texttt{step=10} \((\Delta_\mathrm{tip}=-62.5\,\mathrm{mm}\) and
    \(-75\,\mathrm{mm})\);
  \item on the broader internal structural amplitude sweep
    \(\{20,35,50,75,100,125,150,200,250,300,400\}\,\mathrm{mm}\),
    \texttt{fall\_n} still completes all \(11/11\) amplitudes through
    \(400\,\mathrm{mm}\), whereas the focused robust external sweep over
    \(\{20,35,50,75,100,125,150\}\,\mathrm{mm}\) currently completes only
    \(4/7\) cases on the OpenSees side \((20,35,50,100)\,\mathrm{mm}\).
\end{itemize}

A section-only rerun of the canonical amplitude audit, now with explicit
launcher propagation fixed, confirms the earlier mechanical read instead of
changing it.  The combined section comparator still closes at
\(\kappa_y = 0.010,\,0.015\,\mathrm{m}^{-1}\) and still fails first at
\(0.020\,\mathrm{m}^{-1}\) on the OpenSees \texttt{zeroLengthSection} side.
The completed section bundles also sharpen the efficiency read: at
\(0.010\,\mathrm{m}^{-1}\) \texttt{fall\_n} reports about
\(5.65\times10^{-2}\,\mathrm{s}\) total wall time versus
\(2.74\times10^{-1}\,\mathrm{s}\) in OpenSees, and at
\(0.015\,\mathrm{m}^{-1}\) about \(4.98\times10^{-2}\,\mathrm{s}\) versus
\(2.61\times10^{-1}\,\mathrm{s}\).  So the present section frontier is now
simultaneously a mechanical result and a reproducible software result, not an
artifact of launcher drift.

One deeper correction was still needed before trusting that frontier.  The
external OpenSees section driver had been accepting any
\texttt{ops.analyze(1) == 0} as a valid curvature-control increment.  A
targeted reversal audit showed that this criterion was too weak: some
quasi-Newton profiles could return a nominal solver ``success'' while the
controlled curvature moved in the wrong direction or by an absurd magnitude.
So solver convergence alone was not yet an admissible benchmark step.

The driver now enforces an explicit directional-and-magnitude admissibility
contract in \texttt{scripts/opensees\_reduced\_rc\_column\_reference.py}.  A
step is accepted only if the achieved control increment has the same sign as
the requested \texttt{DisplacementControl} step, remains consistent in
magnitude, and preserves its accepted replay path through recursive cutbacks.
Those admissibility diagnostics are now exported alongside the control traces,
so the contract is explicit rather than implicit.

This materially changes the interpretation of the \(\kappa_y=0.020\) bridge
failure.  Under the old weak acceptance rule, the first reversal could look
like the decisive frontier.  Under the corrected admissibility contract, that
false frontier disappears and the benchmark can be tracked much deeper into the
cycle. The later failure at \texttt{failure\_step}=23 is still useful as a
historical debugging milestone because it proved the wrapper was no longer
lying about the control increment, but it is no longer the operative frontier
once the stronger mechanical admissibility rule below is enforced.

The same pass also closed an operational ambiguity on Windows: the canonical
Python launcher is now normalized through
\texttt{scripts/python\_launcher\_utils.py}, rather than relying on brittle
\texttt{shlex} parsing of quoted absolute paths.  That matters less
mechanically than the admissibility fix, but it matters methodologically: the
same benchmark command can now reproduce the audited runtime without depending
on an ambient shell quirk.

\paragraph{Second admissibility layer: stay on \(N=\mathrm{const}\).}
That directional-and-magnitude correction still left one deeper methodological
gap.  The section bridge is not declared as an arbitrary curvature-controlled
slice; it is declared as an audit of
\[
  \frac{\mathrm{d}M_y}{\mathrm{d}\kappa_y}\Big|_{N=\mathrm{const}}.
\]
So a step cannot be considered valid merely because it satisfies the curvature
control.  It must also remain on the constant-axial-force slice.  For that
reason, \texttt{scripts/opensees\_reduced\_rc\_column\_reference.py} now
enforces a second admissibility contract: the accepted section state must also
satisfy an axial-force residual tolerance before the step is accepted.

This changes the interpretation of the \(\kappa_y=0.020\,\mathrm{m}^{-1}\)
section frontier once more.  The later failure at
\texttt{failure\_step}=23 remains valuable as a debugging milestone because it
proved that the wrapper itself was no longer lying.  But under the stronger
and mechanically correct contract, that later event is no longer the operative
frontier.  The operative frontier is now the \emph{first reversal}:
\begin{itemize}
  \item canonical failed bundle:
    \texttt{tmp\_section\_kappa0p02\_axial\_admissibility\_audit};
  \item \texttt{failure\_step}=9;
  \item \texttt{failure\_target\_curvature\_y}
    \(=+1.75\times10^{-2}\,\mathrm{m}^{-1}\).
\end{itemize}
In other words, once the benchmark requires the OpenSees slice to remain on
the declared \(N=\mathrm{const}\) path, the comparator does not complete the
first unloading increment of the \(0.020\,\mathrm{m}^{-1}\) cycle.

That stronger criterion also sharpens the positive reading of the accepted
branch.  On the shared accepted branch up to
\(\kappa_y=0.020\,\mathrm{m}^{-1}\), the section axial-force closure is now
essentially exact
\((\max \varepsilon_N \approx 2.05\times10^{-7})\), the
moment--curvature mismatch remains modest
\((\max \varepsilon_M \approx 3.00\times10^{-2})\), and the
fiber-stress mismatch remains moderate
\((\max \varepsilon_\sigma \approx 1.68\times10^{-1})\).
The compute-time contrast also remains favorable to \texttt{fall\_n}: about
\(0.101\,\mathrm{s}\) versus \(0.593\,\mathrm{s}\) at
\(0.010\,\mathrm{m}^{-1}\), and about
\(0.109\,\mathrm{s}\) versus \(0.628\,\mathrm{s}\) at
\(0.015\,\mathrm{m}^{-1}\).

So the current section frontier should now be read much more honestly: it is
not the largest curvature where OpenSees returns nominal solver success, but
the largest curvature amplitude for which the external section bridge still
follows the declared cyclic path and remains on the constant-axial-force
slice.  On the current audited matrix, the combined section comparator closes
at \(\kappa_y=0.010,\,0.015\,\mathrm{m}^{-1}\) and fails first at
\(0.020\,\mathrm{m}^{-1}\) on the OpenSees side.

That read has now been pushed one layer deeper through
\texttt{scripts/run\_reduced\_rc\_section\_reversal\_frontier\_audit.py}.  The
result is useful because it rules out an overly simple explanation.  At the
first failing reversal of the section comparator, no materially plausible
single-profile override rescues the external OpenSees slice.  The declared
\texttt{cyclic-diagnostic} bridge, \texttt{Concrete02} with zero tension,
\texttt{Concrete02} with \(\lambda = 0.5\), and a
\texttt{Concrete01} Kent--Park-like control all fail at the same first
unloading step:
\begin{itemize}
  \item \texttt{failure\_step}=9;
  \item \texttt{failure\_target\_curvature\_y}
    \(=+1.75\times10^{-2}\,\mathrm{m}^{-1}\);
  \item \texttt{failure\_trial\_actual\_curvature\_y}
    \(=+2.00\times10^{-2}\,\mathrm{m}^{-1}\).
\end{itemize}
This is stronger and more useful than merely saying that
\texttt{Concrete02} is still imperfect.  The problematic-fiber replay did show
that removing the external tensile branch can match the extremal cover-fiber
history much more closely, but the reversal-frontier audit shows that this
alone does \emph{not} rescue the zeroLengthSection bridge under the full
\(N=\mathrm{const}\) contract.  So the current external section frontier is no
longer best interpreted as a pure uniaxial material-mapping issue; it is a
section-level reversal frontier of the external comparator itself.

The error growth remains informative rather than chaotic. On the section side,
the moment-curvature mismatch still grows from about
\(5.91\times10^{-2}\) at \(0.010\,\mathrm{m}^{-1}\) to about
\(1.08\times10^{-1}\) at \(0.015\,\mathrm{m}^{-1}\), while the branch-only
tangent mismatch still grows from about \(6.72\times10^{-2}\) to about
\(2.82\times10^{-1}\). On the updated combined structural side, the
hysteresis/base-moment mismatch is about \(1.52\times10^{-1}\) /
\(1.39\times10^{-1}\) at \(20\,\mathrm{mm}\), about
\(8.46\times10^{-2}\) / \(8.45\times10^{-2}\) at \(35\,\mathrm{mm}\), about
\(6.63\times10^{-2}\) / \(8.23\times10^{-2}\) at \(50\,\mathrm{mm}\), and
already rises to about \(2.21\times10^{0}\) / \(1.61\times10^{0}\) in the
reopened \(100\,\mathrm{mm}\) case. The current structural story is therefore
no longer best read as a \texttt{fall\_n} continuation frontier but as an
\emph{external-comparator frontier}, and a non-monotone one. The updated
combined amplitude bundle now reports
\texttt{highest\_combined\_completed\_amplitude} \(=100\,\mathrm{mm}\),
\texttt{highest\_fall\_n\_completed\_amplitude}
\(=150\,\mathrm{mm}\) on the focused external sweep, and
\texttt{highest\_opensees\_completed\_amplitude}
\(=100\,\mathrm{mm}\), while the broader internal structural sweep still
remains closed through \(400\,\mathrm{mm}\).

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering

    \centering

  \end{minipage}\hfill
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_structural_amplitude_escalation_errors.png}
  \end{minipage}

Right: combined structural external benchmark on the declared displacement-based reference slice. The section frontier still fails earlier, while the updated structural comparator now exhibits a non-monotone external frontier: \(20,35,50,100\,\mathrm{mm}\) complete, but the first OpenSees-side failure still occurs at \(75\,\mathrm{mm}\).}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-amplitude-escalation-errors}
\end{figure}

  \centering
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_structural_amplitude_family_errors.png}
  \end{minipage}\hfill
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_structural_amplitude_family_frontier.png}
  \end{minipage}

Right: completion frontier under the declared reversal-guarded policy, with an \texttt{arc-length} retry marker when attempted. This historical family audit remains useful, but it has now been superseded internally by the constitutively regularized \texttt{fall\_n} slice, which completes far beyond that earlier \(12.5\)--\(15\,\mathrm{mm}\) window.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-amplitude-family-frontier}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_section_amplitude_escalation_timing.png}
  \end{minipage}\hfill
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_structural_amplitude_escalation_timing.png}
  \end{minipage}
  \caption{Process-time growth under the same amplitude-escalation audit. The benchmark is now rich enough to track not only mechanical mismatch but also computational cost as amplitude increases.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-amplitude-escalation-timing}
\end{figure}

The older \(15\,\mathrm{mm}\) structural frontier should now be read as a
historical debugging milestone rather than as the current internal limit. A
root-cause audit on the \texttt{fall\_n} side showed that the decisive
instability was tied to a crack-onset tangent jump in the tensile branch of the
Kent--Park concrete law. The reduced RC reference section now regularizes that
onset through a \(C^1\) transition in
\texttt{src/materials/constitutive\_models/non\_lineal/KentParkConcrete.hh},
and the declared internal structural slice has been re-probed successfully at
\(100,125,150,200,250,300,400\,\mathrm{mm}\). The corresponding solver-health
trace remains controlled:
\begin{itemize}
  \item \(100\,\mathrm{mm}\): \(\max i_N = 7\),
    \(\overline{i}_N \approx 4.85\), \(\max b = 1\);
  \item \(125\,\mathrm{mm}\): \(\max i_N = 8\),
    \(\overline{i}_N \approx 4.77\), \(\max b = 0\);
  \item \(150\,\mathrm{mm}\): \(\max i_N = 17\),
    \(\overline{i}_N \approx 5.08\), \(\max b = 0\);
  \item \(200\,\mathrm{mm}\): \(\max i_N = 10\),
    \(\overline{i}_N \approx 6.08\), \(\max b = 0\);
  \item \(250\,\mathrm{mm}\): \(\max i_N = 12\),
    \(\overline{i}_N \approx 5.92\), \(\max b = 1\);
  \item \(400\,\mathrm{mm}\): \(\max i_N = 9\),
    \(\overline{i}_N \approx 4.85\), \(\max b = 1\).
\end{itemize}
So the reopened high-amplitude path is not being held together by runaway
bisection or by a pathological Newton trace. The present blocker is no longer
the internal displacement-driven \texttt{fall\_n} algorithm on this slice.

A short PETSc line-search audit at \(400\,\mathrm{mm}\) also proved useful,
but that older single-flag reading is now superseded by a more fundamental
solver-policy change inside \texttt{fall\_n}. The reduced structural benchmark
no longer forces a global \texttt{-snes\_linesearch\_type basic}; instead, the
runtime now declares an object-local SNES profile cascade inside
\texttt{NonlinearAnalysis}:
\begin{itemize}
  \item \texttt{newton\_backtracking} \(=\)
    \texttt{newtonls + bt + preonly/lu},
  \item \texttt{newton\_l2} \(=\)
    \texttt{newtonls + l2 + preonly/lu},
  \item \texttt{newton\_trust\_region} \(=\)
    \texttt{newtontr + preonly/lu}.
\end{itemize}
This is a structural improvement rather than a local tuning patch: it keeps
the benchmark on the same monotone pseudo-time path, avoids polluting the
global PETSc option database, and records the successful SNES profile at each
accepted runtime step. On the updated internal probes, the slice still closes
through at least \(400\,\mathrm{mm}\); at \(250\,\mathrm{mm}\) one reversed
step escalates from \texttt{newton\_backtracking} to \texttt{newton\_l2} and
still converges cleanly, while the \(400\,\mathrm{mm}\) cycle remains
controlled without loss of imposed-vs-total displacement consistency. So the
present structural blocker is no longer an under-specified PETSc Newton policy
on the \texttt{fall\_n} side.

The theoretical point worth preserving is that this was not solved by
``retuning'' a single flag.  The imposed displacement path was re-audited down
to the residual/Jacobian level and the conclusion is now explicit: on the
\texttt{fall\_n} side the quasi-static residual already acts on the total
state
\[
\mathbf{u}_{\mathrm{tot}}=
\mathbf{u}_{\mathrm{global}}+\mathbf{u}_{\mathrm{imposed}},
\]
so the robust correction could not consist in a local Dirichlet patch.  The
actual weak point was architectural: the reduced benchmark still depended on a
global PETSc option that was too fragile and too implicit for a validation
surface.  For that reason the solve policy was lifted into a shared extension
layer, \texttt{src/analysis/NonlinearSolvePolicy.hh}, and the benchmark now
declares its profiles as object-local solver policy rather than as ambient
runtime state.

That layer is now typed at its own boundary as well: the benchmark no longer
needs to scatter raw PETSc string literals in order to say ``Newton with
backtracking'' or ``trust-region''.  Instead it declares a method family and,
when applicable, a line-search family, and only the final PETSc application
step resolves those typed choices into concrete \texttt{SNES}/\texttt{KSP}
names.  This matters for robustness and extensibility: quasi-Newton,
nonlinear-GMRES, stronger trust-region variants, or future
Levenberg--Marquardt-like least-squares routes can be introduced behind the
same explicit interface without hard-coding solver strings throughout the
validation surface.

This change is relevant beyond the current quasi-static slice.  The same
policy representation is now also available to the dynamic route through the
SNES embedded inside PETSc \texttt{TS}.  The present implementation still uses
one active profile at a time in \texttt{DynamicAnalysis}, whereas
\texttt{NonlinearAnalysis} already retries a full local cascade, but the
important design fact is that both routes now share the same extension point.
That keeps the thesis honest with respect to its long-term focus on dynamics:
future trust-region, quasi-Newton, nonlinear-GMRES, or
Levenberg--Marquardt-style least-squares strategies can be introduced behind
the same declared interface without contaminating the material or element
layers with solver-specific branching.

That statement is also now checked in executable form.  The dynamic
\texttt{steppable} regression exercises \texttt{DynamicAnalysis} with a
declared trust-region profile and verifies that the profile reaches the
\texttt{SNES} handle returned by \texttt{TSGetSNES} while the time step still
converges.  The claim therefore remains deliberately scoped: the shared dynamic
extension point and one real trust-region profile are validated end-to-end,
whereas quasi-Newton, nonlinear-GMRES, and Levenberg--Marquardt-like routes
remain explicit growth paths rather than validated dynamic production
baselines.

That same shared solve layer has now been exercised one step further on the
reduced structural benchmark itself through
\texttt{scripts/run\_reduced\_rc\_internal\_hysteresis\_200mm.py}.  The bundle
\texttt{data/output/cyclic\_validation/reboot\_internal\_hysteresis\_200mm}
shows that the declared internal slice reaches the full
\(50,100,150,200\,\mathrm{mm}\) cyclic protocol with
\(V_{\max}\approx 25.05\,\mathrm{kN}\),
\(M_{\max}\approx 71.12\,\mathrm{kN\,m}\),
\(\max i_N = 33\), and \(\max b = 1\), while the accepted steps are still
carried only by \texttt{newton\_backtracking} and \texttt{newton\_l2}.  This is
already useful as a validation statement: the internal large-amplitude
hysteresis is now visible as a frozen bundle and not merely as a transient
debugging trace.

The same exercise exposed one more architectural weak point that was worth
correcting before benchmarking non-Newton routes.  It was not sufficient to
type the nonlinear method family while treating line search as if it belonged
only to \texttt{newtonls}.  In PETSc, \texttt{SNESQN} and
\texttt{SNESNGMRES} also carry an associated \texttt{SNESLineSearch}.  So the
solve-policy layer now applies declared line-search families through the
\texttt{SNESLineSearch} object whenever the active solver exposes one, not only
for Newton with line search.  Concretely, the current reduced-column benchmark
declares:
\begin{itemize}
  \item \texttt{quasi\_newton} with a critical-point line search; and
  \item \texttt{nonlinear\_gmres} with an \(l_2\)/secant line search.
\end{itemize}
Before this correction, quasi-Newton could die behind a PETSc signal-11 crash
and nonlinear-GMRES was not being benchmarked under an explicit line-search
contract.  After the correction both routes fail cleanly and reproducibly,
which is scientifically much more useful than an infrastructure-dependent
abort.

That improved benchmark is now frozen by
\texttt{scripts/run\_reduced\_rc\_nonlinear\_solver\_policy\_benchmark.py} in
\texttt{data/output/cyclic\_validation/reboot\_internal\_solver\_policy\_benchmark\_200mm\_stage3}.
Its present read is already informative:
\begin{itemize}
  \item \texttt{canonical-cascade}, \texttt{newton-backtracking-only},
    \texttt{newton-l2-only}, \texttt{newton-trust-region-dogleg-only},
    \texttt{nonlinear-cg-only}, and \texttt{anderson-only} all complete the full
    \(50,100,150,200\,\mathrm{mm}\) protocol;
  \item \texttt{newton-l2-only} is currently the strongest internal baseline on
    this slice, with reported total wall time
    \(4.25\,\mathrm{s}\) versus \(5.06\,\mathrm{s}\) for the cascade,
    \(6.39\,\mathrm{s}\) for backtracking-only,
    \(10.14\,\mathrm{s}\) for dogleg trust-region,
    \(10.74\,\mathrm{s}\) for Anderson acceleration, and
    \(109.43\,\mathrm{s}\) for nonlinear conjugate gradient, while also
    reducing the Newton work from \(\max i_N = 33, 51, 136, 518,\) and
    \(3257\) down to \(16\);
  \item the completed hysteresis traces remain essentially identical: relative
    to the cascade, the worst pointwise base-shear discrepancy is only
    \(7.8\times10^{-4}\) for \texttt{newton-l2-only} and
    \(4.9\times10^{-4}\) for backtracking-only; dogleg, Anderson, and NCG stay
    on the same physical branch as well;
  \item \texttt{newton-trust-region-only} still fails on the first
    \(50\,\mathrm{mm}\) reversal;
  \item \texttt{quasi-newton-only} and \texttt{nonlinear-gmres-only} now fail
    cleanly on the first lateral branch after long and explicitly audited
    attempts, so they remain experimental growth paths rather than promoted
    baselines;
  \item \texttt{nonlinear-richardson-only} also fails cleanly and early, so it
    remains non-promotable on this slice despite now being wired through the
    same extension surface.
\end{itemize}
This matters because the benchmark is no longer merely asking whether the
internal slice converges; it is already discriminating which declared nonlinear
solve policy is the cheapest honest baseline on the same physical path.  On the
evidence available today, that answer is \texttt{newton-l2-only}, not the full
cascade.  The same evidence also shows that the extension surface is no longer
one-dimensional: \texttt{newton-trust-region-dogleg-only} is now a viable but
slower Newton-family alternative, \texttt{anderson-only} is the strongest
additional non-Newton route, and \texttt{nonlinear-cg-only} is admissible but
too expensive to promote.
