  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.78\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_internal_hysteresis_200mm.pdf}
  \caption{Internal \texttt{fall\_n} hysteresis bundle frozen up to
  \(200\,\mathrm{mm}\).  The current reduced-column slice reaches the full
  declared protocol without leaving the displacement-driven path.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-internal-hysteresis-200mm}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.78\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_internal_solver_policy_timing.pdf}
  \caption{Internal nonlinear solver-policy timing benchmark on the same
  \(200\,\mathrm{mm}\) cyclic slice.  \texttt{newton-l2-only} is currently the
  cheapest completed policy.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-internal-solver-policy-timing}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.78\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_internal_solver_policy_robustness.pdf}
\caption{Internal solver-policy robustness over the full
  \(50,100,150,200\,\mathrm{mm}\) protocol.  The promoted baseline is still
  \texttt{newton-l2-only}; dogleg extends the Newton family, Anderson and NCG
  close the canonical slice at much higher cost, and Richardson, quasi-Newton,
  and nonlinear-GMRES remain open growth paths rather than promoted baselines.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-internal-solver-policy-robustness}
\end{figure}

The OpenSees comparison also becomes clearer under that lens.  On the
structural side, the relevant control is no longer ``OpenSees in general'', but
declared comparator families with distinct roles:
\begin{itemize}
  \item \texttt{ElasticTimoshenkoBeam} as the elastic shear-flexible control;
  \item \texttt{dispBeamColumn} as the primary nonlinear displacement-based
  parity anchor;
  \item \texttt{forceBeamColumn} as an equilibrium-rich sensitivity path.
\end{itemize}
This matters because \texttt{fall\_n} itself uses a mixed-interpolation
Timoshenko beam with explicit shear strains, direct global assembly, and an
exported condensed section tangent.  The benchmark is therefore not a naive
``same element in two codes'' claim, but a controlled comparison between
declared structural slices.

The timing data should be read with the same restraint.  On the present
elasticized Timoshenko parity control, \texttt{fall\_n} is slightly faster in
total wall time (\(0.1608\,\mathrm{s}\) vs \(0.1841\,\mathrm{s}\)) and clearly
lower in process wall time (\(0.2137\,\mathrm{s}\) vs
\(0.4557\,\mathrm{s}\)).  On the present nonlinear
\(100\,\mathrm{mm}\) external cyclic benchmark, however, \texttt{fall\_n}
remains slower
(\(1.3137\,\mathrm{s}\) vs \(0.9714\,\mathrm{s}\) total wall time;
\(1.5158\,\mathrm{s}\) vs \(1.3283\,\mathrm{s}\) process wall time).  So the
current defensible claim is not blanket efficiency superiority.  The current
advantages of \texttt{fall\_n} are architectural and epistemic: typed
formulation control, explicit solver-policy declaration, richer diagnostics,
and cleaner material/section auditability.  Runtime competitiveness is already
visible on some equivalent slices, but still not universal.

That architectural surface is now exercised on a broader structural family
matrix, frozen in
\texttt{scripts/run\_reduced\_rc\_timoshenko\_matrix\_experiment.py} and
\texttt{data/output/cyclic\_validation/reboot\_timoshenko\_matrix\_experiment\_stage2\_hifi2d}.
The matrix spans \texttt{TimoshenkoBeamN} with \(N=2,\dots,10\), four
beam-axis integration families
\((\texttt{legendre},\ \texttt{lobatto},\ \texttt{radau-left},\ \texttt{radau-right})\),
the common internal cyclic protocol
\((50,100,150,200)\,\mathrm{mm}\), and a declared external hi-fi OpenSees
reference over the full protocol.  The point of the matrix is not to crown one
numerical setting prematurely, but to expose how robustness, timing, and
physical coherence move together when the interpolation order and the
beam-axis quadrature are both allowed to vary.

The read is already useful.  Out of the \(36\) structural slices, \(26\)
\texttt{fall\_n} cases complete the full internal
\(200\,\mathrm{mm}\) protocol.  All families complete for \(N=2,3,4\).  The
interesting discrimination begins at higher order.  For \(N\ge5\),
\texttt{radau-left} is currently the strongest family (\(5/6\) completed),
\texttt{radau-right} follows closely (\(4/6\)), \texttt{lobatto} remains
viable (\(4/6\)), and \texttt{legendre} becomes fragile (\(1/6\)).  The timing
side is more nuanced: \texttt{legendre} remains the cheapest family when it
works, while \texttt{radau-right} offers the best current robustness/work
balance among the high-order completed slices, with clearly lower worst Newton
work than \texttt{radau-left} and nearly the same completion rate.  That is
already enough to justify treating beam-axis integration as a first-class
validation variable rather than a hidden implementation detail.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.86\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_timoshenko_matrix_timing_convergence.pdf}
  \caption{Stage-1 structural family matrix for \texttt{TimoshenkoBeamN},
  \(N=2,\dots,10\), and the four declared beam-axis integration families.
  The figure shows why the current read cannot be reduced to a single
  ``best'' quadrature: \texttt{legendre} is fastest on the easy slices, while
  \texttt{radau-left}/\texttt{radau-right} dominate the robustness frontier at
  higher order.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-timoshenko-matrix-timing-convergence}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.86\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_timoshenko_matrix_physical_coherence.pdf}
  \caption{Physical-coherence read for the same matrix over the common
  external hi-fi comparison window.  The OpenSees hi-fi reference now spans the
  full \((50,100,150,200)\,\mathrm{mm}\) protocol, so the error measures are no
  longer short-window placeholders but full-cycle structural coherence
  indicators.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-timoshenko-matrix-physical-coherence}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.86\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_timoshenko_matrix_hysteresis_overlays.pdf}
  \caption{Representative hysteresis overlays for the structural family
  matrix.  The matrix is already rich enough to reveal where the internal
  \texttt{fall\_n} family remains physically coherent as \(N\) and
  beam-axis quadrature change, even before claiming broader cross-solver
  equivalence.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-timoshenko-matrix-hysteresis}
\end{figure}

The external hi-fi comparator also becomes more disciplined under that same
contract.  The old 3D hi-fi route remained trapped in local
\texttt{forceBeamColumn} compatibility failures before becoming a useful
large-amplitude comparator, so the campaign now freezes a single-direction 2D
hi-fi structural slice instead of continuing to over-interpret that blocked 3D
path.  The new reference, frozen in
\texttt{opensees\_hifi\_reference/reference\_manifest.json}, is a
\texttt{dispBeamColumn} column with \(20\) structural elements, Legendre
integration, \(5\) section integration points per element, the same reduced RC
fiber section, and the same axial preload and cyclic drift protocol through the
full \((50,100,150,200)\,\mathrm{mm}\) history.

Two reasoning steps mattered here and should remain explicit.  First, the
hi-fi runner now exposes its model dimension directly, so the same fiber
section and loading policy can be exercised in 2D and 3D instead of hiding the
 benchmark behind one hard-wired OpenSees frame choice.  Second, the OpenSees
solver surface now exposes pure one-profile families
(\texttt{pure-krylov-disp}, \texttt{pure-broyden-disp},
\texttt{pure-secant-disp}, etc.).  This is not a cosmetic extension.  On the
current Windows/OpenSeesPy path, the mixed-profile cascade does not have a
trustworthy domain restore once one candidate profile fails inside an
increment, so the canonical hi-fi comparator is intentionally frozen on a
single-profile family rather than on a hidden mixed fallback.

That dedicated OpenSees profile benchmark is now frozen in
\texttt{data/output/cyclic\_validation/reboot\_opensees\_hifi\_solver\_profile\_benchmark\_200mm}.
Its read is already useful: \texttt{pure-krylov-disp},
\texttt{pure-broyden-disp}, \texttt{pure-secant-disp}, and the old
\texttt{robust-large-amplitude} cascade all complete the full hi-fi
\(200\,\mathrm{mm}\) protocol; plain Newton and line-search-only fail early;
and \texttt{pure-modified-initial-energy} is not promotable on this slice
because it is both late-failing and prohibitively expensive.  The current
declared canonical family is \texttt{pure-secant-disp}, while the fastest
completed family in the benchmark is presently \texttt{pure-krylov-disp}
(\(\sim3.93\,\mathrm{s}\) process wall time on the frozen 2D hi-fi case).

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.84\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_opensees_hifi_solver_profile_timing.pdf}
  \caption{OpenSees hi-fi solver-profile benchmark on the frozen 2D structural
  comparator through \((50,100,150,200)\,\mathrm{mm}\). The figure makes the
  methodological split explicit: pure single-profile families are now audited
  on their own merit, and not hidden inside a mixed fallback cascade whose
  rollback semantics remain opaque on the current OpenSeesPy/Windows path.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-opensees-hifi-profile-timing}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.84\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_opensees_hifi_solver_profile_status.pdf}
  \caption{Completion/failure status for the same OpenSees hi-fi solver-profile
  benchmark. The campaign already supports a disciplined decision: promote
  \texttt{pure-secant-disp} as the declared canonical external family, keep
  \texttt{pure-krylov-disp} as the fastest completed auxiliary profile, and
  treat plain Newton, pure line-search, and modified-initial-energy as
  non-promotable on this structural slice.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-opensees-hifi-profile-status}
\end{figure}

There was also a real constitutive lesson in that transition.  The 2D hi-fi
bridge only became stably reproducible through \(200\,\mathrm{mm}\) once the
OpenSees \texttt{Steel02} reversal surface was moved back toward much more
standard Menegotto--Pinto values
\((R_0,cR_1,cR_2)=(20,0.925,0.15)\).  That does not ``prove'' that OpenSees is
the truth and \texttt{fall\_n} is wrong; it does prove that the previous
external structural bridge had been carrying an unnecessarily pathological
reversal surface that was obscuring the structural comparison itself.

Under this new full-window comparator, the matrix read becomes materially more
useful than it was under the old \(50\,\mathrm{mm}\) external window.  The
best completed \texttt{fall\_n} cases now cluster around the higher-order
\texttt{lobatto}/\texttt{radau-left} families, with representative hysteresis
RMS error of order \(1.5\times10^{-1}\) to \(1.7\times10^{-1}\) and total-work
error of order \(1.0\times10^{-1}\) to \(1.4\times10^{-1}\).  That is still
not closure, but it is already a physically interpretable full-cycle benchmark
rather than a short-window placeholder.

In parallel, the nonlinear solve surface is no longer benchmarked only on one
canonical structural slice.  The representative policy campaign frozen in
\texttt{scripts/run\_reduced\_rc\_solver\_policy\_case\_matrix.py} and
\texttt{data/output/cyclic\_validation/reboot\_solver\_policy\_case\_matrix\_stage1},
plus the supplemental Lobatto-only non-Newton matrix frozen in
\texttt{data/output/cyclic\_validation/reboot\_solver\_policy\_case\_matrix\_stage3\_extended},
now give a cleaner read.  No non-Newton family emerges yet as a promotion
candidate on this structural family.  \texttt{newton-l2-only} is the strongest
current baseline across the harder completed slices.  The newer dogleg
trust-region route is now a real Newton-family alternative: it closes the
Lobatto \(N=2\), \(N=4\), and \(N=10\) slices, but remains consistently slower
than \texttt{newton-l2-only}.  \texttt{anderson-only} is the strongest new
non-Newton route: it completes the Lobatto \(N=2\) and \(N=4\) slices, but
loses robustness at \(N=10\) and remains much more expensive.
\texttt{nonlinear-cg-only} can complete the mid-order Lobatto \(N=4\) slice,
but at prohibitive cost (\(\sim94.7\,\mathrm{s}\) versus
\(\sim6.57\,\mathrm{s}\) for \texttt{newton-l2-only}) and still fails on
\(N=2\) and \(N=10\).  \texttt{nonlinear-richardson-only} fails on all three
representative Lobatto slices.  \texttt{quasi-newton} and
\texttt{nonlinear-gmres} therefore remain useful growth paths rather than
promoted baselines.  The same typed solve-policy surface is now also the
declared extension seam for future TAO/Levenberg--Marquardt-like damped
least-squares routes, so future nonlinear growth does not have to reopen the
driver contracts that this stage already stabilised.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.82\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_solver_policy_case_matrix_overview.pdf}
  \caption{Representative nonlinear solver-policy case matrix on the reduced
  RC structural family.  The figure confirms that the strongest current
  baselines are still Newton-family policies, even though the shared typed
  solve surface is already prepared for non-Newton growth.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-solver-policy-case-matrix-overview}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.82\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_solver_policy_case_matrix_timing.pdf}
  \caption{Timing read for the representative solver-policy matrix.  The
  current result is not ``Newton versus non-Newton'' in the abstract, but a
  more grounded statement: on this structural family, \texttt{newton-l2-only}
  and backtracking remain the cheapest honest completed routes, while the
  non-Newton candidates still need further algorithmic work before promotion.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-solver-policy-case-matrix-timing}
\end{figure}

A deeper structural fiber-history audit then exposed one more comparison bug
that had to be removed before interpreting that frontier.  The structural
OpenSees bridge was still querying fiber states with the native OpenSees
section \(z\)-sign, while the structural benchmark contract already declares
the \texttt{fall\_n} section convention.  Once that \(z\)-projection was
corrected, the apparent structural fiber mismatch dropped sharply on the short
cyclic slice:
\[
\max \varepsilon_{\sigma}^{\mathrm{fiber,struct}} \approx 1.15,\qquad
\operatorname{rms}\varepsilon_{\sigma}^{\mathrm{fiber,struct}}
\approx 3.70\times 10^{-1},
\]
down from the earlier \(\sim 7.96\) and \(\sim 1.72\) read.  The remaining
gap is therefore no longer a diffuse whole-section convention failure; it is
localized mainly in selected unconfined cover fibers during reversal, with the
base and intermediate stations carrying the dominant discrepancy.

That same audit also sharpens the \(15\,\mathrm{mm}\) frontier read.  Under
much stronger guarded displacement continuation, the \texttt{fall\_n}
reference slice no longer stops near the earlier \(11.25\,\mathrm{mm}\)
regime, but reaches about \(13.95\,\mathrm{mm}\) before failing.  The last
converged hotspot on those stronger runs remains very specific: base-station
\texttt{cover\_top} unconfined-concrete fibers are fully open in tension
\((\sigma \approx 0,\ E_t \approx 0.03\,\mathrm{MPa})\) while neighbouring
reinforcing fibers remain active.  This is already enough to say that the
present \(15\,\mathrm{mm}\) frontier is no longer just a weak continuation
default; it is a mixed numerical/constitutive frontier concentrated in the
tensile cover response near the base.

That frontier is now also compared directly against the strongest successful
structural slice, and against the exact failed recursive trial of the current
\(15\,\mathrm{mm}\) run, through
\texttt{scripts/run\_reduced\_rc\_structural\_frontier\_transition\_audit.py}.
The comparison is intentionally narrow: it reuses the same tracked extremal
fiber identified by the hotspot audit
\((\texttt{section\_gp}=0,\ \texttt{cover\_top},\ \texttt{unconfined\_concrete})\)
and the same base-section tangent block.  The result is more informative than
the earlier ``localized hotspot'' statement.  The tracked fiber is already
fully open on the successful \(12.5\,\mathrm{mm}\) slice, with
\(\sigma \approx 0\) and \(E_t \approx 0.03\,\mathrm{MPa}\), and it remains in
that same open state at the last converged frontier point near
\(13.945\,\mathrm{mm}\).  Between those two states, the fiber strain grows by
about \(13.6\%\), the base-section curvature magnitude by about \(11.9\%\),
and the base-moment magnitude by about \(9.6\%\), while the condensed base
flexural tangent softens by only about \(1.6\%\).  The raw section blocks
drift in the same moderate range: \(K_{00}\) drops by about \(7.1\%\),
\(K_{0y}\) by about \(2.3\%\), and \(K_{yy}\) stays nearly unchanged.  The
exact failed recursive target then sits only at \(13.967\,\mathrm{mm}\), i.e.
about \(0.16\%\) beyond the last converged drift.  At that exact failed trial,
the tracked fiber still remains fully open with the same
\(\sigma \approx 0,\ E_t \approx 0.03\,\mathrm{MPa}\), and its strain rises by
only about \(0.15\%\) relative to the last converged point.  The base section
also changes only marginally between those two states: curvature by about
\(0.15\%\), moment by about \(0.14\%\), and the condensed \(EI_y\) by only
\(-4\times 10^{-5}\) in relative terms.  Zone/material aggregates at the base
confirm the same reading: no abrupt redistribution appears between the last
converged point and the exact failed trial; the compressive cover/core and the
reinforcing steel continue to carry the section smoothly.  The new solver
trace adds one more important discriminator: the committed
\(12.5\,\mathrm{mm}\) branch does not need bisection, and the strongest
\(15\,\mathrm{mm}\) bundle still reaches its last converged point as a quiet
committed two-iteration step with zero committed bisection depth.  The actual
break occurs on one attempted runtime step near \(13.967\,\mathrm{mm}\), where
one substep is accepted, bisection is exhausted to depth \(4\) on the
remainder, and SNES exits with reason \(-6\) at residual norm
\(\sim 1.67\times10^{-3}\).  The current \(15\,\mathrm{mm}\) blockage is
therefore better interpreted as a fine continuation frontier acting on an
already degraded section/fiber state, not as a new abrupt local constitutive
collapse that appears only after \(12.5\,\mathrm{mm}\).

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.84\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_structural_frontier_tracked_fiber_transition.pdf}
  \caption{Tracked-fiber transition audit between the strongest successful
  structural slice (\(12.5\,\mathrm{mm}\)) and the last converged point of the
  current \(15\,\mathrm{mm}\) frontier.  The extremal \texttt{cover\_top}
  unconfined-concrete fiber is already fully open at \(12.5\,\mathrm{mm}\) and
  remains open at the frontier; what changes is mainly the strain level, not
  the constitutive regime.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-structural-frontier-tracked-fiber}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.84\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_structural_frontier_section_block_transition.pdf}
  \caption{Base-section block transition over the same interval.  The current
  frontier does not coincide with a sharp collapse of the base section:
  \(K_{00}\), \(K_{0y}\), \(K_{yy}\), and the condensed \(EI_y\) drift only
  moderately between the successful \(12.5\,\mathrm{mm}\) slice and the last
  converged frontier state near \(13.945\,\mathrm{mm}\).}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-structural-frontier-section-block}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.84\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_structural_frontier_solver_trace_transition.pdf}
  \caption{Solver-trace transition over the same structural frontier pair.
  The committed branch remains calm up to the last converged point, while the
  actual \(15\,\mathrm{mm}\) failure concentrates on one attempted runtime
  step near \(13.967\,\mathrm{mm}\) that exhausts bisection depth \(4\). This
  reinforces the reading of the current barrier as a narrow continuation
  frontier, not as a diffuse branch-wide loss of equilibrium.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-structural-frontier-solver-trace}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.84\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_structural_frontier_zone_transition.pdf}
  \caption{Base-section zone/material aggregates at the structural frontier.
  The transition from the last converged state to the exact failed recursive
  trial does not show an abrupt redistribution between tensile cover,
  compressive cover/core, and reinforcing steel. This supports the reading of
  the \(15\,\mathrm{mm}\) barrier as a narrow continuation frontier rather
  than a newly triggered local collapse.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-structural-frontier-zone-transition}
\end{figure}

One more question then becomes unavoidable: is the tracked extremal cover fiber
following the wrong local physics, or is it following the right sequence with
slightly different timing?  That is now frozen separately in
\texttt{scripts/run\_reduced\_rc\_structural\_fiber\_closure\_audit.py} on the
successful \(12.5\,\mathrm{mm}\) structural bundle.  The audit follows the
same base \texttt{cover\_top} unconfined-concrete fiber through the full cycle
and identifies six local milestones: initial compression, first positive
strain, opening, peak-open state, closure, and recompression.  The result is
reassuring.  Both \texttt{fall\_n} and OpenSees show the same broad physical
ordering: initial compression, tensile opening on the positive branch, closure
near the return through zero drift, and re-entry into compression on the
reverse branch.  The mismatch is therefore not in the existence of those local
events.

The mismatch is instead in their timing and residual state.  On the positive
branch, OpenSees drops that fiber to zero tensile stress one structural step
earlier than \texttt{fall\_n}.  Closure and compressive re-engagement then
occur at the same structural step on both sides (\texttt{step = 6}, the zero
drift crossing), so the reverse-branch ordering itself is not wrong.  The most
telling discrepancy appears on the final return to zero drift: \texttt{fall\_n}
has already released the fiber back to \(\sigma = 0\) with residual
\(E_t \approx 0.03\,\mathrm{MPa}\), while OpenSees still keeps a small
compressive traction \(\sigma \approx -3.16\times 10^{-2}\,\mathrm{MPa}\) and
a large tangent \(E_t \approx 1.46\times 10^{4}\,\mathrm{MPa}\).  This makes
the remaining structural gap look less like a wrong local mechanism and more
like a constitutive timing/residual-state difference around crack closure and
final re-contact of the cover concrete.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.84\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_structural_fiber_closure_events.pdf}
  \caption{Tracked structural fiber closure audit on the successful
  \(12.5\,\mathrm{mm}\) benchmark.  Both solvers follow the same broad
  open/close/recompress sequence on the extremal base cover fiber; the main
  difference is when the fiber is fully released and how much residual
  compressive state remains at the final return to zero drift.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-structural-fiber-closure-events}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.84\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_structural_fiber_closure_phase_path.pdf}
  \caption{Local phase path of the same tracked fiber.  The broad topology is
  the same on both sides; the remaining difference is a timing/residual-state
  mismatch around release and re-contact, not a qualitatively different local
  constitutive mechanism.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-structural-fiber-closure-phase}
\end{figure}

\section{Stage-1 closure of the reduced-column reboot}

The first reduced-column validation stage can now be closed, but only under a
strict interpretation of what ``closed'' means.  The canonical closure bundle
is frozen by
\texttt{scripts/run\_reduced\_rc\_stage1\_closure\_audit.py} in
\texttt{data/output/cyclic\_validation/reboot\_stage1\_closure\_audit}.  Its
claim is \emph{not} that large-amplitude physical equivalence is already
finished.  Its claim is narrower and more robust:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item the internal \texttt{fall\_n} reduced-column slice is now
    algorithmically stable on the declared displacement-driven path;
  \item the elasticized external Timoshenko parity control closes tightly
    enough to remove gross geometry, section-layout, and observable-extraction
    mismatch from the active suspicion list;
  \item the external section and structural frontiers are now declared
    explicitly instead of being inferred from scattered logs; and
  \item the remaining nonlinear gap is localized rather than diffuse.
\end{enumerate}

The closure bundle makes that statement quantitative.  On the internal side,
the declared \texttt{fall\_n} structural slice now completes through
\(200\,\mathrm{mm}\) under the shared PETSc SNES profile cascade.  The same
closure bundle now also freezes the internal nonlinear-solver read: the
promoted baseline is \texttt{newton-l2-only}; \texttt{newton-trust-region-dogleg-only}
already closes the same canonical protocol as a viable but slower Newton
alternative; and \texttt{anderson-only} together with
\texttt{nonlinear-cg-only} close that canonical slice as validated but much
more expensive non-Newton extension paths.  On the
elasticized external parity side, the stiffness-equivalent
\texttt{ElasticTimoshenkoBeam} control closes with
\(\max \varepsilon_V \approx 1.32\times10^{-4}\) on base shear and
essentially zero section-tangent mismatch, which is strong enough to remove
gross beam-theory or observable-contract mismatch from the active frontier.
On the section side, the external comparator still closes only through
\(\kappa_y = 0.015\,\mathrm{m^{-1}}\) and still fails first at
\(0.020\,\mathrm{m^{-1}}\); the dedicated reversal-frontier profile audit also
shows that no materially plausible single-profile external concrete override
rescues that first reversal.  On the structural side, the external comparator
is now explicitly non-monotone: it fails first at \(75\,\mathrm{mm}\), reopens
at \(100\,\mathrm{mm}\), and fails again at \(125/150\,\mathrm{mm}\), while
the internal \texttt{fall\_n} slice remains complete over the same audited
amplitude set.

This is also the first stage where timing can be written down honestly instead
of rhetorically.  The closure bundle keeps two anchors:
\begin{itemize}
  \item on the elasticized Timoshenko parity control, \texttt{fall\_n} is
    slightly faster in total wall time
    (\(0.1608\,\mathrm{s}\) versus \(0.1841\,\mathrm{s}\)) and clearly lower in
    process time (\(0.2137\,\mathrm{s}\) versus \(0.4557\,\mathrm{s}\));
  \item on the internal \(200\,\mathrm{mm}\) policy benchmark,
    \texttt{newton-l2-only} is the cheapest completed route
    (\(\sim4.74\,\mathrm{s}\) process wall time), dogleg is a viable but slower
    Newton alternative (\(\sim10.63\,\mathrm{s}\)), \texttt{anderson-only} is
    already validated but much more expensive (\(\sim11.18\,\mathrm{s}\)), and
    \texttt{nonlinear-cg-only} is admitted only at prohibitive cost
    (\(\sim109.85\,\mathrm{s}\));
  \item on the nonlinear \(100\,\mathrm{mm}\) external benchmark, OpenSees is
    still faster (\(0.9714\,\mathrm{s}\) versus \(1.3137\,\mathrm{s}\) in total
    wall time).
\end{itemize}
So the defensible architectural claim is not blanket runtime superiority.  The
current strengths of \texttt{fall\_n} are explicit formulation control, typed
solver-policy surfaces, stronger auditability of state/tangent extraction, and
cleaner preservation of the reasoning chain that led to the present frontier.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.78\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_stage1_closure_frontier.png}
  \caption{Canonical stage-1 frontier summary.  The internal
  \texttt{fall\_n} slice now completes through \(200\,\mathrm{mm}\); the
  external structural bridge reopens non-monotonically through
  \(100\,\mathrm{mm}\); and the external section bridge still stops first at
  \(\kappa_y = 0.020\,\mathrm{m^{-1}}\).}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-stage1-closure-frontier}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.78\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_stage1_closure_timing.png}
  \caption{Canonical stage-1 timing anchors.  \texttt{fall\_n} is already
  competitive on the elasticized Timoshenko parity control; the promoted
  internal baseline is \texttt{newton-l2-only}; and OpenSees remains faster on
  the nonlinear \(100\,\mathrm{mm}\) external benchmark.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-stage1-closure-timing}
\end{figure}

\section{Why some attractive extensions remain conditional}

The reboot is intentionally critical with respect to architectural ambition.
Several features are scientifically attractive:
\begin{itemize}
  \item alternative cyclic concrete models or continuum damage-plasticity
    variants \cite{LeeFenves1998_PlasticDamageModelCyclicConcrete,KoBathe2026};
  \item richer reinforced-concrete section and confinement descriptions
    \cite{Mander1988_ConfinedConcrete};
  \item flexibility-based beam-column elements and plastic-hinge integration
    schemes \cite{Spacone1996_FibreBeamColumnModel,Scott2008_SoftwarePatternsBeamColumnModels};
  \item richer Timoshenko beam section placement rules and quadrature families
    \cite{Li2013_FiberSectionTimoshenkoElementOpenSees}.
\end{itemize}

However, scientific precision requires a harder distinction:
\begin{quote}
  \emph{valuable future capability} is not the same as
  \emph{current blocker to the first reference validation campaign}.
\end{quote}
The present plan therefore implements such features only when the audited
campaign shows that the current baseline cannot close its gates honestly.

\section{Continuum bring-up baseline}

The next gate in the master plan was to enter the reduced-column continuum path
with embedded longitudinal bars without yet opening the full Hex20/Hex27
campaign. The useful result of this iteration is more basic and, precisely for
that reason, more valuable: the continuum driver itself is no longer the active
frontier.

The canonical artifact is now
\texttt{scripts/run\_reduced\_rc\_continuum\_bringup\_audit.py}, which freezes
its output in
\texttt{data/output/cyclic\_validation/reboot\_continuum\_bringup\_audit}. The
audit was designed to separate three questions that had initially appeared
entangled:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item whether the small-strain continuum with imposed top-face displacement
    was stable on its own;
  \item whether the embedded-bar boundary treatment introduced a null mode or a
    spurious rebar--host gap; and
  \item whether the so-called monotonic runner was actually monotonic, rather
    than a disguised cyclic branch.
\end{enumerate}

The correction is structural rather than local. The continuum baseline now
exposes:
\begin{itemize}
  \item an explicit reinforcement policy
    (\texttt{continuum\_only} versus
    \texttt{embedded\_longitudinal\_bars});
  \item an explicit embedded-boundary policy
    (\texttt{dirichlet\_rebar\_endcap} versus
    \texttt{full\_penalty\_coupling}); and
  \item a monotonic protocol with its own runtime-step semantics, instead of a
    hidden reuse of the cyclic displacement helper.
\end{itemize}
The same typed PETSc/SNES solve-policy surface already audited on the
structural slice is reused here, so the continuum path is not running on an ad
hoc nonlinear solver contract.

The present audited read is already sharp enough to justify the transition to
the continuum phase proper:
\begin{itemize}
  \item \texttt{continuum\_only\_elastic\_monotonic\_hex8\_2x2x4} completes in
    \(\sim 0.132\,\mathrm{s}\) with
    \(|V_{\mathrm{base}}|_{\max} \approx 2.026\,\mathrm{kN}\);
  \item \texttt{embedded\_elastic\_monotonic\_hex8\_2x2x4} completes in
    \(\sim 0.167\,\mathrm{s}\) with
    \(|V_{\mathrm{base}}|_{\max} \approx 2.129\,\mathrm{kN}\), which is about
    \(5.1\%\) stiffer than the plain continuum control;
  \item \texttt{continuum\_only\_nonlinear\_monotonic\_hex8\_2x2x8} completes
    in \(\sim 6.64\,\mathrm{s}\) with
    \(|V_{\mathrm{base}}|_{\max} \approx 4.634\,\mathrm{kN}\);
  \item \texttt{embedded\_nonlinear\_monotonic\_hex8\_2x2x8} completes in
    \(\sim 10.49\,\mathrm{s}\) with
    \(|V_{\mathrm{base}}|_{\max} \approx 5.163\,\mathrm{kN}\), i.e. about
    \(11.4\%\) stiffer than the plain nonlinear continuum control; and
  \item \texttt{embedded\_nonlinear\_cyclic\_hex8\_2x2x8} completes the short
    cyclic smoke bundle \((1.25, 2.50)\,\mathrm{mm}\) in
    \(\sim 34.60\,\mathrm{s}\) over \(24\) runtime steps.
\end{itemize}

One observable is especially important at this stage: the top rebar--face gap
stays at machine precision (\(\sim 10^{-19}\,\mathrm{m}\)) on the embedded
slice. That does not prove final physical validity of the coupling law, but it
does remove the suspicion of a gross kinematic mismatch from the active
frontier of the bring-up matrix.

The later structural--continuum objection forced a stricter reading of this
same gate.  Kinematic host--bar closure is necessary, but it is not sufficient:
the continuum comparison must also preserve the imposed axial resultant during
the lateral cycle. The diagnostic artifact
\texttt{scripts/plot\_structural\_continuum\_equivalence\_diagnosis.py}
compares two \(50\,\mathrm{mm}\) routes. The historical
\texttt{full\_penalty\_coupling + host\_surface\_only} path completes, but it
is not valid equivalence evidence: for a target axial load of
\(0.02\,\mathrm{MN}\), the base axial reaction reaches
\(0.2598\,\mathrm{MN}\), a maximum deviation of \(0.2398\,\mathrm{MN}\). The
\texttt{dirichlet\_rebar\_endcap + composite\_section\_force\_split} route
preserves the axial resultant with a maximum deviation of only
\(9.0\times10^{-10}\,\mathrm{MN}\), and reduces the peak-normalized RMS
base-shear gap from \(68.4\%\) to \(21.9\%\).

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.90\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/structural_continuum_equivalence_diagnosis.png}
  \caption{Structural--continuum equivalence diagnosis. The old penalty path
  completes the nonlinear solve but violates the imposed axial resultant. The
  Dirichlet endcap plus composite preload split restores axial balance; the
  remaining gap can now be interpreted as localization/material mismatch
  rather than a load-application artifact.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-structural-continuum-equivalence-diagnosis}
\end{figure}

This changes the promoted validation policy. Until
\texttt{full\_penalty\_coupling} passes the axial-resultant gate, it remains an
experimental interior-coupling route rather than the structural-equivalence
baseline. The promoted comparison route is
\texttt{dirichlet\_rebar\_endcap + composite\_section\_force\_split}; only
after this filter does it make sense to tune concrete cracking, confinement, or
an XFEM/DG replacement.

The reminder that the mesh can also be biased toward the loaded tip forced one
more architectural correction: the longitudinal bias is no longer an implicit
``fixed-end only'' scalar. The prism builder now exposes an explicit
\texttt{longitudinal\_bias\_location} policy with three admissible choices:
\texttt{fixed-end}, \texttt{loaded-end}, and \texttt{both-ends}. This is not a
cosmetic mesh flag. In a cantilever RC column, the fixed end carries the
plastic hinge, while the loaded end carries the imposed lateral Dirichlet field
and the axial-preload transfer. Moving all the refinement to the loaded end
therefore tests a real hypothesis, but it is not automatically admissible.

The audit artifact
\texttt{scripts/plot\_structural\_continuum\_bias\_location\_audit.py}
freezes that decision. On the validated
\texttt{dirichlet\_rebar\_endcap + composite\_section\_force\_split} gate, a
\(4\times4\times4\) \texttt{Hex8} mesh with the cyclic crack-band host gives:
\begin{itemize}
  \item at \(50\,\mathrm{mm}\), the original fixed-end-biased gate has a
    peak-normalized RMS base-shear gap of \(12.88\%\) and a steel hinge work
    ratio of \(0.539\);
  \item the loaded-end-only bias is rejected: the global gap rises to
    \(109.29\%\) and the steel hinge work ratio collapses to
    \(1.7\times 10^{-4}\), because the fixed-end hinge has been under-resolved;
  \item the both-ends bias is the first promoted mesh policy: at
    \(50\,\mathrm{mm}\), the global gap falls to \(5.23\%\) and the steel hinge
    work ratio rises to \(1.273\);
  \item at \(100\,\mathrm{mm}\), both-ends bias still improves the steel hinge
    work ratio from \(0.772\) to \(1.050\), but the global base-shear gap
    remains large (\(39.44\%\)).
\end{itemize}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.95\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/structural_continuum_bias_location_audit.png}
  \caption{Longitudinal-bias audit for the promoted
  structural--continuum gate. Loaded-end-only refinement is not physically
  admissible for the cantilever closure because it removes resolution from the
  fixed-end hinge. Biasing both ends preserves the hinge and resolves the
  loaded boundary; the \(100\,\mathrm{mm}\) residual gap therefore points back
  to the host concrete crack/shear-transfer law, not to embedded-bar slip or
  axial load application.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-structural-continuum-bias-location-audit}
\end{figure}

This is a useful negative result: refining the loaded face is necessary, but
not sufficient. The steel kinematic transfer remains closed at
\(\mathcal{O}(10^{-10})\) strain gap in all accepted cases, and the axial
reaction has already passed the resultant gate. The remaining high-amplitude
gap must therefore be attacked through the concrete host law: crack-band shear
retention, open-crack compression transfer, confinement, or a later XFEM/DG
localization route.

A first crack-band shear-retention sensitivity was then run at the
\(100\,\mathrm{mm}\) both-ends gate. The point was not to calibrate by hand,
but to determine whether the residual gap behaves like a shear-transfer
problem. The artifact
\texttt{scripts/plot\_structural\_continuum\_crack\_shear\_retention\_audit.py}
compares constant residual shear ratios \(0.05,0.10,0.20,0.40\). The best
global point in this small matrix is \(0.10\): the peak-normalized RMS
base-shear gap drops to \(23.24\%\), while the steel hinge work ratio remains
close to unity (\(0.961\)). Lowering the ratio further to \(0.05\) is not a
clean improvement: the global gap is \(29.34\%\), the hinge work ratio falls to
\(0.828\), and the solve cost grows to \(522.8\,\mathrm{s}\). Increasing the
ratio to \(0.40\) is clearly rejected: the global gap reaches \(100.0\%\), even
though the steel hinge work ratio remains near \(1.11\).

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.92\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/structural_continuum_crack_shear_retention_audit.png}
  \caption{Residual shear-retention sensitivity for the \(100\,\mathrm{mm}\)
  both-ends gate. A constant residual factor is influential, but it is not a
  complete closure mechanism: too much residual shear over-stiffens the global
  loop, while too little shear transfer becomes expensive and starts to
  under-dissipate the steel hinge.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-structural-continuum-crack-shear-retention-audit}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.78\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/structural_continuum_crack_shear_retention_hysteresis.png}
  \caption{Base-shear hysteresis for the same crack-band shear-retention
  sensitivity. The shape change confirms that the residual structural--
  continuum gap is now governed by the host concrete crack-transfer law rather
  than by the steel chain.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-structural-continuum-crack-shear-retention-hysteresis}
\end{figure}

The promoted next constitutive step is therefore not another scalar sweep. The
host concrete law needs a physically richer crack-transfer surface in which
shear retention depends on crack opening, normal compression/closure, and
possibly confinement. This is also the right place for the XFEM/DG branch to
enter: if the smeared crack-band remains too diffusive, the localization
kinematics should be improved before further numerical tuning.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.80\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_continuum_bringup_base_shear.png}
  \caption{Canonical continuum bring-up matrix. Introducing the embedded
  longitudinal bars raises the peak base reaction in both the elasticized and
  nonlinear monotonic controls, with a magnitude consistent with a physical
  stiffening effect and without loss of solver stability.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-continuum-bringup-base-shear}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.80\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_continuum_bringup_timing.png}
  \caption{Solve times for the first reduced RC continuum bring-up matrix. The
  embedded nonlinear cyclic slice is already expensive but now stable enough to
  justify the next planned phase: Hex20/Hex27, richer material comparisons, and
  then the structural--continuum comparison on top of the same driver.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-continuum-bringup-timing}
\end{figure}

\section{Continuum Hex-order extension matrix}

The next step was not simply ``run Hex20 and Hex27'' but restore the same
physical slice already used on the structural reference. In particular, the
structural reduced-column baseline already carried an explicit
\(0.02\,\mathrm{MN}\) axial preload with an equilibrated preload stage, while
the early continuum bring-up had intentionally omitted that load to isolate the
driver. The current extension matrix corrects that gap at the baseline level.

The canonical artifact is now
\texttt{scripts/run\_reduced\_rc\_continuum\_order\_matrix\_audit.py}, frozen
in
\texttt{data/output/cyclic\_validation/reboot\_continuum\_order\_matrix\_audit\_axial}.
Its methodological role is to answer three distinct questions:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item which hex families remain physically coherent under the same axial
    preload semantics;
  \item which families remain computationally viable for a short nonlinear or
    cyclic slice; and
  \item which family should be promoted first for the structural--continuum
    comparison gate.
\end{enumerate}

The correction is architectural as well as physical:
\begin{itemize}
  \item the continuum run spec now exposes
    \texttt{axial\_compression\_force\_mn},
    \texttt{use\_equilibrated\_axial\_preload\_stage}, and
    \texttt{axial\_preload\_steps};
  \item the axial load is applied through a consistent top-face traction on the
    host continuum, not through an ad hoc nodal-force patch; and
  \item \texttt{Domain::create\_boundary\_from\_plane(...)} now also supports a
    range-restricted overload, which is exactly what allows the top-face load
    boundary to be created on the continuum host while excluding embedded rebar
    elements that have no physical surface to load.
\end{itemize}

Under the matched-topology elastic slice
\((0.5\,\mathrm{mm},\,2\times2\times4,\,0.02\,\mathrm{MN})\), the read is
already decisive:
\begin{itemize}
  \item \texttt{Hex8} completes in \(\sim 0.44\,\mathrm{s}\) with
    \(|V_{\mathrm{base}}|_{\max} \approx 2.129\,\mathrm{kN}\);
  \item \texttt{Hex20} completes in \(\sim 2.57\,\mathrm{s}\) with
    \(|V_{\mathrm{base}}|_{\max} \approx 0.493\,\mathrm{kN}\);
  \item \texttt{Hex27} completes in \(\sim 5.27\,\mathrm{s}\) with
    \(|V_{\mathrm{base}}|_{\max} \approx 0.492\,\mathrm{kN}\).
\end{itemize}

Those two higher-order families are almost indistinguishable in the elastic
observable: they differ by only about \(0.21\%\). More importantly, the
canonical structural elastic reference
(\texttt{TimoshenkoBeamN<10>} with Radau-right quadrature, frozen in
\texttt{reboot\_structural\_reference\_elastic\_0p5mm\_radau\_right}) gives
\(|V_{\mathrm{base}}|_{\max} \approx 0.536\,\mathrm{kN}\). So
\texttt{Hex20/Hex27} lie only about \(8\%\) low, while \texttt{Hex8} is about
\(4.3\times\) stiffer and almost \(297\%\) above that structural reference.
That is the current strongest indication that the low-order continuum slice is
locking-dominated or otherwise too stiff to promote as the physical continuum
reference.

The nonlinear read must be interpreted more carefully. On the matched-topology
monotonic slice \((2.5\,\mathrm{mm},\,2\times2\times4)\):
\begin{itemize}
  \item \texttt{Hex8} completes in \(\sim 18.56\,\mathrm{s}\) with
    \(|V_{\mathrm{base}}|_{\max} \approx 12.667\,\mathrm{kN}\);
  \item \texttt{Hex20} completes in \(\sim 247.25\,\mathrm{s}\) with
    \(|V_{\mathrm{base}}|_{\max} \approx 2.647\,\mathrm{kN}\); and
  \item \texttt{Hex27} exceeds the current \(480\,\mathrm{s}\) audit budget.
\end{itemize}
This is not yet a formal nonlinear convergence statement across formulations,
because the structural reference still uses a fiber-section beam slice while
the continuum path uses a 3D concrete law plus embedded steel bars. It is,
however, a very useful viability frontier. Even under that weaker reading,
\texttt{Hex8} remains suspiciously stiff.

The short preloaded cyclic cost-controlled slice is sharper still:
\begin{itemize}
  \item \texttt{Hex8} \((2\times2\times4)\) completes in
    \(\sim 42.84\,\mathrm{s}\);
  \item \texttt{Hex20} \((2\times2\times4)\) exceeds the current
    \(240\,\mathrm{s}\) audit budget; and
  \item \texttt{Hex27} \((1\times1\times4)\) also exceeds the current
    \(240\,\mathrm{s}\) audit budget.
\end{itemize}
The important negative result is therefore not ``Hex20/Hex27 are bad'', but
``the first preloaded nonlinear/cyclic continuum matrix is already stable
enough to reveal that Hex8 should remain a low-order control, not the promoted
physical reference''.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.82\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_continuum_order_matrix_base_shear.png}
  \caption{Preloaded continuum hex-order matrix. The elastic matched-topology
  slice immediately separates the low-order \texttt{Hex8} response from the
  clustered \texttt{Hex20/Hex27} band, while the nonlinear rows expose the
  current runtime frontier rather than a completed convergence claim.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-continuum-order-matrix-base-shear}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.82\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_continuum_order_matrix_timing.png}
  \caption{Solve times for the preloaded continuum hex-order matrix. The
  physical promotion path is now constrained at least as much by runtime cost
  as by constitutive modeling, especially once axial preload is restored.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-continuum-order-matrix-timing}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.70\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_continuum_order_matrix_status.png}
  \caption{Completion map of the first preloaded continuum hex-order matrix.
  The current gate is no longer ``can the continuum run?'', but ``which family
  is physically coherent and operationally affordable enough to promote first?''}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-continuum-order-matrix-status}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Ko-Bathe cracking audit with embedded bars}

The next continuum step was to stop treating cracking as an implicit side
effect and make it a first-class observable of the reduced-column pilot. The
canonical artifact is now
\texttt{scripts/run\_reduced\_rc\_continuum\_crack\_audit.py}, summarized in
\texttt{reboot\_continuum\_ko\_bathe\_crack\_audit/continuum\_crack\_audit\_summary.json}.

This audit also forced an architectural clarification. The generalized
\texttt{TrussElement<Dim, NNodes>} and the reinforced prismatic builder can now
mount either two-node or three-node embedded bars. That flexibility mattered
immediately:
\begin{itemize}
  \item \texttt{Hex20 + 3-node} embedded bars did not close robustly enough to
    deserve promotion as the default baseline;
  \item \texttt{Hex20 + 2-node} embedded bars completed cleanly and tracked the
    \texttt{Hex27 + 3-node} cracking response closely; and
  \item \texttt{Hex27 + 3-node} completed cleanly and remains the higher-fidelity
    host/bar pairing.
\end{itemize}

That is why the validated automatic policy is now:
\[
\texttt{Hex20 automatic} \rightarrow \texttt{two\_node\_linear rebar},
\qquad
\texttt{Hex27 automatic} \rightarrow \texttt{three\_node\_quadratic rebar}.
\]
This is not a patch. It is the promoted default after explicitly auditing host
interpolation, embedded-bar interpolation, and nonlinear closure.

The first frozen monotonic cracked comparison is already physically coherent:
\begin{itemize}
  \item \texttt{Hex20 auto} completes in \(\sim 43.97\,\mathrm{s}\) with
    \(|V_{\mathrm{base}}|_{\max} \approx 20.999\,\mathrm{kN}\);
  \item \texttt{Hex27 auto} completes in \(\sim 164.83\,\mathrm{s}\) with
    \(|V_{\mathrm{base}}|_{\max} \approx 21.345\,\mathrm{kN}\);
  \item the peak base-shear ratio \(\texttt{Hex20}/\texttt{Hex27}\) is
    \(\sim 0.984\);
  \item both first crack at the same drift, \(\sim 6.67\,\mathrm{mm}\);
  \item \texttt{Hex20} reaches \(48\) cracked Gauss points with
    \(\max \varepsilon_{\mathrm{crack}} \approx 7.21\times10^{-4}\); and
  \item \texttt{Hex27} reaches \(42\) cracked Gauss points with
    \(\max \varepsilon_{\mathrm{crack}} \approx 7.34\times10^{-4}\).
\end{itemize}

Equally important, the audit now writes three synchronized VTK/PVD surfaces per
case: mesh, Gauss cloud, and explicit crack planes. The Gauss output carries
the Ko-Bathe crack fields (\texttt{qp\_num\_cracks},
\texttt{qp\_crack\_normal\_*}, \texttt{qp\_crack\_strain\_*},
\texttt{qp\_crack\_closed\_*}), while the crack-plane output turns the active
Gauss-point crack state into an explicit geometric object for ParaView.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.82\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_continuum_ko_bathe_base_shear.png}
  \caption{Ko-Bathe cracking audit: monotonic precompressed continuum response
  for the validated automatic pairings. \texttt{Hex20} and \texttt{Hex27}
  remain very close in peak force even after cracking develops.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-continuum-ko-bathe-base-shear}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.92\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_continuum_ko_bathe_crack_progression.png}
  \caption{Ko-Bathe cracking audit: cracked-Gauss-point count and maximum crack
  opening surrogate. The onset drift is identical in the two validated
  automatic pairings, while the spread and opening remain of the same order.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-continuum-ko-bathe-crack-progression}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.70\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_continuum_ko_bathe_timing.png}
  \caption{Solve times of the first Ko-Bathe cracking audit. \texttt{Hex20}
  closes the cracked slice in about \(26.7\%\) of the \texttt{Hex27} solve
  time, which is exactly why it remains the first promoted continuum reference
  while \texttt{Hex27} stays alive as the higher-fidelity branch.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-continuum-ko-bathe-timing}
\end{figure}

The first structural--continuum local steel comparison exposed one more
baseline issue that had to be corrected before the comparison could be taken
seriously. Matching the same eight-bar layout was necessary, but not
sufficient. In the promoted \texttt{Hex20 + two\_node\_linear rebar} pairing,
the embedded-truss Gauss point closest to the base does not sit on the clamped
plane; on the audited \texttt{2x2x2} host it sits at
\(z \approx 0.338\,\mathrm{m}\). Comparing that directly against the
structural base section would still mix different physical locations.

For that reason the new audit
\texttt{scripts/run\_reduced\_rc\_structural\_continuum\_steel\_hysteresis\_audit.py}
does not merely track the same bar by \((y,z)\). It also interpolates the
structural steel-fiber history axially between beam section stations to the
same physical height as the selected embedded-truss Gauss point before the
loops are compared. On the structural side this means interpolating between the
Lobatto stations bracketing \(z \approx 0.338\,\mathrm{m}\); on the continuum
side this means selecting the top-right embedded bar
\((y = z = 0.095\,\mathrm{m})\) and its closest base-side Gauss point.

The first canonical cyclic pilot of that bridge is now frozen in
\texttt{data/output/cyclic\_validation/reboot\_structural\_continuum\_steel\_hysteresis\_audit\_hex20\_2x2x2\_20mm}.
It uses the same precompressed \(5\)--\(10\)--\(15\)--\(20\,\mathrm{mm}\)
protocol on both sides:
\begin{itemize}
  \item structural reference:
    \texttt{TimoshenkoBeamN<10> + Lobatto + newton-l2-only};
  \item continuum reference:
    \texttt{Hex20 + embedded bars + structural\_matched\_eight\_bar} on
    \texttt{2x2x2}; and
  \item the same axial preload:
    \(0.02\,\mathrm{MN}\).
\end{itemize}

That matched-height bridge still left one methodological error alive: the
nominal structural steel fiber selected by \texttt{max(z,y)} did not lie on
the same active bending side as the embedded bar. The runner now audits that
orientation parity explicitly. It infers the active structural bending
coordinate from the exported section curvatures, enumerates the structurally
compatible steel-fiber candidates, and promotes the candidate that best
matches the embedded bar on the initial elastic branch. The corresponding
audit table is written to
\texttt{structural\_orientation\_candidates.csv} in the bundle.

That first \(2\times2\times2\) bridge is now superseded by the promoted local
continuum baseline
\[
\texttt{Hex20 4x4x2 + cover\_core\_split + cover\_aligned + structural\_matched\_eight\_bar + production\_stabilized + fracture\_secant + fixed-end } l_b,
\]
compared against
\(\texttt{TimoshenkoBeamN<10> + Lobatto + newton-l2-only + clamped top rotation}\).
The promoted bridge is now frozen in four canonical bundles:
\texttt{reboot\_structural\_continuum\_promoted\_monotonic\_audit},
\texttt{reboot\_structural\_continuum\_promoted\_cyclic\_20mm\_audit},
\texttt{reboot\_structural\_continuum\_promoted\_cyclic\_25mm\_audit}, and
\texttt{reboot\_structural\_continuum\_promoted\_cyclic\_30mm\_audit}.  The
read is materially cleaner:
\begin{itemize}
  \item on the monotonic \(20\,\mathrm{mm}\) bridge, the global gap drops to
    \(\varepsilon_V^{\max} \approx 3.81\times10^{-1}\),
    \(\varepsilon_V^{\mathrm{rms}} \approx 2.74\times10^{-1}\), while the
    local steel comparison drops to
    \(\varepsilon_{\sigma}^{\max} \approx 1.03\times10^{-1}\),
    \(\varepsilon_{\sigma}^{\mathrm{rms}} \approx 7.07\times10^{-2}\);
  \item extending that same promoted bridge to
    \(5\)--\(10\)--\(15\)--\(20\,\mathrm{mm}\) still converges on both sides,
    with \(\varepsilon_V^{\mathrm{rms}} \approx 4.12\times10^{-1}\) and
    \(\varepsilon_{\sigma}^{\mathrm{rms}} \approx 3.67\times10^{-1}\);
  \item pushing one step further to
    \(5\)--\(10\)--\(15\)--\(20\)--\(25\,\mathrm{mm}\) keeps the promoted
    bridge stable, with \(\varepsilon_V^{\mathrm{rms}} \approx 4.02\times10^{-1}\)
    and \(\varepsilon_{\sigma}^{\mathrm{rms}} \approx 3.71\times10^{-1}\);
  \item extending once more to
    \(5\)--\(10\)--\(15\)--\(20\)--\(25\)--\(30\,\mathrm{mm}\) still keeps
    the promoted bridge operational, with
    \(\varepsilon_V^{\mathrm{rms}} \approx 3.90\times10^{-1}\) and
    \(\varepsilon_{\sigma}^{\mathrm{rms}} \approx 3.79\times10^{-1}\);
  \item the orientation audit still promotes structural fiber \(85\)
    \((y=+0.095\,\mathrm{m}, z=-0.095\,\mathrm{m})\) instead of the nominal
    fiber \(87\).
\end{itemize}

This is no longer an infrastructure failure. Once the same bar, same height,
same preload, same cyclic protocol, and the correct structural-side parity are
enforced on the promoted baseline, the reduced structural slice and the
continuum slice still do not tell the same steel story, but they do so inside a
much narrower and better localized gap.

The more important correction of this pass is that we can now say what the
remaining gap is \emph{not}.  The continuum benchmark now exports the
host-projected axial strain on the same embedded-bar path, so the local
\(host \leftrightarrow\) truss transfer can be audited directly on the
promoted cyclic \(20\,\mathrm{mm}\) slice.  The canonical artifact is now
\texttt{reboot\_structural\_continuum\_promoted\_cyclic\_20mm\_audit/structural\_continuum\_steel\_hysteresis\_summary.json},
and its transfer diagnostics are still almost at machine precision:
\[
\max |\Delta \varepsilon_{host-bar}| \approx 2.20\times10^{-10}, \qquad
\mathrm{rms} |\Delta \varepsilon_{host-bar}| \approx 8.47\times10^{-11},
\]
\[
\max |g_{axial}| \approx 1.86\times10^{-11}\,\mathrm{m}, \qquad
\max |\varepsilon_{truss}-\varepsilon_{projected\ truss}| = 0.
\]
That means the embedded bar is following the projected host kinematics almost
exactly on the selected path.  The open structural--continuum steel mismatch is
therefore no longer credibly explained by a weak penalty transfer or by a
misaligned bar-selection rule.  It points back to the remaining continuum
physics: host constitutive response, distributed cracking, and the way the
continuum shares curvature and tension stiffening with the embedded steel.

To make that statement concrete, we added a dedicated host-neighborhood audit
around the same selected embedded bar path.  The canonical artifact is now
\texttt{reboot\_continuum\_host\_bar\_locality\_promoted\_30mm\_audit/continuum\_host\_bar\_locality\_summary.json},
and its message is strong: the nearest host Gauss point is already much softer
and much more damaged than the bar itself.  On the promoted \texttt{Hex20}
cyclic \(30\,\mathrm{mm}\) slice we now measure
\[
\varepsilon_{\varepsilon}^{\max} \approx 1.17\times10^{-1}, \qquad
\varepsilon_{\varepsilon}^{\mathrm{rms}} \approx 8.14\times10^{-2},
\]
for bar-vs-host axial strain, but
\[
\varepsilon_{\sigma}^{\max} \approx 8.98\times10^{1}, \qquad
\varepsilon_{\sigma}^{\mathrm{rms}} \approx 2.55\times10^{1},
\]
for bar-vs-host axial stress.  The same host point reaches a peak crack count
of three and a maximum crack opening of about
\(9.19\times10^{-4}\), with first cracking around
\(-10\,\mathrm{mm}\) drift.  So the remaining gap is no longer
``bar transfer versus structural fiber''; it is the concrete neighborhood that
the continuum steel is actually living in.

That locality audit still leaves one practical question open: should the
promoted local continuum remain the interior embedded branch, now that the
boundary-bar branch is clearly cheaper?  The dedicated family comparison
\texttt{reboot\_structural\_continuum\_family\_comparison\_30mm} now answers
that on the same clamped structural reference.  Over the
\(5\)--\(10\)--\(15\)--\(20\)--\(25\)--\(30\,\mathrm{mm}\) window:
\begin{itemize}
  \item the promoted \texttt{embedded interior} branch improves the same
    global error to \(\varepsilon_V^{\mathrm{rms}} \approx 3.16\times10^{-1}\)
    while closing in \(\sim 2998\,\mathrm{s}\); and
  \item the \texttt{boundary} branch is cheaper still and globally closer to
    the beam hysteresis
    (\(\varepsilon_V^{\mathrm{rms}} \approx 2.43\times10^{-1}\),
    \(\sim 2628\,\mathrm{s}\)),
    but only by moving the bars to a different physical family.
\end{itemize}
This is why the promotion does not change.  The boundary bars remain a valid
and useful control branch, but the interior embedded chain is still the
reference local model because it preserves the intended structural steel
geometry while materially outperforming the plain host.

That statement is now backed by a dedicated branch-locality comparison in
\texttt{reboot\_structural\_continuum\_branch\_locality\_comparison\_30mm/branch\_locality\_comparison\_summary.json}.
The boundary branch is not simply ``a cheaper version'' of the same steel
layout.  Relative to the structural target, it shifts the selected bar by
\(30\,\mathrm{mm}\) in each section axis:
\[
|y|,|z|:\quad 95\,\mathrm{mm} \rightarrow 125\,\mathrm{mm}.
\]
Along that shifted path the local neighborhood also becomes more severe:
\begin{itemize}
  \item peak nearest-host crack opening grows from about
    \(0.768\,\mathrm{mm}\) to \(0.874\,\mathrm{mm}\);
  \item peak steel stress rises from about
    \(133.9\,\mathrm{MPa}\) to \(169.9\,\mathrm{MPa}\); and
  \item the better global bridge therefore comes with a more aggressive local
    host/bar story, not from a neutral cost reduction alone.
\end{itemize}
This is the stronger reason the promotion does not change. The boundary bars
remain an explicit physical control branch, but the interior embedded chain is
still the reference local model for the future microscale path.

This read has now been extended and, importantly, cleaned of one hidden
methodological inconsistency. The structural--continuum bridge script was still
running the promoted \texttt{Hex20 4x4x2} continuum with
\texttt{longitudinal\_bias\_power = 3}, whereas the canonical
\texttt{plain / embedded interior / boundary} family audit at \texttt{4x4x2}
was using the uniform longitudinal layout. With only \(n_z = 2\), that shifts
the first bar Gauss point appreciably along the member axis and contaminates
the local comparison. The promoted bridge now uses the same uniform
\texttt{4x4x2} longitudinal layout as the family audit, and the current
canonical large-amplitude bundles are:
\begin{itemize}
  \item \texttt{reboot\_structural\_continuum\_promoted\_cyclic\_40mm\_audit},
  \item \texttt{reboot\_structural\_continuum\_promoted\_cyclic\_50mm\_audit},
  \item \texttt{reboot\_continuum\_cover\_core\_cyclic\_audit\_40mm\_branches},
  \item \texttt{reboot\_continuum\_cover\_core\_cyclic\_audit\_50mm\_branches}.
\end{itemize}

The resulting read is already strong enough to promote as the present front:
\begin{itemize}
  \item the promoted interior bridge now closes through
    \(5\)--\(10\)--\(20\)--\(40\,\mathrm{mm}\), with
    \(\varepsilon_V^{\mathrm{rms}} \approx 4.11\times10^{-1}\),
    \(\varepsilon_{\sigma}^{\mathrm{rms}} \approx 4.22\times10^{-1}\), and
    \(\max |\Delta \varepsilon_{host-bar}| \approx 8.84\times10^{-11}\);
  \item it also closes through
    \(5\)--\(10\)--\(20\)--\(30\)--\(40\)--\(50\,\mathrm{mm}\), with
    \(\varepsilon_V^{\mathrm{rms}} \approx 4.80\times10^{-1}\),
    \(\varepsilon_{\sigma}^{\mathrm{rms}} \approx 4.36\times10^{-1}\), and
    \(\max |\Delta \varepsilon_{host-bar}| \approx 1.13\times10^{-10}\);
  \item the nearest-host locality remains much softer and more cracked than
    the embedded steel path: the promoted host neighborhood reaches
    \(\max w_c \approx 1.03\times10^{-3}\,\mathrm{m}\) at \(40\,\mathrm{mm}\)
    and \(\approx 1.27\times10^{-3}\,\mathrm{m}\) at \(50\,\mathrm{mm}\).
\end{itemize}

The family audit also now closes all three \texttt{Hex20 4x4x2} branches at
both \(40\) and \(50\,\mathrm{mm}\). At \(50\,\mathrm{mm}\):
\begin{itemize}
  \item \texttt{plain}: \(V_{\max} \approx 39.07\,\mathrm{kN}\),
    \(t \approx 1802\,\mathrm{s}\);
  \item \texttt{embedded interior}: \(V_{\max} \approx 48.63\,\mathrm{kN}\),
    \(t \approx 4011\,\mathrm{s}\);
  \item \texttt{boundary}: \(V_{\max} \approx 55.04\,\mathrm{kN}\),
    \(t \approx 3113\,\mathrm{s}\).
\end{itemize}
So the promoted interior branch is no longer merely surviving at moderate
amplitudes; it remains fully operational through \(50\,\mathrm{mm}\). The
boundary control is still cheaper and stronger, but it gets there by moving
the bars \(30\,\mathrm{mm}\) outward in each section axis and by worsening the
local host neighborhood. At \(50\,\mathrm{mm}\), for example, the peak nearest
host crack opening grows from about \(1.20\,\mathrm{mm}\) on the interior
branch to about \(1.48\,\mathrm{mm}\) on the boundary branch, while the peak
steel stress grows from about \(230.6\,\mathrm{MPa}\) to
\(289.3\,\mathrm{MPa}\). That is why the promotion still does not change: for
future microscale work, the honest local baseline remains the
\texttt{embedded interior} branch.

The next amplitude step now strengthens that read. The promoted bridge also
closes through \(60\,\mathrm{mm}\), frozen in
\texttt{reboot\_structural\_continuum\_promoted\_cyclic\_60mm\_audit}, with
\(\varepsilon_V^{\mathrm{rms}} \approx 5.69\times10^{-1}\),
\(\varepsilon_\sigma^{\mathrm{rms}} \approx 4.98\times10^{-1}\), and embedded
transfer still closing essentially to machine precision
\((\max |\Delta \varepsilon_{host-bar}| \approx 1.38\times10^{-10})\). The
family audit at the same window,
\texttt{reboot\_continuum\_cover\_core\_cyclic\_audit\_60mm\_branches}, is
equally informative:
\begin{itemize}
  \item \texttt{plain}: \(V_{\max} \approx 44.54\,\mathrm{kN}\),
    \(t \approx 1820\,\mathrm{s}\);
  \item \texttt{embedded interior}: \(V_{\max} \approx 56.33\,\mathrm{kN}\),
    \(t \approx 3419\,\mathrm{s}\);
  \item \texttt{boundary}: \(V_{\max} \approx 64.06\,\mathrm{kN}\),
    \(t \approx 2878\,\mathrm{s}\).
\end{itemize}
At \(60\,\mathrm{mm}\), the dedicated locality comparison in
\texttt{reboot\_structural\_continuum\_branch\_locality\_comparison\_60mm}
actually becomes more favorable to the promoted interior branch again. The
boundary branch remains cheaper, but now it is also globally worse
\((\varepsilon_V^{\mathrm{rms,active}} \approx 1.13\) versus
\(\approx 1.03)\) and locally harsher
\((w_{c,\max} \approx 1.87\,\mathrm{mm}\) versus
\(\approx 1.43\,\mathrm{mm}\),
\(\sigma_{s,\max} \approx 351.8\,\mathrm{MPa}\) versus
\(\approx 282.6\,\mathrm{MPa})\).

Pushing the same promoted bridge one stage further, to the
\(75\,\mathrm{mm}\) cyclic window in
\texttt{reboot\_structural\_continuum\_promoted\_cyclic\_75mm\_audit}, shows
where the next real frontier lies:
\begin{itemize}
  \item the continuum still completes;
  \item the embedded transfer still closes almost to machine precision
    \((\max |\Delta \varepsilon_{host-bar}| \approx 1.78\times10^{-10})\);
  \item but the global hysteresis mismatch jumps sharply
    \((\varepsilon_V^{\mathrm{rms}} \approx 2.30)\); while
  \item the local steel mismatch rises much more moderately
    \((\varepsilon_\sigma^{\mathrm{rms}} \approx 5.50\times10^{-1})\).
\end{itemize}
So the current promoted local model is no longer failing because of the
embedded tie or because it cannot survive larger amplitudes. It now survives
through \(75\,\mathrm{mm}\), and the next frontier is more specific: the host
continuum physics around the steel path is drifting away from the structural
reference faster than the steel carrier itself.

The family audit at the same window,
\texttt{reboot\_continuum\_cover\_core\_cyclic\_audit\_75mm\_branches}, confirms
that all three \texttt{Hex20 4x4x2} branches are still operational:
\begin{itemize}
  \item \texttt{plain}: \(V_{\max} \approx 51.02\,\mathrm{kN}\),
    \(t \approx 2002\,\mathrm{s}\);
  \item \texttt{embedded interior}: \(V_{\max} \approx 66.50\,\mathrm{kN}\),
    \(t \approx 4051\,\mathrm{s}\);
  \item \texttt{boundary}: \(V_{\max} \approx 74.87\,\mathrm{kN}\),
    \(t \approx 3458\,\mathrm{s}\).
\end{itemize}
And the updated locality comparison in
\texttt{reboot\_structural\_continuum\_branch\_locality\_comparison\_75mm}
keeps the same promotion logic as the \(60\,\mathrm{mm}\) window, only more
clearly:
\begin{itemize}
  \item \texttt{embedded interior}:
    \(\varepsilon_V^{\mathrm{rms,active}} \approx 1.10\),
    \(\varepsilon_\sigma^{\mathrm{rms,active}} \approx 0.80\);
  \item \texttt{boundary}:
    \(\varepsilon_V^{\mathrm{rms,active}} \approx 1.22\),
    \(\varepsilon_\sigma^{\mathrm{rms,active}} \approx 0.96\).
\end{itemize}
So once the window is large enough, the \texttt{boundary} branch is not only a
different physical family; it is also a worse comparator for the intended
steel path, even though it remains somewhat cheaper. That is a much more robust
place to stop this validation stage than the earlier ambiguity in which the
boundary path sometimes looked globally better.

To test whether that remaining gap was truly concentrated in the immediate
host neighborhood of the promoted steel path, the continuum benchmark now
exposes an explicit host-probe surface:
\begin{itemize}
  \item \texttt{--host-probe label:x:y:z};
  \item \texttt{host\_probe\_history.csv}; and
  \item the canonical audit
    \texttt{reboot\_continuum\_host\_probe\_family\_audit\_75mm}.
\end{itemize}
This is a real benchmark-surface extension rather than an ad hoc plotter.  It
lets the same executable report the nearest actual host Gauss-point history at
any declared coordinate, regardless of whether the branch has embedded steel.
On the current \(75\,\mathrm{mm}\) window, using the promoted interior steel
coordinate \((x,y,z)=(0.095,\,0.095,\,0.338119785)\,\mathrm{m}\), the read is
surprisingly sharp:
\begin{itemize}
  \item \texttt{plain}: host-probe active stress RMS versus the promoted
    interior branch \(\approx 6.52\times10^{-2}\);
  \item \texttt{boundary}: the same metric is only
    \(\approx 1.26\times10^{-2}\);
  \item the corresponding active strain RMS values are
    \(\approx 1.87\times10^{-2}\) and \(\approx 1.21\times10^{-2}\);
  \item all three branches recover the same nearest-host distance
    \(\approx 1.5787\times10^{-1}\,\mathrm{m}\), because the host mesh itself
    is unchanged.
\end{itemize}
So the concrete point right at the intended steel coordinate does \emph{not}
separate nearly as much as the global hysteresis does.  This narrows the
physics frontier substantially: the remaining \texttt{beam vs continuum} gap
is now better read as a more distributed host-field difference than as a
purely pointwise failure of the concrete immediately surrounding the steel.
The new probe surface also validates cleanly against the same-run embedded-bar
host read on the promoted interior branch:
\[
\max |\Delta \sigma_{host}^{probe-bar}| \approx 5.0\times10^{-8}\,\mathrm{MPa},
\qquad
\max |\Delta \varepsilon_{host}^{probe-bar}| \approx 4.0\times10^{-12}.
\]
So this benchmark extension is now trustworthy enough to stay on the promoted
validation ladder.

  \centering
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_continuum_host_probe_stress_75mm.png}
  \end{minipage}\hfill
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_continuum_host_probe_crack_opening_75mm.png}
  \end{minipage}
  \caption{Explicit host-probe comparison at the promoted steel coordinate on
  the \(75\,\mathrm{mm}\) window. Left: host axial stress. Right: host crack
  opening. The three branches remain much closer at that single physical host
  coordinate than the global hysteresis gap alone would suggest, which is why
  the current frontier is now interpreted as a more distributed host-field
  difference rather than a strictly pointwise steel-neighborhood failure.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-continuum-host-probe-75mm}
\end{figure}

  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.82\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_structural_continuum_hysteresis_overlay_50mm.png}
  \caption{First structural--continuum cyclic pilot on the embedded-bar
  continuum slice. The comparison is still far from closed, but it is now
  physically aligned: same eight-bar layout, same preload, and same declared
  cyclic protocol.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-structural-continuum-hysteresis-overlay}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \begin{minipage}{0.58\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_structural_continuum_branch_locality_overlay_50mm.png}
  \end{minipage}\hfill
  \begin{minipage}{0.38\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_structural_continuum_branch_locality_overview_50mm.png}
  \end{minipage}
  \caption{Interior-versus-boundary branch locality comparison on the
  \(20\,\mathrm{mm}\) cyclic window. The boundary branch is globally closer and
  cheaper, but it also shifts the steel outward and produces a more strongly
  cracked host neighborhood around the selected bar.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-continuum-branch-locality}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering

  and the embedded truss of the promoted \texttt{Hex20} continuum pilot.
  Left: stress--strain hysteresis. Right: stress versus imposed drift. The
  mismatch is now a physical validation result, not a bar-layout,
  height-alignment, or section-side artifact.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-structural-continuum-steel-overlay}
\end{figure}

  structural--continuum bridge. Left: embedded steel strain versus the
  host-projected axial strain on the same path. Right: projected host--bar
  mismatch. The transfer itself closes almost to machine precision, so the
  remaining steel discrepancy must come from the continuum physics rather than
  from the kinematic coupling layer.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-structural-continuum-embedded-transfer}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]

  embedded bar and the nearest host Gauss point no longer tell the same local
  constitutive story: strain stays of the same order, but the host stress is
  far softer and the host neighborhood accumulates significant cracking.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-continuum-host-bar-locality}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_structural_continuum_family_hysteresis_50mm.png}
  \end{minipage}\hfill
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_structural_continuum_family_error_timing_50mm.png}
  \end{minipage}
  \caption{Structural-vs-continuum family audit over the promoted
  \(5\)--\(10\)--\(15\)--\(20\,\mathrm{mm}\) window. Left: hysteresis overlay for the
  clamped structural reference against the three cover/core-aware continuum
  branches. Right: active global error versus cost. The boundary branch is
  globally closer and cheaper, but it belongs to a different physical family,
  so the interior embedded branch remains the promoted local baseline.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-structural-continuum-family}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.82\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_structural_continuum_family_steel_50mm.png}
  \caption{Steel-stress comparison for the embedded continuum families against
  the same matched structural fiber. The boundary branch is again closer on the
  global benchmark, but it does so by relocating the bars onto the host
  boundary rather than by improving the structural-matched interior physics.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-structural-continuum-family-steel}
\end{figure}

Pushing the same promoted bridge one step further, to the
\(5\)--\(10\)--\(15\)--\(20\)--\(25\,\mathrm{mm}\) cyclic window frozen in
\texttt{reboot\_structural\_continuum\_promoted\_cyclic\_25mm\_audit}, keeps the
bridge itself stable: the global base-shear RMS stays near
\(4.02\times 10^{-1}\), the local steel RMS stays near
\(3.71\times 10^{-1}\), and the embedded transfer still closes almost to
machine precision
\((\max |\varepsilon_{\text{bar}}-\varepsilon_{\text{host}}| \approx 2.77\times10^{-10})\).
But the family comparison at
\texttt{reboot\_structural\_continuum\_family\_comparison\_25mm} exposes the
first sharp operational frontier: the promoted \texttt{embedded interior}
branch no longer finishes within the current \(2400\,\mathrm{s}\) budget,
whereas the \texttt{boundary} branch still completes in
\(\sim 2077\,\mathrm{s}\) with active global RMS
\(\sim 2.57\times 10^{-1}\), peak nearest-host crack opening
\(\sim 0.731\,\mathrm{mm}\), and peak steel stress \(\sim 141\,\mathrm{MPa}\).
That keeps \texttt{boundary} as a useful frontier control branch, but not as a
legitimate replacement for the structural-matched interior baseline that we
want to promote toward future microscale work.

The same validation thread turns the quadratic rebar path into an explicit
frontier rather than a vague possibility.  The new preload-only audit
\texttt{reboot\_quadratic\_embedded\_preload\_audit\_v1/quadratic\_embedded\_preload\_summary.json}
is sharper than the older ad-hoc probes:
\begin{itemize}
  \item the \texttt{Truss2 preload} control completes in
        \(\sim 0.62\,\mathrm{s}\);
  \item the \texttt{Truss3 zero-load} control also completes in
        \(\sim 0.20\,\mathrm{s}\);
  \item but \texttt{Truss3 preload} fails before the first accepted step; and
  \item reducing \(\alpha/E_c\) from \(10^4\) to \(10^2\) does not rescue it.
\end{itemize}
That is a strong negative result: the quadratic route is not failing because of
cyclic reversal or Ko--Bathe cracking.  It already fails in an
\emph{elasticized preload-only} setting.  The standalone quadratic truss unit
test still passes, so the open issue is localized even more tightly to the
embedded-solid quadratic-bar chain under axial preload, not to the isolated
\texttt{TrussElement<3>} interpolation taken by itself.

That standalone statement is now frozen as a proper benchmark rather than as a
mere unit test. The canonical artifact
\texttt{reboot\_truss3\_cyclic\_compression\_baseline/runtime\_manifest.json}
drives a single \texttt{TrussElement<3,3>} through a purely compressive
Menegotto--Pinto return protocol using the \emph{same steel factory} that feeds
both the reduced RC fiber section and the embedded-continuum rebar carriers.
The setup is deliberately affine: the middle node follows
\(u_m = \tfrac{1}{2}u_r\), so the exact solution is uniform axial strain and
all nonuniformity should vanish.

The benchmark closes essentially to machine precision:
\[
\max |\Delta \sigma| \approx 3.41\times10^{-13}\,\mathrm{MPa},\qquad
\max |\Delta E_t| \approx 4.51\times10^{-10}\,\mathrm{MPa},
\]
with Gauss-point strain spread
\[
\max \Delta \varepsilon_{\mathrm{gp}} \approx 3.04\times10^{-18},
\]
stress spread
\[
\max \Delta \sigma_{\mathrm{gp}} \approx 3.98\times10^{-13}\,\mathrm{MPa},
\]
and middle-node force closure
\[
\max |f_m| \approx 6.81\times10^{-17}\,\mathrm{MN}.
\]
This is already a strong positive result: the isolated quadratic truss
formulation is not the source of the continuum-vs-structural gap.

The benchmark also forced one correction in the validation logic itself. For a
quadratic truss driven by the affine path
\(\mathbf{u}_e(\delta)=\delta[0,0,0,\tfrac{1}{2},0,0,1,0,0]^T\), the
equivalent element tangent cannot be read from the raw matrix entry
\(K_{rr}\) alone. The correct scalar tangent is the projection of the element
matrix onto the imposed kinematic path,
\[
\frac{\mathrm{d}F_r}{\mathrm{d}\delta}
  = \mathbf{e}_r^T \mathbf{K}_e
    \begin{bmatrix}
