      0 & 0 & 0 & \tfrac{1}{2} & 0 & 0 & 1 & 0 & 0
    \end{bmatrix}^{\!T},
\]
and only after multiplying by \(L/A\) does that projected stiffness map back
to the equivalent uniaxial tangent. Once that projection was enforced, the
element-level tangent closed with the Menegotto tangent just as tightly as the
stress history itself. That matters because it removes one more false suspect
from the embedded-bar chain: the open frontier now remains genuinely in the
host-bar-preload combination, not in the standalone \texttt{Truss<3>} member.

The same standalone bundle also makes one constitutive detail explicit. After
the larger compressive returns, the current Menegotto--Pinto parameterization
can leave a non-zero stress at zero total strain; in the canonical bundle the
final zero-strain return reaches about \(+3.63\times10^2\,\mathrm{MPa}\). The
direct material point and the standalone quadratic truss reproduce exactly the
same residual return, so that behaviour must be read as a constitutive outcome
of the audited steel law under this protocol, not as an interpolation defect
of \texttt{TrussElement<3,3>}.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.95\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_truss3_cyclic_menegotto_equivalence.png}
  \caption{Standalone \texttt{TrussElement<3,3>} cyclic audit against the
  direct Menegotto--Pinto material point using the same steel factory as the
  reduced RC section and the promoted continuum rebar path. The stress history
  and the projected element tangent both close essentially to machine
  precision.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-truss3-menegotto-equivalence}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.82\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_truss3_cyclic_force_displacement.png}
  \caption{Standalone quadratic-truss force--displacement loop and
  Gauss-point stress-spread trace. The affine protocol keeps the three
  Gauss-point stresses collapsed to the same path, while the non-zero return at
  zero total strain remains a constitutive feature of the current
  Menegotto--Pinto parameterization rather than an element inconsistency.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-truss3-force-displacement}
\end{figure}

That isolated closure then enabled a stricter steel-chain audit, frozen in
\texttt{reboot\_steel\_chain\_audit\_75mm/steel\_chain\_summary.json}. The same
\((5,10,20,30,40,50,60,75)\,\mathrm{mm}\) cyclic window was replayed through four
levels of the chain:
\begin{itemize}
  \item direct Menegotto--Pinto point;
  \item standalone \texttt{Truss<3>};
  \item the promoted continuum embedded bar; and
  \item the matched structural steel trace used in the beam--continuum bridge.
\end{itemize}

The read is now much sharper. On the promoted continuum bar, the chain
\(\texttt{material} \rightarrow \texttt{Truss<3>} \rightarrow
\texttt{embedded bar}\) collapses essentially to machine precision:
\[
\mathrm{rms}|\Delta \sigma| \approx 2.30\times10^{-7}\,\mathrm{MPa},\qquad
\mathrm{rms}_{\text{active}}(\varepsilon_\sigma) \approx 1.34\times10^{-9}.
\]
On the already interpolated matched structural steel trace, however, the same
direct material and standalone truss replays still show a much larger mismatch
\((\mathrm{rms}|\Delta \sigma| \approx 12.41\,\mathrm{MPa})\). That could be
misread as a constitutive error on the structural side, but the same audit
then replays the actual lower and upper structural sites separately and
interpolates those site replays back to the matched station:
\begin{itemize}
  \item lower site replay:
    \(\mathrm{rms}|\Delta \sigma| \approx 3.74\,\mathrm{MPa}\);
  \item upper site replay: essentially exact; and
  \item interpolated site replay:
    \(\mathrm{rms}|\Delta \sigma| \approx 1.87\,\mathrm{MPa}\).
\end{itemize}
So the large structural-side gap is no longer credibly attributed to the steel
material assignment or to the standalone \texttt{Truss<3>} carrier. It is
primarily a trace-construction effect: the promoted matched structural trace is
an axial interpolation between two section-fiber histories, not the history of
a single constitutive material point. At \(75\,\mathrm{mm}\), the same audit
also shows a physically meaningful asymmetry: the lower structural site is the
one drifting first, while the upper site still tracks the constitutive replay
almost exactly. That moves the open frontier away from the continuum steel
carrier and toward the distributed structural section response under large
reversal.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_steel_chain_replay_75mm.png}
  \end{minipage}\hfill
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_steel_chain_structural_sites_75mm.png}
  \end{minipage}
  \caption{Steel-chain audit at \(75\,\mathrm{mm}\). Left: direct replay on the
  promoted matched traces. Right: replay of the actual lower and upper
  structural sites, showing that the large mismatch belongs mainly to the axial
  interpolation of beam-fiber histories, not to the Menegotto assignment or to
  the standalone \texttt{Truss<3>} formulation.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-steel-chain-audit}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.72\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_quadratic_embedded_preload_frontier.png}
  \caption{Quadratic embedded-bar preload frontier. The two-node preload case
  and the three-node zero-load control both complete, but the three-node
  preload case still fails before the first accepted step even after reducing
  the penalty scale.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-quadratic-embedded-preload-frontier}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Continuum mesh and nonlinear-solver audit on the steel bridge}

Once the structural--continuum steel bridge was physically aligned, the next
question became narrower and more falsifiable: can the remaining mismatch still
be explained by a coarse continuum mesh or by an unlucky nonlinear solver
choice?  The canonical artifact is now
\texttt{reboot\_structural\_continuum\_refinement\_solver\_audit/structural\_continuum\_refinement\_solver\_audit\_summary.json}.

The mesh audit is already quite sharp:
\begin{itemize}
  \item \texttt{Hex20 2x2x2 + newton-l2-only} closes in
    \(\sim 194.05\,\mathrm{s}\) with
    \(\varepsilon_V^{\mathrm{rms}} \approx 1.2702\) and
    \(\varepsilon_\sigma^{\mathrm{rms}} \approx 4.446\times10^{-1}\);
  \item \texttt{Hex20 3x3x2 + newton-l2-only} closes in
    \(\sim 718.25\,\mathrm{s}\) with essentially the same mismatch,
    \(\varepsilon_V^{\mathrm{rms}} \approx 1.2684\) and
    \(\varepsilon_\sigma^{\mathrm{rms}} \approx 4.444\times10^{-1}\); and
  \item \texttt{Hex20 2x2x4 + newton-l2-only} still exceeds the current
    \(1800\,\mathrm{s}\) audit budget.
\end{itemize}

This is the important negative result of the pass: the open
structural--continuum gap is no longer credibly explained by cross-section
resolution alone. Refining \texttt{2x2x2} to \texttt{3x3x2} multiplies cost by
about \(3.7\times\) while buying essentially no closure in the global loop or
in the local steel history.

The solver audit is equally instructive on the same \texttt{Hex20 2x2x2}
slice:
\begin{itemize}
  \item \texttt{newton-l2-only} remains the promoted continuum baseline;
  \item \texttt{canonical-cascade} also closes, but at
    \(\sim 346.60\,\mathrm{s}\);
  \item \texttt{newton-trust-region-dogleg-only} does close the full slice,
    but only in \(\sim 1844.27\,\mathrm{s}\), so it is a viable Newton-family
    fallback, not a promotable baseline;
  \item \texttt{anderson-only} and \texttt{nonlinear-gmres-only} both fail
    before the first accepted preload step, with repeated divergence at
    vanishingly small increments and no exported rebar history;
  \item \texttt{nonlinear-cg-only} still exceeds the current
    \(1200\,\mathrm{s}\) budget; and
  \item \texttt{arc-length} was tested as a branch candidate and fails
    immediately for a principled reason: the reduced-column continuum baseline
    does not yet expose a dedicated arc-length wrapper for the embedded-solid
    slice.
\end{itemize}

That distinction matters methodologically. The audit now separates:
\begin{itemize}
  \item routes that are physically valid but too expensive
    (\texttt{dogleg});
  \item routes that fail to initialize the preload
    (\texttt{anderson}, \texttt{ngmres}); and
  \item routes that are not yet exposed as validated continuations
    (\texttt{arc-length} on this continuum baseline).
\end{itemize}

This pass also hardened the benchmark infrastructure itself. The continuum
audit workers now kill the full Windows process tree on timeout instead of
leaving orphan PETSc benchmark processes alive in the background. That is not
cosmetic cleanup: without it, timing and completion status of the solver audit
would be methodologically ambiguous.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_structural_continuum_mesh_refinement_hysteresis.png}
  \end{minipage}\hfill
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_structural_continuum_mesh_refinement_steel_error.png}
  \end{minipage}
  \caption{Continuum mesh audit on the aligned steel bridge. Left: global
  hysteresis overlays for the completed \texttt{Hex20} meshes. Right: RMS
  steel-stress mismatch. The physical gap barely moves from
  \texttt{2x2x2} to \texttt{3x3x2} despite the much larger cost.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-continuum-mesh-audit}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_structural_continuum_solver_policy_timing.png}
  \end{minipage}\hfill
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_structural_continuum_solver_policy_steel_error.png}
  \end{minipage}
  \caption{Continuum nonlinear-solver audit on the aligned steel bridge.
  Left: reported solve time. Right: local steel mismatch for the policies that
  actually close. The promoted conclusion is not ``use every solver'', but
  ``keep \texttt{newton-l2-only} as baseline, reserve \texttt{dogleg} as an
  expensive fallback, and do not promote \texttt{arc-length} until the
  continuum baseline exposes a dedicated continuation wrapper''.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-continuum-solver-audit}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Monotonic continuum foundation audit}

Before reopening larger cyclic windows on the embedded-solid slice, we stepped
back once more and asked a more basic question: is the continuum mismatch
really coming from the embedded bars, the longitudinal mesh, or the crack
stabilization profile?  The canonical artifact is now
\texttt{reboot\_continuum\_physics\_foundation\_audit\_v3/continuum\_physics\_foundation\_summary.json}.

The first result remains a useful negative one. Refining the longitudinal mesh
from \texttt{Hex20 2x2x2} to \texttt{Hex20 2x2x10} reduces the peak embedded
gap by almost one order of magnitude, but it does not materially close the
elastic bridge: the embedded elastic response moves only from about
\(0.497\,\mathrm{kN}\) to \(0.483\,\mathrm{kN}\), while the structural beam
reference remains at about \(0.536\,\mathrm{kN}\).  A base-clustered
\(z\)-bias does not rescue that gap either.

The second result is equally sharp. Varying the penalty coupling scale over two
orders of magnitude, from \(10^3 E_c\) to \(10^5 E_c\), leaves both the
elastic and the nonlinear \texttt{Hex20 2x2x2} response essentially unchanged.
This matters because it removes another tempting but weak explanation: the
current global mismatch is not being driven by a poorly tuned embedding spring.

The third result is the most instructive. We made the Ko-Bathe 3D profile
explicit and separated two paths:
\begin{itemize}
  \item \texttt{benchmark\_reference}: requested
    \(t_p = 0.02\), internally clamped to the article floor
    \(t_p = 0.05\), with paper-reference crack retention
    \(\eta_N = 10^{-4}\), \(\eta_S = 0.1\); and
  \item \texttt{production\_stabilized}: implicit
    \(t_p \approx 0.0965\) for \(f'_c = 30\,\mathrm{MPa}\), with the
    FE\textsuperscript{2}-oriented stabilized profile
    \(\eta_N = 0.2\), \(\eta_S = 0.5\).
\end{itemize}

That asymmetry was real and worth correcting. Yet the corrected audit also
shows that it is not the dominant source of the global monotonic gap. On the
host-only \texttt{Hex20 2x2x2} slice at \(20\,\mathrm{mm}\), switching from
\texttt{production\_stabilized} to \texttt{benchmark\_reference} changes the
crack field substantially: the peak cracked-Gauss-point count rises from
\(42\) to \(48\) and the maximum crack opening drops from about
\(7.35\times10^{-4}\) to \(4.65\times10^{-4}\).  On the embedded slice, the
same switch moves the cracked-Gauss-point count from \(48\) to \(66\) and
reduces the peak crack opening from about \(7.21\times10^{-4}\) to
\(4.81\times10^{-4}\).  But the peak base shear hardly moves at all:
\(\sim 17.09\,\mathrm{kN}\) for the host-only column and
\(\sim 20.73\,\mathrm{kN}\) for the embedded one in both profiles.

This is the important constitutive conclusion of the pass. The benchmark had
indeed been mixing a production-oriented crack profile into a physical
validation path, and that needed to be made explicit. But once that asymmetry
is corrected, the dominant monotonic gap still does not collapse. The host
continuum alone remains much stronger than the reduced structural baseline
(\(\sim 17.09\,\mathrm{kN}\) versus \(\sim 7.67\,\mathrm{kN}\)), so the next
scientific target is no longer the embedding penalty or the crack profile in
isolation. It is the effective compressive/sectional response of the Ko-Bathe
3D host under the same reduced-column loading path.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_continuum_foundation_penalty_sensitivity.png}
  \end{minipage}\hfill
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_continuum_foundation_nonlinear_overlay.png}
  \end{minipage}
  \caption{Monotonic continuum foundation audit. Left: penalty sensitivity,
  showing that the coupling scale is not the dominant cause of the gap. Right:
  nonlinear push-over overlays, now with the benchmark-reference and
  production-stabilized Ko-Bathe profiles separated explicitly. The crack
  statistics change, but the global base-shear mismatch remains essentially in
  place.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-continuum-foundation-audit}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Crack-band confinement and shear-retention control}

The next continuum pass separated three mechanisms that had been too easy to
mix in the same input knob: proxy tensile stiffness before cracking, residual
tension stiffness after cracking, and residual shear transfer across an open
crack.  The continuum run specification now exposes the crack-band parameters
\(\eta_t^{res}\), \(\eta_s^{res}\), and \(\eta_c^{open}\) through
\texttt{--concrete-crack-band-residual-tension-ratio},
\texttt{--concrete-crack-band-residual-shear-ratio}, and
\texttt{--concrete-crack-band-open-compression-transfer-ratio}.  The older
\texttt{--concrete-tension-stiffness-ratio} remains the proxy-material input;
it no longer silently controls the cyclic crack-band residual floor.

The confined core is now passed consistently to
\texttt{CyclicCrackBandConcrete3D}: the core branch uses
\[
  K = 1+\rho_s f_{yh}/f'_c,\qquad
  f'_{cc}=K f'_c,\qquad
  \varepsilon_{c0,c}=-0.002K,
\]
with the Kent--Park confinement contribution
\(\varepsilon_{50h}=0.75\rho_s\sqrt{h'/s}\).  This is intentionally a
controlled Kent--Park-compatible confinement bridge, not yet a full Mander
model, but it prevents the continuum core from using a higher strength without
the matching ductility and descending-branch slope.

The decisive observation was not the tension floor.  On the cheap
\texttt{Hex8 4x4x2} monotonic control at \(20\,\mathrm{mm}\), the previous
crack-band shear retention \(\eta_s^{res}=0.20\) produced
\(|V_b|_{\max}=37.44\,\mathrm{kN}\), while the free-rotation structural
reference was \(7.72\,\mathrm{kN}\).  Holding the confinement and all boundary
conditions fixed but reducing only the open-crack shear retention to
\(\eta_s^{res}=0.01\) moved the continuum to
\(|V_b|_{\max}=8.32\,\mathrm{kN}\), with peak-normalized RMS path error
\(5.35\times10^{-2}\).  The solve time also dropped from roughly
\(102\,\mathrm{s}\) to \(10.9\,\mathrm{s}\).  The physical reading is that
stirrups should not be represented by a large global shear-retention floor in
every cracked concrete point.  They should enter as confined-core ductility
and, later, as an explicit transverse-reinforcement policy.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.72\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/continuum_structural_cyclic_50mm_shear_floor_001.png}
  \caption{First cyclic structural--continuum control after separating
  confinement from open-crack shear retention.  With
  \(\eta_s^{res}=0.01\), the \texttt{Hex8 4x4x2} continuum reaches
  \(19.26\,\mathrm{kN}\) against \(18.06\,\mathrm{kN}\) for the free-rotation
  structural fiber reference at \(50\,\mathrm{mm}\).  The loop shape is not yet
  closed, especially on unloading/reloading, but the peak strength and
  algorithmic stability are no longer dominated by artificial shear locking in
  cracked concrete.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-continuum-shear-retention-50mm}
\end{figure}

The same candidate was then pushed to \(200\,\mathrm{mm}\).  With only two
steps per branch, the first \(50\,\mathrm{mm}\) point followed an unacceptable
spurious path; with eight steps per branch the path is reproducible.  The
continuum completes in \(83.4\,\mathrm{s}\), the structural reference in
\(47.5\,\mathrm{s}\).  The large-amplitude comparison is not closed:
\[
  |V_b|_{\max}^{cont}=34.53\,\mathrm{kN},\qquad
  |V_b|_{\max}^{struct}=23.16\,\mathrm{kN},\qquad
  \mathrm{RMS}_{V_b}=3.32\times10^{-1}.
\]
Thus \(\eta_s^{res}=0.01\) is a useful stabilization and low-amplitude
equivalence point, not a final physical concrete law.  The next continuum
model must add a richer crack-closing/degradation memory and an explicit
transverse-reinforcement policy instead of hiding stirrup effects in one
global open-crack shear-retention scalar.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.72\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/continuum_structural_cyclic_200mm_steps8_shear_floor_001.png}
  \caption{Large-amplitude cyclic escalation of the same crack-band branch at
  \(200\,\mathrm{mm}\), now with eight steps per branch.  The model remains
  algorithmically stable, but it overestimates the peak strength relative to
  the free-rotation structural fiber reference.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-continuum-shear-retention-200mm}
\end{figure}

\paragraph{Discrete transverse reinforcement audit.}
The following pass tested the hypothesis that the remaining large-amplitude
gap was mainly missing stirrup confinement or transverse shear reinforcement.
The implementation was deliberately architectural rather than case-specific:
\texttt{PrismaticDomainBuilder} now accepts arbitrary embedded rebar
polylines, stores their host interpolation coordinates, and reuses the same
penalty-coupling kernel used by the longitudinal bars.  The reduced-column
benchmark exposes this through
\texttt{--transverse-reinforcement-mode embedded-stirrup-loops}.  Each loop is
placed at the Kent--Park tie spacing, on the core boundary
\((b-2c)\times(h-2c)\), with an equivalent stirrup area derived from the
volumetric ratio,
\[
  A_{st} =
  {\rho_s A_{core}s \over p_{loop}},
  \qquad
  p_{loop}=2\{(b-2c)+(h-2c)\}.
\]
This makes the trial falsifiable: if the missing mechanism were simply
perfectly bonded transverse steel, the curve should move toward the structural
reference.

The first implementation pass did move the curve, but a subsequent reaction
audit showed that this reading was incomplete.  The benchmark now separates
two support quantities:
\[
  V_{\mathrm{cut}}
  =
  \sum_{a\in\Gamma_0}
  f^{\mathrm{mat}}_{a,x},
  \qquad
  V_{\mathrm{alg}}
  =
  \sum_{a\in\Gamma_0}
  \left(
    f^{\mathrm{mat}}_{a,x}
    +
    f^{\mathrm{pen}}_{a,x}
  \right),
\]
where \(V_{\mathrm{cut}}\) is the physical material cut through concrete and
bars, while \(V_{\mathrm{alg}}\) is an equilibrium diagnostic of the PETSc
system including penalty constraints.  The cyclic hysteresis observable keeps
using \(V_{\mathrm{cut}}\); the algebraic quantity is written only to the
manifest/control trace to expose possible artificial constraint reactions.

With that split, the \(200\,\mathrm{mm}\) audit reads as follows.  The current
free-rotation structural reference gives
\(|V_b|_{\max}\approx23.16\,\mathrm{kN}\).  The crack-band continuum without
discrete stirrups gives \(42.48\,\mathrm{kN}\).  Adding fully bonded stirrup
loops gives \(42.10\,\mathrm{kN}\), and reducing the effective stirrup area to
\(25\%\) gives \(42.10\,\mathrm{kN}\) as well.  The transverse steel remains
elastic in both cases:
\(|\sigma_{st}|_{\max}\approx13.7\,\mathrm{MPa}\) for full area and
\(\approx54.6\,\mathrm{MPa}\) for the quarter-area audit.  Thus the missing
equivalence is no longer credibly explained by absent transverse steel.  The
dominant open front is the continuum host plus longitudinal embedded-bar
mechanism: the host-only control already contributes about
\(19.5\,\mathrm{kN}\), and the longitudinal steel branch raises the peak to
about \(42.5\,\mathrm{kN}\).  The explicit stirrup branch is therefore kept as
an extension point and a negative/control branch until bond-slip, dowel action
and localized crack-opening semantics are promoted.

The same pass exposed a cost and localization gate for longitudinal
refinement.  A direct \(\texttt{Hex8}\;4\times4\times6\) crack-band run at
\(200\,\mathrm{mm}\), even in monotonic form, did not complete within the
diagnostic wall-time budget and had to be stopped.  The cheaper tensile
crack-band proxy does complete, but its response is not reliable enough for a
quantitative equivalence gate.  Holding the characteristic length fixed at
\(l_c=1600\,\mathrm{mm}\) does not by itself fix the response.  The useful
crack-band evidence comes instead from the intermediate
\(\texttt{Hex8}\;4\times4\times4\) branch: it completes to
\(50\), \(100\), and \(150\,\mathrm{mm}\) with peaks of about
\(14.94\), \(23.73\), and \(27.41\,\mathrm{kN}\), respectively, under the same
fixed-\(l_c\) policy.  The corresponding positive structural branch gives
about \(18.03\), \(23.50\), and \(24.98\,\mathrm{kN}\).  Thus the refined
continuum is physically plausible through \(150\,\mathrm{mm}\), but the
\(200\,\mathrm{mm}\) continuation remains an algorithmic/material frontier:
both the single LU--L2 profile and the canonical cascade stalled within the
diagnostic budget.  The next continuum gate is therefore not blind refinement,
but a continuation/material-stabilization study targeted at the post-cracking
frontier between \(150\) and \(200\,\mathrm{mm}\).

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.82\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/continuum_monotonic_refinement_probe_fixedlc1600.png}
  \caption{Monotonic structural--continuum refinement probe with fixed
  crack-band length \(l_c=1600\,\mathrm{mm}\).  The
  \(\texttt{Hex8}\;4\times4\times4\) continuum branch is credible through
  \(150\,\mathrm{mm}\), but the \(200\,\mathrm{mm}\) step requires a stronger
  continuation/material treatment before it can be promoted to the cyclic
  validation matrix.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-continuum-monotonic-refinement-probe-fixedlc}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.78\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/continuum_transverse_reinforcement_audit_200mm.png}
  \caption{Discrete transverse-reinforcement audit at \(200\,\mathrm{mm}\).
  The corrected material-cut resultant shows that perfectly bonded embedded
  stirrup loops remain elastic and do not explain the structural--continuum
  gap.  The remaining front is the host plus longitudinal embedded-bar
  mechanism, while transverse reinforcement stays as a future bond-slip/dowel
  extension.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-continuum-transverse-reinforcement-audit-200mm}
\end{figure}

\paragraph{Structural plastic-hinge audit.}
The continuum comparison also forced a sanity check on the structural
reference itself.  The answer is nuanced.  The structural model does capture a
plastic hinge in the reduced, section-distributed sense: curvature and steel
yielding concentrate near the fixed end, while the upper stations remain
mostly elastic.  However, the local hinge fields are quadrature-sensitive and
must not be treated as a unique continuum-scale truth.

At \(200\,\mathrm{mm}\), the fast \(\texttt{N=4}\) Gauss--Legendre reference
has only three active section stations and its first station is not at the
physical base, but at \(z\approx180\,\mathrm{mm}\).  It gives
\(|V_b|_{\max}\approx25.05\,\mathrm{kN}\), base-side
\(\max|\kappa_y|\approx6.99\times10^{-2}\,\mathrm{m}^{-1}\), and peak steel
stress around \(440\,\mathrm{MPa}\).  Switching the same \(N=4\) element to
Lobatto puts a section exactly at the base and gives a similar moment but a
slightly lower global peak,
\(|V_b|_{\max}\approx22.23\,\mathrm{kN}\).  Increasing to \(N=10\) makes the
global shear remain in the same range, \(\approx23.4\text{--}23.9\,\mathrm{kN}\),
but it changes the local hinge read substantially.  The current best
structural hinge diagnostic is \(\texttt{N=10}\) Lobatto: it has a base
station, resolves yielded steel over approximately the first
\(260\,\mathrm{mm}\), and gives
\(\max|\kappa_y|\approx2.80\times10^{-1}\,\mathrm{m}^{-1}\) at the base.
The \(\texttt{N=10}\) Legendre variant, by contrast, keeps the global shear
stable but over-localizes local curvature/stress near the base-side station.

The methodological consequence is important.  The structural family is useful
as a global reference envelope for \(V_b\)-drift and section-level hysteretic
capacity, but the \(\texttt{N=4}\) Legendre slice should not be used as the
sole local target for continuum crack patterns, local strain fields, or
plastic-hinge length.  For local equivalence, the comparison must either use a
structural family envelope or promote an endpoint-resolved family such as
\(\texttt{N=10}\) Lobatto as the structural local reference.

\subsection{Monotonic structural-vs-continuum kinematic equivalence audit}

With the continuum constitutive baseline clarified, the next question was more
elementary and more consequential than another blind solver sweep: were we even
comparing the continuum against the right structural kinematics?  The
canonical artifact is now
\texttt{reboot\_continuum\_monotonic\_equivalence\_audit\_v2/continuum\_monotonic\_equivalence\_summary.json}.

The audit is intentionally monotonic and precompressed.  It introduces two
structural controls for the same reduced-column beam slice:
\begin{itemize}
  \item a \emph{free-rotation} control, which is the usual structural
    reduced-column reference with imposed lateral drift and free tip bending
    rotation; and
  \item a \emph{clamped-rotation} control, which constrains the tip bending
    rotation. This is a deliberately stronger guided-cap control, appropriate
    only when the continuum top face is also prevented from developing the
    axial displacement gradient associated with section rotation.
\end{itemize}

The outcome is subtle in the elastic regime and strong in the nonlinear one.
On the elastic monotonic bridge at \(0.5\,\mathrm{mm}\), the continuum still
matches the free-rotation beam slightly better than the clamped one.  For the
embedded \texttt{Hex20 2x2x2} slice, the RMS path error is only
\(\sim 9.87\times10^{-3}\) against the free beam and
\(\sim 1.20\times10^{-2}\) against the clamped one.  The refined elastic
\texttt{Hex20 2x2x10} and the base-biased \texttt{bias=3} variant show the
same pattern, with RMS errors of order \(8.6\text{--}8.7\times10^{-3}\)
against the free beam and \(1.23\text{--}1.24\times10^{-2}\) against the
clamped control.

In the nonlinear monotonic pushover at \(20\,\mathrm{mm}\), however, the
picture flips.  The continuum is much closer to the clamped structural control
than to the free-rotation beam.  For the embedded \texttt{Hex20 2x2x2} slice,
the RMS base-shear path error drops from \(\sim 9.97\times10^{-1}\) against
the free beam to only \(\sim 1.79\times10^{-1}\) against the clamped control.
For the host-only \texttt{Hex20 2x2x2} column, the same comparison improves
from \(\sim 7.18\times10^{-1}\) to \(\sim 2.52\times10^{-1}\).

This result is now read more cautiously after the elastic axis/BC control
below.  Prescribing the same lateral displacement \(u_x\) on the full
continuum top face does \emph{not} by itself impose a guided beam cap: the top
face axial displacement \(u_z\) is still free, so the solid can realize the
axial gradient associated with section rotation.  The clamped beam therefore
remains a useful stronger control, but it is not the default structural
comparator for the current lateral-only continuum boundary condition.  It
would become the right comparator only after an explicit rigid-cap/MPC policy
ties the top-face axial displacement field to a single axial generalized
motion while suppressing bending rotation.

The same pass also clarifies the present role of longitudinal refinement.  In
the elastic bridge, moving from \texttt{Hex20 2x2x2} to \texttt{Hex20 2x2x10}
still improves the embedded-gap metric but barely moves the global force path.
In the nonlinear monotonic pilot, the \texttt{Hex20 2x2x10} cases are still
outside the present runtime budget on this machine, so they cannot yet be
promoted as a physically superior continuum baseline.  The current evidence
therefore supports a narrower but much cleaner claim:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item the first promoted continuum reference remains \texttt{Hex20 2x2x2};
  \item the free-rotation beam slice is the default comparator for the current
    lateral-only top-face continuum boundary condition, while the clamped beam
    slice is retained as the explicit guided-cap control; and
  \item longitudinal refinement beyond \texttt{NZ=2} remains an open
    operational frontier rather than a validated physical improvement.
\end{enumerate}

The follow-up elastic control is intentionally blunt.  It uses a
\(5\,\mathrm{mm}\) monotonic elasticized displacement with the same axial
preload, the same eight-bar section and a \texttt{Hex8} \(6\times6\times12\)
continuum mesh.  The reproducible driver is
\texttt{scripts/run\_reduced\_rc\_elastic\_axis\_stiffness\_control.py}.  The
terminal secant stiffnesses are
\[
\begin{array}{l|r|r}
\text{case} & |V(5\,\mathrm{mm})|[\mathrm{kN}] &
K_{\mathrm{sec}}[\mathrm{kN/mm}]\\
\hline
\text{structural free top rotation} & 5.420 & 1.084\\
\text{structural clamped top rotation} & 21.348 & 4.270\\
\text{continuum lateral top face} & 6.595 & 1.319\\
\text{continuum uniform axial top cap} & 32.719 & 6.544
\end{array}
\]
Thus the continuum is only \(1.217\) times the free-rotation beam stiffness,
but only \(0.309\) times the clamped/guided stiffness; the clamped structural
control is \(3.94\) times the free one.  The new
\texttt{uniform\_axial\_penalty\_cap} audit branch deliberately ties all
top-face axial displacements to one free axial generalized motion; it is
therefore a useful guardrail for future rigid-cap/MPC work, but it is not yet
the promoted comparator because its secant stiffness is \(1.53\) times the
structural clamped control on this mesh.  This diagnostic removes a major
false lead: the large structural--continuum softness seen against the clamped
beam was primarily a boundary-kinematics mismatch, not a material or
embedded-bar failure.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.78\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_elastic_axis_stiffness_control.png}
  \caption{Elastic axis/BC stiffness control.  The current continuum benchmark
  prescribes the lateral top-face translation but leaves top-face axial
  displacements free; its elastic stiffness is therefore close to the
  free-rotation structural beam.  The explicit uniform-axial top-cap branch is
  retained as a non-promoted guided-cap audit because it over-stiffens this
  elastic control relative to the structural clamped beam.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-elastic-axis-stiffness-control}
\end{figure}

The same correction was then propagated to the large cyclic bridge without
rerunning the expensive continuum: the \texttt{Hex8} \(6\times6\times12\),
corotational, tensile crack-band proxy continuum was compared against a fresh
\texttt{TimoshenkoBeamN<10>} reference with free top rotation and the
\texttt{fine} section-fiber profile.  On the
\(\pm 50,\pm100,\pm150,\pm200\,\mathrm{mm}\) protocol, the peak-normalized
RMS base-shear error dropped to \(1.586\times10^{-1}\), compared with about
\(3.64\times10^{-1}\) when the clamped beam was used.  The orientation-matched
steel loop work also moved to a physically comparable range:
\[
  W_s^{\mathrm{struct}}=113.3~\mathrm{MPa},\qquad
  W_s^{\mathrm{cont}}=135.8~\mathrm{MPa},\qquad
  W_s^{\mathrm{cont}}/W_s^{\mathrm{struct}}=1.199 .
\]
The embedded transfer gap stayed closed
\((\max|\Delta\varepsilon_{\mathrm{host-bar}}|=2.4\times10^{-9})\).  This is
the current promoted interpretation of the \(200\,\mathrm{mm}\) cyclic
continuum bridge: the bar coupling and steel model are behaving coherently,
while the remaining global mismatch is now a continuum host/localization
question rather than an obvious boundary-condition error.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_structural_continuum_hysteresis_overlay_corotational_tensile_crack_band_damage_proxy_et0p1_6x6x12_bias2_200mm.png}
  \end{minipage}\hfill
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_structural_continuum_steel_hysteresis_overlay_corotational_tensile_crack_band_damage_proxy_et0p1_6x6x12_bias2_200mm.png}
  \end{minipage}
  \caption{Large-amplitude cyclic bridge after the top-rotation correction.
  Left: base-shear hysteresis for the free-rotation structural reference and
  the \texttt{Hex8} \(6\times6\times12\) continuum. Right: local hysteresis of
  the orientation-matched structural steel fiber and embedded truss point.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-free-rotation-cyclic-200mm}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.84\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_continuum_monotonic_equivalence_overlay.png}
  \caption{Monotonic structural-vs-continuum equivalence audit.  In the
  elastic regime the continuum stays slightly closer to the free-rotation beam,
  but in the nonlinear regime it becomes markedly closer to the clamped
  structural control.  This exposes boundary-condition equivalence as a real
  part of the continuum gap.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-continuum-monotonic-equivalence-overlay}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_continuum_monotonic_equivalence_error.png}
  \end{minipage}\hfill
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_continuum_monotonic_equivalence_timing.png}
  \end{minipage}
  \caption{Error and timing surfaces of the same monotonic equivalence audit.
  The nonlinear \texttt{Hex20 2x2x2} continuum is much closer to the clamped
  beam control than to the free-rotation beam, while the refined nonlinear
  \texttt{NZ=10} cases remain outside the current runtime budget and therefore
  cannot yet be promoted as validated physical improvements.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-continuum-monotonic-equivalence-error-timing}
\end{figure}

\paragraph{Ko--Bathe pertinence and the promoted local continuum baseline.}
The monotonic beam-vs-continuum equivalence audit still left one important
question open: was Ko--Bathe 3D itself the wrong local concrete model, or was
the benchmark simply penalizing it through an over-expensive baseline?  The
new audit
\texttt{scripts/run\_reduced\_rc\_continuum\_local\_model\_baseline\_audit.py}
closes that question much more cleanly.

The main correction is methodological rather than cosmetic.  The old continuum
baseline combined three heavy or hidden choices at once:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item a numerically differentiated tangent path
    (\texttt{adaptive-central-difference-with-secant-fallback});
  \item a fixed crack-band length \(l_b = 100\,\mathrm{mm}\) regardless of the
    host mesh; and
  \item the pre-fix quadratic longitudinal bias geometry, which had been
    placing mid-side levels incorrectly when the host was clustered toward the
    fixed end.
\end{enumerate}
None of those choices should remain implicit in a promoted local-model
baseline.  The continuum driver now exposes them explicitly and the promoted
slice uses:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item \texttt{production-stabilized} Ko--Bathe 3D;
  \item the \texttt{fracture-secant} material tangent;
  \item explicit crack-band semantics tied to the host mesh; and
  \item the corrected straight-sided quadratic longitudinal bias geometry.
\end{enumerate}

The resulting read is strong enough to guide promotion:
\begin{itemize}
  \item on the nonlinear monotonic \texttt{Hex20 2x2x2} embedded slice at
    \(20\,\mathrm{mm}\), the promoted baseline preserves essentially the same
    global force path as the old legacy benchmark while cutting solve time from
    about \(58.22\,\mathrm{s}\) to about \(24.50\,\mathrm{s}\);
  \item the old cost issue is therefore \emph{not} evidence that Ko--Bathe 3D
    should already be replaced.  It is evidence that the old baseline was
    mis-specified;
  \item adding a strong fixed-end bias \((\texttt{bias}=3)\) to the same
    \texttt{Hex20 2x2x2} host improves the clamped-beam monotonic bridge
    modestly, from \(\mathrm{RMS}\approx 3.221\times10^{-1}\) to
    \(\mathrm{RMS}\approx 3.177\times10^{-1}\), without changing the order of
    cost;
  \item on that biased host, the global force path is essentially unchanged
    when the crack-band length is evaluated from the mean host edge
    \((l_b \approx 1600\,\mathrm{mm})\) or from the fixed-end host edge
    \((l_b \approx 400\,\mathrm{mm})\), even though the constitutive effect of
    \(l_b\) is still verified independently at the material level;
  \item the host-only \texttt{Hex20 2x2x2} biased slice remains clearly worse
    than the embedded-bar slice, so the promoted local RC model should keep
    embedded longitudinal reinforcement active.
\end{itemize}

That last point matters.  The best representative local-validation slice for
this iteration is no longer the cheapest uniform host, but the still-manageable
\texttt{Hex20 2x2x2} host with fixed-end bias and explicit fixed-end
characteristic length.  The uniform \texttt{Hex20 2x2x2} slice remains useful
as the cheapest nonlinear control, but the biased slice is now the more
informative local RC baseline.

The refined-host frontier is also much clearer after the same audit:
\begin{itemize}
  \item \texttt{Hex20 2x2x10} elasticized closes comfortably in about
    \(9.88\) to \(12.17\,\mathrm{s}\), so the refined host topology itself is
    not intrinsically prohibitive;
  \item the nonlinear \texttt{Hex20 2x2x6} and \texttt{Hex20 2x2x10} slices,
    both with fixed-end bias and fixed-end crack-band length, still time out
    under the current \(300\,\mathrm{s}\) budget at only
    \(10\,\mathrm{mm}\) monotonic amplitude;
  \item the present barrier is therefore not ``continuous geometry is too
    expensive'' and not ``embedded trusses are fundamentally broken''; it is a
    nonlinear local-material frontier of the refined continuum slice.
\end{itemize}

That interpretation is now also protected by regression.  The constitutive test
\texttt{tests/test\_ko\_bathe\_concrete\_3d.cpp} explicitly verifies that a
shorter crack-band length retains more tensile stress on the 3D post-peak
branch, and \texttt{tests/test\_reduced\_rc\_column\_continuum\_baseline.cpp}
checks that the promoted continuum baseline actually reports the fracture-secant
tangent together with the mesh-evaluated longitudinal characteristic length.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.84\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_continuum_local_model_baseline_overlay.png}
  \caption{Local-model baseline audit for the reduced RC continuum column.
  The legacy Ko--Bathe benchmark, the promoted uniform \texttt{Hex20 2x2x2}
  slice, and the base-biased variants are compared against the clamped
  structural monotonic control.  The biased \texttt{Hex20 2x2x2} slice
  improves the bridge modestly without changing the order of cost.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-continuum-local-baseline-overlay}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_continuum_local_model_baseline_error_timing.png}
  \end{minipage}\hfill
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_continuum_local_model_baseline_frontier.png}
  \end{minipage}
  \caption{Left: error-vs-cost view of the promoted local continuum baseline.
  Right: operational frontier of the refined host.  The refined
  \texttt{Hex20} topology is cheap in the elasticized slice but remains a
  nonlinear frontier at \texttt{NZ}\(\ge 6\), so it is not yet promotable as
  the baseline local model for this stage.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-continuum-local-baseline-error-timing}
\end{figure}

The promoted local baseline should not remain a single-point statement, so the
same chain was then pushed through a monotonic amplitude sweep frozen in
\texttt{reboot\_continuum\_monotonic\_amplitude\_audit\_v2\_promoted/continuum\_monotonic\_amplitude\_summary.json}.
That second artifact is important because it confirms that the promoted
\texttt{Hex20 2x2x2} embedded slice with strong fixed-end bias and fixed-end
crack-band semantics is not merely a better-looking \(20\,\mathrm{mm}\)
snapshot.  Against the clamped beam comparator, it improves the RMS base-shear
path error consistently across the whole \(2.5\text{--}20\,\mathrm{mm}\)
window:
\[
\begin{aligned}
2.5\,\mathrm{mm}:&\quad 3.3897\times10^{-1}\rightarrow 3.3635\times10^{-1},\\
5.0\,\mathrm{mm}:&\quad 2.5472\times10^{-1}\rightarrow 2.5167\times10^{-1},\\
10.0\,\mathrm{mm}:&\quad 2.0323\times10^{-1}\rightarrow 2.0011\times10^{-1},\\
15.0\,\mathrm{mm}:&\quad 1.8673\times10^{-1}\rightarrow 1.8378\times10^{-1},\\
20.0\,\mathrm{mm}:&\quad 1.7915\times10^{-1}\rightarrow 1.7647\times10^{-1}.
\end{aligned}
\]
Just as importantly, that improvement does not come through a hidden cost
explosion: the promoted slice remains in the same cost class as the uniform
control and is often slightly cheaper, while the host-only biased slice
remains faster but physically worse.  This upgrades the conclusion from
``plausible promoted slice'' to ``promoted local RC baseline with amplitude
support''.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_continuum_monotonic_amplitude_base_shear.png}
  \end{minipage}\hfill
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_continuum_monotonic_amplitude_error.png}
  \end{minipage}
  \caption{Monotonic amplitude sweep of the promoted local continuum baseline.
  Left: peak base-shear evolution against the clamped beam control. Right:
  RMS path error over the same amplitude window.  The promoted embedded slice
  stays consistently better than the uniform control without changing the cost
  class.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-continuum-monotonic-amplitude}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.62\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_continuum_monotonic_amplitude_timing.png}
  \caption{Timing view of the same monotonic amplitude sweep. The promoted
  fixed-end-biased embedded slice remains in the same operational cost class as
  the uniform control, which is why it is now the recommended local continuum
  baseline for the next validation stage.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-continuum-monotonic-amplitude-timing}
\end{figure}

That promoted monotonic slice still left one uncomfortable gap in the argument:
the preload semantics of the embedded reinforcement chain had never been
audited as a first-class object.  The next pass therefore reopened the problem
in the narrowest falsifiable form, frozen in
\texttt{reboot\_embedded\_preload\_transfer\_audit\_v2\_divtol/embedded\_preload\_transfer\_summary.json}.
Two questions were separated deliberately:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item how the axial preload enters the composite section; and
  \item whether the open \texttt{Truss<3>} frontier on the continuum pilot is
    truly mechanical or only a PETSc divergence-test artifact.
\end{enumerate}

The first answer is useful and honest.  On the promoted
\texttt{Hex20 2x2x2 + Truss<2>} preload-only slice, splitting the axial preload
explicitly between the host face and the top rebar end improves the axial
end-gap, but only modestly:
\[
\begin{aligned}
\text{top bar-face axial gap}:&\quad 3.46\times10^{-5}\,\mathrm{m}
\rightarrow 2.93\times10^{-5}\,\mathrm{m},\\
\text{embedding-gap norm}:&\quad 3.47\times10^{-5}\,\mathrm{m}
\rightarrow 2.93\times10^{-5}\,\mathrm{m}.
\end{aligned}
\]
The host-only preload path was therefore not fully faithful, but the improved
path does not magically close the interface either.

The second answer is even more important because it sharpens the architectural
reading dramatically:
\begin{itemize}
  \item \texttt{Hex20 + Truss<3> + preload} still fails before the first
    accepted step;
  \item the same \texttt{Truss<3>} path succeeds on \texttt{Hex27 1x1x1}; and
  \item the same \texttt{Truss<3>} path also succeeds on \texttt{Hex20} when
    the preload is zero.
\end{itemize}
The accurate statement is therefore no longer ``quadratic embedded rebar is
unstable'', but the much narrower and more useful one:
\[
\texttt{Hex20 + embedded quadratic rebar + axial preload}
\]
is the open slice.

The PETSc side was also reopened explicitly instead of assuming that
\texttt{SNES\_DIVERGED\_DTOL} already settled the matter.  The continuum
benchmark now exposes \texttt{--snes-divergence-tolerance} as a typed benchmark
surface, and the same minimal \texttt{Hex20 1x1x1 + Truss<3>} preload case was
rerun with \texttt{divtol = unlimited}.  That did not rescue the case.  It
converted the early \texttt{DTOL} failure into a long stagnation that exhausted
the \(300\,\mathrm{s}\) audit budget.  This is precisely the kind of result
the reboot needed: the typed solver seam was worth adding, but the frontier is
still not honestly explained away as a PETSc false positive.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.72\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_embedded_preload_transfer_frontier.png}
  \caption{Embedded preload-transfer frontier.  Left-to-right, the audit shows
  that explicit preload splitting modestly improves the promoted
  \texttt{Hex20 + Truss<2>} slice, but does not rescue the
  \texttt{Hex20 + Truss<3>} preload chain.  Disabling PETSc's divergence test
  does not recover that frontier either; it only turns early divergence into a
  long stalled solve.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-embedded-preload-transfer-frontier}
\end{figure}

The promoted continuum read is therefore much cleaner:
\begin{itemize}
  \item \texttt{Hex20} \(\rightarrow\) \texttt{Truss<2>} remains the promoted
      efficient local RC baseline;
  \item \texttt{Hex27} \(\rightarrow\) \texttt{Truss<3>} remains a viable
      higher-fidelity host/bar route; and
  \item \texttt{Hex20} \(\rightarrow\) \texttt{Truss<3>} should not be promoted
      until the preload frontier is closed for real, not hidden behind solver
      settings.
\end{itemize}

That frontier also forced a more basic coupling audit. The current
\texttt{PenaltyCoupling} implementation does anchor interior quadratic rebar
nodes to the host continuum; the new regression in
\texttt{tests/test\_reduced\_rc\_column\_continuum\_baseline.cpp} freezes the
distinction directly on a single \texttt{Hex20} host. A two-node embedded truss
leaves no interior bar DOF to couple, whereas a three-node embedded truss does
couple its midpoint. So the open
\(\texttt{Hex20 + Truss<3> + preload}\) slice should not be interpreted as a
fictitious slip caused by forgotten interior bar nodes. The stronger closure
check is already frozen in
\texttt{reboot\_structural\_continuum\_steel\_hysteresis\_audit\_hex20\_2x2x2\_bias3\_fixedend\_20mm\_hostbar\_v3/structural\_continuum\_steel\_hysteresis\_summary.json},
where the promoted embedded path closes to essentially machine precision:
\[
\begin{aligned}
\max |\Delta \varepsilon_{\text{host-bar}}| &\approx 2.22\times10^{-10},\\
\mathrm{rms}(|\Delta \varepsilon_{\text{host-bar}}|) &\approx 8.52\times10^{-11},\\
\max \|\Delta u_{\text{projected}}\| &\approx 1.88\times10^{-11}\,\mathrm{m}.
\end{aligned}
\]
So the quadratic bar midpoint is not merely present in the mesh topology; it is
kinematically locked to the host on the promoted path.

The same pass also reopened the predictor question instead of assuming that a
secant seed must help near fracture. The canonical artifact is now
\texttt{scripts/run\_reduced\_rc\_continuum\_predictor\_audit.py}, frozen in
\texttt{data/output/cyclic\_validation/reboot\_continuum\_predictor\_audit\_v4\_full/continuum\_predictor\_audit\_summary.json}.
The audit stays on the promoted local slice
\(\texttt{Hex20 2x2x2 + embedded bars + bias}=3 + l_b^{\mathrm{fixed-end}}\)
with \texttt{newton-l2-only}, and sweeps monotonic amplitudes
\((2.5,5,10,20)\,\mathrm{mm}\) across five seed policies:
\texttt{current-state-only}, \texttt{secant}, \texttt{adaptive-secant},
\texttt{linearized-equilibrium}, and
\texttt{hybrid-secant-linearized}. The updated read is now materially stronger:
\begin{itemize}
  \item all five policies complete the full promoted window;
  \item none of them moves the first-crack drift or the
    macroscopic base-shear response on the promoted slice.
  \item \texttt{secant} remains the fastest path only on the easiest
    pre-crack case (\(2.5\,\mathrm{mm}\), \(18.94\,\mathrm{s}\));
  \item \texttt{hybrid-secant-linearized} becomes the fastest policy at
    \(5,10,20\,\mathrm{mm}\)
    (\(19.71\,\mathrm{s}\), \(26.87\,\mathrm{s}\), \(44.60\,\mathrm{s}\));
  \item averaged over the promoted window,
    \texttt{hybrid-secant-linearized} is now the cheapest policy
    (\(28.71\,\mathrm{s}\)) ahead of \texttt{secant}
    (\(29.21\,\mathrm{s}\)) and \texttt{current-state-only}
    (\(29.45\,\mathrm{s}\)); and
  \item \texttt{linearized-equilibrium} is still slightly heavier on the
    promoted slice (\(30.65\,\mathrm{s}\) mean wall time), so it is not the
    promoted general default by itself.
\end{itemize}

This is no longer only a negative result. The new predictor surface in
\texttt{src/analysis/NLAnalysis.hh} is now a genuine extension seam and it also
reopens the pathological preload frontier. The direct probes on
\(\texttt{Hex20 + Truss<3> + preload}\) now separate clearly:
\begin{itemize}
  \item \texttt{current-state-only} still fails before the first accepted
    preload step;
  \item \texttt{linearized-equilibrium} completes the same minimal frontier
    slice; and
  \item \texttt{secant-with-linearized-fallback} also completes it.
\end{itemize}
That is why the continuum benchmark default is no longer
\texttt{current-state-only}; it was promoted to the hybrid typed predictor
\texttt{secant\_with\_linearized\_fallback}. Architecturally, that is the right
compromise: it preserves the cheap secant path once converged history exists,
but it no longer leaves the first preload step defenseless on the open
quadratic-embedded frontier.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_continuum_predictor_timing.png}
  \end{minipage}\hfill
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_continuum_predictor_fracture_onset.png}
  \end{minipage}
  \caption{Predictor audit on the promoted local continuum slice. Left: wall-time
  read across amplitudes. Right: first-crack drift. The new predictor seam is
  real and reusable; the promoted hybrid predictor now stays cheap on the
  regular \texttt{Hex20 2x2x2} window and still provides a first-step rescue
  path on the open \texttt{Hex20 + Truss<3> + preload} frontier.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-continuum-predictor-audit}
\end{figure}

The same line of work then stepped back from predictors and asked a more basic
question: whether the promoted local continuum was still being regularized by a
physically admissible crack-band length once the host mesh was strongly biased
toward the fixed end. The canonical artifact is now
\texttt{scripts/run\_reduced\_rc\_continuum\_cover\_core\_foundation\_audit.py},
frozen in
\texttt{data/output/cyclic\_validation/reboot\_continuum\_cover\_core\_audit/continuum\_cover\_core\_foundation\_summary.json}.
This audit did not only compare meshes. It reopened the whole host-side
foundation of the local RC column:
\begin{itemize}
  \item the host concrete can now be zoned explicitly as
    \texttt{cover\_core\_split};
  \item the transverse mesh can be made \texttt{cover\_aligned}, so cover and
    confined core correspond to actual host elements; and
  \item the reinforced continuum can now carry an explicit
    \texttt{boundary\_matched\_eight\_bar} layout, instead of forcing every bar
    path into the interior host cells.
\end{itemize}

The first two monotonic \texttt{Hex20 4x4x2} results are useful because they
show what is \emph{not} the main source of the remaining discrepancy:
\begin{itemize}
  \item without steel, moving from the old uniform host to
    \texttt{cover\_core\_split + cover\_aligned} changes peak base shear only
    from about \(17.06\,\mathrm{kN}\) to \(17.44\,\mathrm{kN}\); and
  \item therefore explicit cover/core zoning is worth keeping for physical
    honesty, but it is not by itself the dominant driver of the global force
    gap.
\end{itemize}

The rebar-layout read is equally important. On the same \texttt{Hex20 4x4x2}
host, both the structural interior-bar path and the new boundary-bar path are
kinematically admissible, but they are not equivalent:
\begin{itemize}
  \item \texttt{embedded interior} closes with
    \(|V_{\mathrm{base}}|_{\max} \approx 21.07\,\mathrm{kN}\);
  \item \texttt{embedded boundary} closes with
    \(|V_{\mathrm{base}}|_{\max} \approx 23.59\,\mathrm{kN}\); and
  \item the boundary-bar path is also cheaper on that slice
    (\(\sim 96.7\,\mathrm{s}\) versus \(\sim 150.8\,\mathrm{s}\)).
\end{itemize}
So boundary bars cannot be silently mixed into the promoted local model as if
they were merely a cheaper rendering of the same physics. They must remain an
explicit branch of the audit.

An explicit \texttt{cover\_core\_interface\_eight\_bar} probe then closed an
important ambiguity. For the current canonical reduced-column geometry, that
probe collapses exactly to the promoted
\texttt{structural\_matched\_eight\_bar} branch, because the eight canonical
steel paths already lie on the cover/core interfaces. The interface-bar idea
is therefore not wrong; it is simply not a distinct physical family for the
current geometry, so it should be recorded as a semantic equivalence rather
than promoted as an extra baseline branch.

The strongest result of the pass came from the biased host. On the
\texttt{Hex20 4x4x4} cover/core slice with \(\texttt{bias}=3\):
\begin{itemize}
  \item the old
    \texttt{mean\_longitudinal\_host\_edge\_mm} crack-band length yields an
    absurd crack opening of about \(1.083\,\mathrm{m}\), even though the global
    base-shear trace remains at only \(\sim 8.84\,\mathrm{kN}\); whereas
  \item the corrected
    \texttt{fixed\_end\_longitudinal\_host\_edge\_mm} policy restores a
    physically plausible crack opening of about \(3.87\times10^{-4}\,\mathrm{m}\)
    with essentially the same global force path.
\end{itemize}

That is a correction of physical fidelity, not a cosmetic numerical tweak. The
problem was not simply that Ko--Bathe is ``too heavy'', nor that the continuum
had become too nonlinear in the abstract. The problematic part of the chain was
the combination of:
\begin{itemize}
  \item a strongly biased longitudinal mesh, and
  \item a characteristic-length policy that stopped being admissible on that
    mesh.
\end{itemize}
The correct promotion is therefore more precise:
\begin{itemize}
  \item Ko--Bathe remains on the current local RC continuum path;
  \item strong fixed-end bias is promoted only together with the fixed-end
    host-edge crack length; and
  \item \texttt{Hex20 4x4x10} remains an operational frontier, not a promoted
    local baseline yet.
\end{itemize}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_continuum_cover_core_monotonic_overlay.png}
  \end{minipage}\hfill
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_continuum_characteristic_length_sensitivity.png}
  \end{minipage}
  \caption{Continuum cover/core foundation audit. Left: monotonic overlay for
  the new \texttt{Hex20 4x4x2} host variants. Right: characteristic-length
  sensitivity on the biased \texttt{Hex20 4x4x4} host. The global force trace
  barely moves, but the wrong crack-band length produces an unphysical crack
  opening, which is why the fixed-end host-edge length is now the promoted
  policy for strongly biased local meshes.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-continuum-cover-core-foundation}
\end{figure}

The same question was then rerun in a narrower mesh-foundation audit, frozen in
\texttt{reboot\_continuum\_monotonic\_mesh\_foundation\_audit/continuum\_monotonic\_mesh\_foundation\_summary.json}.
This pass kept the same nonlinear concrete profile, the same embedded interior
bars, the same axial preload and the same monotonic push-over to
\(20\,\mathrm{mm}\), while comparing:
\begin{itemize}
  \item \texttt{Hex20 4x4x2 uniform} as the current promoted baseline;
  \item \texttt{Hex20 6x6x2 uniform} as a pure transverse-refinement control;
    and
  \item \texttt{Hex20 4x4x4 bias=3 fixed-end} as a longitudinal-refinement
    control.
\end{itemize}

The read removes another tempting explanation. Pure transverse refinement
barely moves the physics:
\[
\begin{aligned}
\texttt{Hex20 4x4x2}:&\quad |V_{\mathrm{base}}|_{\max}\approx 21.069\,\mathrm{kN},
\quad \varepsilon_V^{\mathrm{rms}}\approx 3.13\times10^{-1},\\
\texttt{Hex20 6x6x2}:&\quad |V_{\mathrm{base}}|_{\max}\approx 21.059\,\mathrm{kN},
\quad \varepsilon_V^{\mathrm{rms}}\approx 3.14\times10^{-1}.
\end{aligned}
\]
So, on this monotonic \(20\,\mathrm{mm}\) slice, the promoted continuum
baseline is not obviously too coarse in the section. The longitudinally
refined branch is physically more consequential but also much more expensive:
\[
\texttt{Hex20 4x4x4 bias=3 fixed-end}:\quad
|V_{\mathrm{base}}|_{\max}\approx 20.637\,\mathrm{kN},\;
\varepsilon_V^{\mathrm{rms}}\approx 3.25\times10^{-1},
\]
with \(642\) cracked Gauss points, \(w_{\max}\approx 0.761\,\mathrm{mm}\), and
\(\sigma_{s,\max}\approx 115.2\,\mathrm{MPa}\). That is still not enough to
promote it as the new default local model, but it is enough to say something
cleaner: if a stronger physical shift is still required, it is more likely to
come from the longitudinal direction than from simply packing more elements
across the section.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_continuum_monotonic_mesh_foundation_overlay.png}
  \end{minipage}\hfill
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_continuum_monotonic_mesh_foundation_overview.png}
  \end{minipage}
  \caption{Monotonic continuum mesh-foundation audit. Left: base-shear overlays
  against the clamped structural control. Right: combined read of force,
  cracked Gauss points, crack opening, steel stress and wall time. Pure
  transverse refinement contributes little, whereas longitudinal refinement is
  physically more consequential but still too costly to promote by default.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-continuum-monotonic-mesh-foundation}
\end{figure}

With that monotonic foundation in place, the next question was whether the same
promotion survives the first short cyclic window. The canonical artifact is now
\texttt{scripts/run\_reduced\_rc\_continuum\_cover\_core\_cyclic\_audit.py},
frozen in
\texttt{data/output/cyclic\_validation/reboot\_continuum\_cover\_core\_cyclic\_audit/continuum\_cover\_core\_cyclic\_summary.json}.
The cyclic window keeps the same axial preload, the same \texttt{Hex20} host
family, and the same cover/core-aware section, while comparing:
\begin{itemize}
  \item \texttt{continuum-only};
  \item \texttt{embedded interior};
  \item \texttt{embedded boundary}; and
  \item one first higher-cost
    \(\texttt{Hex20 4x4x4 + bias}=3 + l_b^{\mathrm{fixed-end}}\) branch.
\end{itemize}

The first short cyclic read is already quite sharp:
\begin{itemize}
  \item the three \texttt{Hex20 4x4x2} branches all close the
    \(10\,\mathrm{mm}\) cyclic window;
  \item \texttt{continuum-only} reaches
    \(|V_{\mathrm{base}}|_{\max} \approx 8.91\,\mathrm{kN}\) with
    \(264\) cracked Gauss points;
  \item \texttt{embedded interior} reaches
    \(|V_{\mathrm{base}}|_{\max} \approx 10.71\,\mathrm{kN}\) with peak steel
    stress \(\approx 44.8\,\mathrm{MPa}\);
  \item \texttt{embedded boundary} reaches
    \(|V_{\mathrm{base}}|_{\max} \approx 11.97\,\mathrm{kN}\) with peak steel
    stress \(\approx 57.3\,\mathrm{MPa}\); and
  \item the strongly biased
    \(\texttt{Hex20 4x4x4 + bias}=3 + l_b^{\mathrm{fixed-end}}\) interior-bar
    branch still does not close within the present \(4800\,\mathrm{s}\) budget.
\end{itemize}

Two conclusions follow immediately. First, the monotonic read survives
reversal: boundary bars remain a valid comparison branch, but they are still
physically different from the interior embedded chain and cannot be promoted as
the same local model. Second, the corrected fixed-end characteristic-length
policy is necessary but not sufficient to make the higher-cost biased branch a
runtime-ready cyclic baseline.

The next closure pass then asked a narrower question than ``should Ko--Bathe be
replaced?'': whether the promoted local slice actually benefits from keeping
the same constitutive family while changing only the crack-stabilization
profile. The canonical artifact is now
\texttt{scripts/run\_reduced\_rc\_continuum\_cover\_core\_profile\_probe.py},
frozen in
\texttt{data/output/cyclic\_validation/reboot\_continuum\_cover\_core\_profile\_probe/continuum\_cover\_core\_profile\_probe\_summary.json}.
On the promoted \texttt{Hex20 4x4x2} slice with interior embedded bars, the
\texttt{benchmark\_reference} and \texttt{production\_stabilized} profiles:
\begin{itemize}
  \item produce the same peak base shear in both monotonic and short cyclic
    windows;
  \item preserve essentially the same peak crack opening and peak steel stress;
  \item materially reduce the counted active cracked Gauss points under the
    stabilized profile (\(282 \rightarrow 258\) monotonic,
    \(432 \rightarrow 252\) cyclic); and
  \item remain at least competitive in wall time
    (\(152.7\,\mathrm{s}\) versus \(163.4\,\mathrm{s}\) monotonic, and nearly
    tied in the short cyclic window).
\end{itemize}

That read keeps the constitutive conclusion both narrow and defensible. The
present evidence does \emph{not} support replacing the Ko--Bathe route
wholesale merely because the host problem is expensive. What it \emph{does}
support is promoting the stabilized profile as the efficient local baseline,
because it regularizes internal crack activity without distorting the macro
response on the promoted slice.

This is also the point where the Spiliopoulos--Lykidis solid-RC methodology is
actually useful to adapt. Three ideas transfer well to the present chain:
\begin{itemize}
  \item embedded \texttt{Truss<3>} reinforcement as a first-class continuum
    path;
  \item crack loading/unloading state updates inside the nonlinear iteration,
    which is already close to the current closure/reopening treatment; and
  \item a dynamic viewpoint in which time integration becomes a sequence of
    nonlinear equilibrium solves, which fits naturally with the typed PETSc
    solve-policy seam already opened in \texttt{fall\_n}.
\end{itemize}
What the paper does not justify here is a blind constitutive swap. On the
promoted local slice, the stronger conclusion is to keep the family, keep the
stabilized profile, and continue removing cost from the host/bar chain and the
characteristic-length policy before attempting a material replacement.

Before reopening the next large cyclic window, one more stabilization break was
necessary because two lower-level issues were still contaminating the read of
the continuum benchmark.

The first one was the support resultant itself. The continuum benchmark exports
\(V_{\mathrm{base}}\) and the axial support reaction as a discrete nodal
reaction sum. That is a perfectly admissible quantity for higher-order
elements, even when midside consistent nodal forces change sign locally, but
only if the support set is the \emph{full constrained support}. The embedded
branches had quietly omitted the constrained rebar-endcap nodes from that
support set. The correction is now frozen in
\texttt{src/validation/ReducedRCColumnContinuumBaseline.cpp}, and the
regression in
\texttt{tests/test\_reduced\_rc\_column\_continuum\_baseline.cpp} now checks
two things explicitly:
\begin{itemize}
  \item \texttt{Hex20} and \texttt{Hex27} recover the applied top-face traction
    magnitude through the base reaction, despite the higher-order consistent
    load pattern; and
  \item the embedded branch now includes the rebar-endcap support reaction, so
    split host/steel preload no longer undercounts the support force.
\end{itemize}
This means that any embedded-continuum bundle generated before that correction
must now be read as historical, not as a quantitative base-shear reference.

The following pass exposed a deeper PETSc indexing issue in the embedded
penalty transfer.  The old Jacobian assembly used the shorthand
\(\mathrm{offset}+d\) for component \(d\) after asking PETSc for a global point
offset.  That is only safe for fully unconstrained points.  Once a boundary
node has partial Dirichlet data, PETSc compresses the active global section and
the component map is no longer affine in that way.  The penalty coupling now
maps every local rebar and host component through the DM local-to-global map
and lets PETSc mark constrained components explicitly.  This is the reason the
rebar end nodes can remain coupled without introducing artificial axial slip at
the top boundary.

The immediate corrected cyclic evidence is:
\begin{itemize}
  \item \(\texttt{Hex8 2x2x4}\), corotational,
    \(200\,\mathrm{mm}\): \(|V|_{\max}\approx157.65\,\mathrm{kN}\),
    \(\|\Delta u_{\mathrm{embed}}\|_{\max}\approx2.5\times10^{-10}\,\mathrm{m}\),
    \(|\sigma_s|_{\max}\approx434.5\,\mathrm{MPa}\), solve time
    \(\approx12.2\,\mathrm{s}\);
  \item \(\texttt{Hex8 4x4x8}\), corotational,
    \(200\,\mathrm{mm}\): \(|V|_{\max}\approx60.39\,\mathrm{kN}\),
    \(\|\Delta u_{\mathrm{embed}}\|_{\max}\approx3.4\times10^{-10}\,\mathrm{m}\),
    \(|\sigma_s|_{\max}\approx439.0\,\mathrm{MPa}\), solve time
    \(\approx123.6\,\mathrm{s}\).
\end{itemize}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.78\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_continuum_componentwise_coupling_200mm.pdf}
  \caption{Corrected continuum hysteresis after switching the embedded-bar
  penalty transfer to component-wise PETSc local-to-global indexing.  The
  figure is a preliminary diagnostic, not yet a structural-vs-continuum
  validation claim.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-componentwise-coupling-200mm}
\end{figure}

With the penalty transfer corrected, the structural--continuum comparison
becomes a true local-equivalence question rather than a coupling diagnostic.
The comparison script now writes the section-axis map explicitly: the
structural active bending coordinate maps with opposite sign to continuum
\texttt{RebarBar::ly}, i.e. the prism local \(x\) coordinate, while the
passive coordinate maps directly to \texttt{RebarBar::lz}, i.e. the prism
local \(y\) coordinate.  This matters because the old label-wise comparison
could pair an embedded bar with the physically opposite structural fiber.

The first post-fix matrix uses the common \(50,100,150,200\,\mathrm{mm}\)
protocol, full penalty coupling, corotational continuum kinematics, the
tensile crack-band damage proxy, and the same Menegotto--Pinto steel as the
fiber section.  The compact result is:
\begin{itemize}
  \item \(\texttt{Hex8 2x2x4}\), uniform: first steel integration point at
    \(z\approx169.1\,\mathrm{mm}\), steel loop ratio
    \(W_c/W_s\approx0.23\), global peak-normalized RMS error
    \(\approx1.12\), solve time \(\approx59.5\,\mathrm{s}\);
  \item \(\texttt{Hex8 2x2x12}\), longitudinal bias \(2.0\): first steel
    point at \(z\approx4.7\,\mathrm{mm}\), \(W_c/W_s\approx0.77\), global
    peak-normalized RMS error \(\approx0.39\), solve time
    \(\approx231.8\,\mathrm{s}\);
  \item \(\texttt{Hex8 4x4x8}\), cover/core aligned and uniform in height:
    first steel point at \(z\approx84.5\,\mathrm{mm}\),
    \(W_c/W_s\approx0.11\), global peak-normalized RMS error
    \(\approx0.43\), solve time \(\approx496.1\,\mathrm{s}\);
  \item \(\texttt{Hex8 4x4x8}\), cover/core aligned with longitudinal bias
    \(1.5\): first steel point at \(z\approx29.9\,\mathrm{mm}\),
    \(W_c/W_s\approx0.36\), global peak-normalized RMS error
    \(\approx0.35\), solve time \(\approx542.8\,\mathrm{s}\).
  \item \(\texttt{Hex8 4x4x12}\), cover/core aligned with longitudinal bias
    \(2.0\): first steel point at \(z\approx4.7\,\mathrm{mm}\),
    \(W_c/W_s\approx0.69\), global peak-normalized RMS error
    \(\approx0.37\), solve time \(\approx767.1\,\mathrm{s}\).
\end{itemize}
Thus the strongest promoted candidate in this pass is the
\(\texttt{4x4x12}\) cover/core branch with longitudinal bias \(2.0\).  It does
not yet close the structural loop perfectly, but it is the first branch that
combines near-base steel sampling, transverse cover/core fidelity, closed
embedded transfer, and a global hysteresis error comparable to the cheaper
uniform branch.  The remaining gap is now a material/localization calibration
problem, not a fake bar-slip problem.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.90\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_local_equivalence_matrix_200mm.pdf}
  \caption{First corrected structural--continuum local-equivalence matrix at
  \(200\,\mathrm{mm}\).  The energy ratio \(W_c/W_s\) compares the embedded
  steel loop against the axis-mapped structural fiber loop; the global and
  local RMS errors are normalized by the corresponding structural peak to
  avoid meaningless blow-up near cyclic zero crossings.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-local-equivalence-matrix-200mm}
\end{figure}

The promoted branch was then replotted with the kinematic policy carried in
the artifact name to avoid confusing the result with a small-strain or
total-Lagrangian run.  This \(\texttt{Hex8}\;4\times4\times12\) cover/core
branch uses the corotational continuum policy, the tensile crack-band damage
proxy, full component-wise embedded coupling, and the fixed-end crack-band
length.  The complete \(50,100,150,200\,\mathrm{mm}\) cyclic protocol completed
with no failed attempts.  Its peak-normalized global RMS error is
\(\approx0.37\), its peak-normalized steel-stress RMS error is \(\approx0.25\),
the steel-loop ratio is \(W_c/W_s\approx0.69\), and the maximum embedded gap is
\(\approx3.35\times10^{-10}\,\mathrm{m}\).

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.48\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_structural_continuum_hysteresis_overlay_corotational_tensile_crack_band_damage_proxy_et0p1_4x4x12_bias2_200mm.pdf}
  \hfill
  \includegraphics[width=0.48\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_structural_continuum_steel_hysteresis_overlay_corotational_tensile_crack_band_damage_proxy_et0p1_4x4x12_bias2_200mm.pdf}
  \caption{Corotational structural--continuum hysteresis overlays for the
  promoted \(\texttt{Hex8}\;4\times4\times12\) local branch at
  \(200\,\mathrm{mm}\).  Left: global base-shear versus drift.  Right:
  axis-matched structural steel fiber versus embedded truss steel.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-corotational-4x4x12-hysteresis-200mm}
\end{figure}

The same stabilization pass also froze the reinforcement-area equivalence
contract.  The promoted structural fiber section and the promoted continuum
branch \texttt{structural\_matched\_eight\_bar} both resolve
\[
  n_s = 8,\qquad
  A_b = 2.010619298\times10^{-4}\,\mathrm{m^2},\qquad
  A_s = 1.608495439\times10^{-3}\,\mathrm{m^2},
\]
over a gross section area \(A_g = 6.25\times10^{-2}\,\mathrm{m^2}\), hence
\[
  \rho_s^{\mathrm{long}} = A_s/A_g \approx 2.573592702\times10^{-2}.
\]
Both benchmark drivers now write this block into
\texttt{runtime\_manifest.json}.  The continuum manifest additionally writes
\texttt{area\_equivalent\_to\_structural\_baseline}; it is true for the
promoted eight-bar branch and deliberately false for
\texttt{enriched\_twelve\_bar}, whose area is larger by construction.

The second issue was the need for a deliberately cheap local control branch.
Replacing Ko--Bathe outright would still be premature, but the validation did
need a host model cheap enough to expose whether the embedded steel can produce
approximate hysteresis at all. The resulting route is now explicit in
\texttt{src/materials/constitutive\_models/lineal/OrthotropicBimodularConcreteProxy.hh}
and in the new benchmark surface mode
\texttt{orthotropic\_bimodular\_proxy}. It keeps:
\begin{itemize}
  \item a compression-side modulus at the usual code-level proxy
    \(E_c \approx 4700\sqrt{f'_c}\,\mathrm{MPa}\),
  \item a reduced tension-side stiffness ratio,
  \item a diagonal small-strain tangent without Poisson coupling, and
  \item the same nonlinear Menegotto--Pinto steel already used by the section
    and the embedded truss chain.
\end{itemize}

The canonical artifact is now
\texttt{scripts/run\_reduced\_rc\_continuum\_proxy\_host\_audit.py}, frozen in
\texttt{data/output/cyclic\_validation/reboot\_continuum\_proxy\_host\_audit/continuum\_proxy\_host\_summary.json}.
The audit shows that the cheap host is useful, but only in a narrow sense:
\begin{itemize}
  \item embedded interior, \(30\,\mathrm{mm}\), \(t_r = 0.10\):
    \(\sim 77.7\,\mathrm{s}\), \(\sigma_{s,\max}\sim 164.7\,\mathrm{MPa}\);
  \item embedded interior, \(50\,\mathrm{mm}\), \(t_r = 0.10\):
    \(\sim 102.4\,\mathrm{s}\), \(\sigma_{s,\max}\sim 274.7\,\mathrm{MPa}\);
  \item embedded interior, \(75\,\mathrm{mm}\), \(t_r = 0.10\):
    \(\sim 204.3\,\mathrm{s}\), \(\sigma_{s,\max}\sim 412.2\,\mathrm{MPa}\);
  \item boundary bars, \(75\,\mathrm{mm}\), \(t_r = 0.10\):
    \(\sim 202.5\,\mathrm{s}\), \(\sigma_{s,\max}\sim 419.6\,\mathrm{MPa}\).
\end{itemize}

So the cheap host does serve a purpose: it reaches a near-yield steel cycle far
earlier and much more cheaply than the current Ko--Bathe host. But it also
stays uncracked by construction and leaves a much larger host-bar strain gap
than the promoted baseline. That is why it is now promoted only as an
\emph{accelerated control branch}, not as the future microscale local model.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_continuum_cover_core_cyclic_hysteresis_20mm.png}
  \end{minipage}\hfill
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_continuum_cover_core_cyclic_crack_opening_20mm.png}
  \end{minipage}

  foundation. Left: hysteresis overlay of the completed \texttt{Hex20 4x4x2}
  branches. Right: crack-opening progression over the same protocol. The
  interior embedded branch remains the promoted efficient local baseline, while
  the boundary-bar branch stays as an explicit physical comparison path over the
  \(20\,\mathrm{mm}\) window.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-continuum-cover-core-cyclic}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_continuum_cover_core_profile_monotonic_overlay.png}
  \end{minipage}\hfill

  slice. Left: monotonic overlay for \texttt{benchmark\_reference} versus
  \texttt{production\_stabilized}. Right: short cyclic overlay on the same

  crack activity without changing the macro force path materially.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-continuum-cover-core-profile}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_continuum_proxy_host_hysteresis_overlay.png}
  \end{minipage}\hfill
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_continuum_proxy_host_steel_frontier.png}
  \end{minipage}
  \caption{Cheap proxy-host control branch for the reduced RC continuum. Left:
  base-shear hysteresis overlays for the promoted embedded interior branch and
  its boundary-bar control. Right: peak steel stress frontier. The branch is
  computationally useful because it reaches near-yield steel cycles quickly,
  but it remains a control branch because the host stays uncracked by
  construction.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-continuum-proxy-host}
\end{figure}

The cheap branch was then pushed to the originally intended large cyclic window
(\(\pm 200\,\mathrm{mm}\)) against the ten-node Lobatto Timoshenko structural
reference while keeping the same eight-bar steel area and nonlinear
Menegotto--Pinto steel. The canonical artifact is
\texttt{data/output/cyclic\_validation/reboot\_structural\_continuum\_proxy\_host\_cyclic\_200mm\_audit/structural\_continuum\_steel\_hysteresis\_summary.json}.
