For \(E_t/E_c=0.10\), the structural solve reported
\(104.9\,\mathrm{s}\) and the continuum proxy solve
\(65.0\,\mathrm{s}\). The continuum steel reached
\(|\sigma_s|\approx 429\,\mathrm{MPa}\) and
\(|\varepsilon_s|\approx 6.59\times10^{-3}\), so this test rules out the
earlier suspicion that the embedded steel was simply trapped in the elastic
range. The selected host--bar transfer gap was also essentially closed
(\(\max|\Delta\varepsilon|\approx2.1\times10^{-10}\)), which makes fictitious
slip an unlikely explanation for the remaining discrepancy.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_structural_continuum_hysteresis_overlay_orthotropic_bimodular_proxy_et0p1_200mm.png}
  \end{minipage}\hfill
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_structural_continuum_steel_hysteresis_overlay_orthotropic_bimodular_proxy_et0p1_200mm.png}
  \end{minipage}
  \caption{Large-amplitude structural-to-continuum proxy-host audit. Left:
  base-shear hysteresis for the structural Timoshenko reference and the
  \texttt{Hex20} continuum proxy at \(E_t/E_c=0.10\). Right:
  orientation-matched steel hysteresis. The embedded steel yields, but the
  proxy host remains too non-degrading to reproduce the full cracked
  structural loop.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-proxy-structural-continuum-200mm}
\end{figure}

The tension-stiffness sensitivity at the same amplitude is summarized by:
\[
\begin{array}{c|c|c|c|c}
E_t/E_c &
t_{\mathrm{solve}}^{\mathrm{cont}}[\mathrm{s}] &
\epsilon_{V,\mathrm{base}}^{\mathrm{rms}} &
\epsilon_{\sigma_s}^{\mathrm{rms}} &
W_s^{\mathrm{cont}}[\mathrm{MPa}]\\
\hline
0.10 & 65.0 & 1.30 & 0.72 & 4.65\\
0.03 & 68.1 & 1.13 & 0.80 & 3.06\\
0.01 & 83.7 & 1.13 & 0.88 & 0.90
\end{array}
\]
This is useful precisely because it is a controlled negative result: reducing
the host tensile stiffness can soften the global force path, but it also
suppresses the local steel loop work instead of making the proxy equivalent to
a cracked concrete host. The proxy branch is therefore retained as a fast
solver/material control case. The physically promoted local continuum still
requires a cracking host, either the stabilized Ko--Bathe branch already under
audit or a later XFEM/DG family.

\subsection{Lightweight tensile crack-band damage proxy}

The next control branch adds tensile degradation without returning to the full
cost of the Ko--Bathe local host. The new constitutive relation is
\texttt{TensileCrackBandDamageConcreteProxy3D}. It is intentionally a proxy:
compression-side normal stiffness is recovered when a diagnostic crack closes,
tension-side normal stiffness and shear are degraded by a scalar damage
variable, and the tangent used by the global solve is secant rather than a
fully consistent damage tangent.

The damage driver is the positive-principal-strain measure
\begin{equation}
  \kappa =
  \left(
    \sum_{i=1}^{3} \left\langle \varepsilon_i \right\rangle_+^2
  \right)^{1/2},
\end{equation}
with irreversible history \(\bar\kappa=\max_t \kappa(t)\). The cracking
threshold is
\begin{equation}
  \varepsilon_0 = \frac{f_t}{E_t},
\end{equation}
and the post-peak tensile stress scale is regularized by the crack-band
length \(l_c\):
\begin{equation}
  \sigma_t(\bar\kappa)
  =
  f_t
  \exp\!\left(
    -\frac{\bar\kappa-\varepsilon_0}
           {G_f/(f_t l_c)}
  \right).
\end{equation}
The scalar damage is then chosen so that the degraded secant stress satisfies
\((1-d)E_t\bar\kappa=\sigma_t(\bar\kappa)\), subject to a small residual
stiffness floor. This makes the branch cheap enough for broad cyclic sweeps
while preserving the two features needed in this stage: irreversible tensile
degradation and crack-band sensitivity to the host element length.

The first full cyclic audit used the refined cheap mesh requested for this
stage:
\[
  \texttt{Hex8}\quad 6\times6\times12,\qquad
  E_t/E_c=0.10,\qquad
  u_{\max}=200~\mathrm{mm}.
\]
The run completed the shared \(50,100,150,200~\mathrm{mm}\) cyclic protocol.
The manifest reports equivalent steel area relative to the structural
baseline, nonlinear Menegotto--Pinto steel, \(3020/3456\) cracked Gauss
points at the end of the run, peak steel stress
\(|\sigma_s|_{\max}\approx444.7~\mathrm{MPa}\), and peak steel strain
\(|\varepsilon_s|_{\max}\approx1.45\times10^{-2}\). Thus the steel is no
longer plausibly trapped in the elastic range and the host does degrade in
tension.

The same evidence also prevents over-promotion. The run required
\(1653.2~\mathrm{s}\), with an average of \(31.1\) Newton iterations and a
maximum of \(104\) on the accepted runtime trace. The local steel loop work of
the continuum trace remains much smaller than the matched structural fiber
trace, \(15.1~\mathrm{MPa}\) versus \(120.4~\mathrm{MPa}\). Therefore this
branch is not a final physical local model; it is a better intermediate
control branch for damage, solver and mesh audits.

The frozen artifacts are:
\begin{itemize}
  \item
  \texttt{data/output/cyclic\_validation/reboot\_structural\_continuum\_damage\_proxy\_cyclic\_200mm\_6x6x12\_hex8\_audit/structural\_continuum\_steel\_hysteresis\_summary.json};
  \item
  \texttt{doc/figures/validation\_reboot/reduced\_rc\_structural\_continuum\_hysteresis\_overlay\_tensile\_crack\_band\_damage\_proxy\_et0p1\_200mm.png};
  \item
  \texttt{doc/figures/validation\_reboot/reduced\_rc\_structural\_continuum\_steel\_hysteresis\_overlay\_tensile\_crack\_band\_damage\_proxy\_et0p1\_200mm.png}.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{PETSc factorization policy and fixed-end sampling}

The remaining cost was then audited one level lower, at the PETSc
matrix/linear-solve boundary.  The correction is architectural rather than a
global options tweak: the linear solver tuning now lives inside
\texttt{NonlinearSolveProfile}.  The new validation preset
\texttt{newton-l2-lu-symbolic-reuse-only} preserves the same nonlinear solve
used by the promoted continuum runs, namely Newton line search with the
L2/secant line search and \(\texttt{KSPPREONLY}+\texttt{PCLU}\), but it also
declares a factorization policy:
\[
  \texttt{MATORDERINGRCM},\qquad
  \texttt{PCFactorSetReuseOrdering}=\mathrm{true},\qquad
  \texttt{PCFactorSetReuseFill}=\mathrm{true}.
\]
This is deliberately weaker than reusing a whole preconditioner.  For the
present finite-element mesh the sparsity graph is fixed across Newton
iterations, so reusing symbolic ordering and fill information is admissible;
the numerical factorization is still rebuilt from the current tangent values.
By contrast, \texttt{KSPSetReusePreconditioner} is left off by default because
it can preserve a stale numerical preconditioner across changing nonlinear
tangents.

The first timing evidence is intentionally conservative.  On
\(\texttt{Hex8}\;4\times4\times2\) at \(20\,\mathrm{mm}\), the new preset
reports \(2.364\,\mathrm{s}\) versus \(2.293\,\mathrm{s}\) for the previous
\texttt{newton-l2-only} profile.  On
\(\texttt{Hex8}\;6\times6\times4\) at \(50\,\mathrm{mm}\), it reports
\(51.605\,\mathrm{s}\) versus \(51.799\,\mathrm{s}\).  The response is
identical in both comparisons.  Therefore this change is not promoted as a
large speedup yet; it is promoted as the correct typed seam for later testing
of block AIJ storage, symmetric storage, lagged preconditioners, iterative
KSPs, and the PETSc \(\texttt{TS}\) dynamic path.

The more important physical result came from the same pass.  The uniform
\(\texttt{Hex8}\;6\times6\times12\) damage-proxy mesh samples the first rebar
Gauss point at \(z=56.35\,\mathrm{mm}\).  In a monotonic \(50\,\mathrm{mm}\)
probe, that point reaches only
\(\varepsilon_s\approx2.4\times10^{-3}\).  With the same transverse mesh and
\(\texttt{longitudinal\_bias\_power}=2\), the first point moves to
\(z=4.70\,\mathrm{mm}\) and reaches
\(\varepsilon_s\approx1.1\times10^{-2}\).  The selected host--bar strain gap
remains of order \(10^{-9}\), so the discrepancy is not explained by a
fictitious embedded-bar slip.  It is primarily a sampling/localization issue
near the fixed end, amplified by the difference between structural
section-kinematics and the three-dimensional continuum field.

The biased full cyclic run completed the same
\(50,100,150,200\,\mathrm{mm}\) protocol:
\[
\begin{array}{l|c|c}
\text{case} & t_{\mathrm{solve}}[\mathrm{s}] &
W_s^{\mathrm{cont}}[\mathrm{MPa}]\\
\hline
\texttt{6x6x12 uniform} & 1653.2 & 15.1\\
\texttt{6x6x12 bias=2} & 1345.6 & 119.6
\end{array}
\]
The matched structural trace for the biased comparison gives
\(W_s^{\mathrm{struct}}\approx169.0\,\mathrm{MPa}\).  Thus the fixed-end bias
does not make the continuum identical to the Timoshenko section model, nor
should it: the continuum may warp and localize cracks in ways a plane-section
beam cannot.  But it removes the major artificial under-sampling of the steel
cycle and becomes the better continuation point for the local-model branch.

The relevant artifacts are:
\begin{itemize}
  \item
  \texttt{data/output/cyclic\_validation/reboot\_structural\_continuum\_damage\_proxy\_cyclic\_200mm\_6x6x12\_hex8\_bias2\_audit/structural\_continuum\_steel\_hysteresis\_summary.json};
  \item
  \texttt{doc/figures/validation\_reboot/reduced\_rc\_structural\_continuum\_hysteresis\_overlay\_tensile\_crack\_band\_damage\_proxy\_et0p1\_6x6x12\_bias2\_200mm.png};
  \item
  \texttt{doc/figures/validation\_reboot/reduced\_rc\_structural\_continuum\_steel\_hysteresis\_overlay\_tensile\_crack\_band\_damage\_proxy\_et0p1\_6x6x12\_bias2\_200mm.png}.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Structural section-fiber refinement audit}

The continuum branch was still substantially softer than the structural
reference, so the structural section itself was re-opened before drawing any
continuum conclusion.  The executable
\texttt{fall\_n\_reduced\_rc\_column\_reference\_benchmark} now accepts
\texttt{--section-fiber-profile} with four concrete-patch profiles:
\texttt{coarse}, \texttt{canonical}, \texttt{fine}, and \texttt{ultra}.  The
steel layout is not part of the refinement variable: all profiles keep the same
eight bars at \(y,z=\{0,\pm0.095\}\,\mathrm{m}\) and the same total steel area
\(A_s=1.60849544\times10^{-3}\,\mathrm{m^2}\).  The new regression
\texttt{test\_rc\_section\_layout.cpp} freezes this invariant by checking
concrete area, steel area, first moments and bar coordinates for every profile.

The audit also re-checked the apparently trivial but dangerous axis question.
In the structural column the imposed lateral displacement is DOF \(x\), the
column axis is \(z\), and the active bending observable is
\((\kappa_y,M_y)\).  In the continuum column the top-face displacement and the
support resultant are also extracted in component \(x\).  The steel comparison
keeps the explicit map
\[
  z_{\mathrm{struct}} = -\,l_y^{\mathrm{RebarBar}}, \qquad
  y_{\mathrm{struct}} = l_z^{\mathrm{RebarBar}},
\]
so the active bending side is not inferred from a coincident CSV column name.

The \(50,100,150,200\,\mathrm{mm}\) cyclic run with
\texttt{TimoshenkoBeamN<10>}, Lobatto stations, clamped top bending rotation,
and \(-0.02\,\mathrm{MN}\) axial compression gave:
\[
\begin{array}{l|r|r|c|c|c}
\text{profile} & n_{\mathrm{fib}} & t_{\mathrm{solve}}[\mathrm{s}] &
\text{status} & |V|_{\max}[\mathrm{kN}] &
\epsilon_{V,\mathrm{rms}}^{\mathrm{vs\ ultra}}\\
\hline
\texttt{coarse} & 29 & 541.2 & \text{failed at }175\,\mathrm{mm} &
50.12 & 2.29\times10^{-2}\\
\texttt{canonical} & 92 & 107.6 & \text{completed} &
49.37 & 1.47\times10^{-2}\\
\texttt{fine} & 344 & 130.2 & \text{completed} &
49.81 & 3.13\times10^{-3}\\
\texttt{ultra} & 764 & 244.7 & \text{completed} &
49.95 & 0
\end{array}
\]
Thus the old canonical section is not exact, but it is not the source of the
large structural--continuum softness either.  Refining the structural fiber
section moves the structural hysteresis by only a few percent and actually
slightly increases the reference peak stiffness.  The continuum gap must
therefore continue to be investigated in the continuum field: localization near
the fixed end, plane-section versus three-dimensional kinematics, crack-band
regularization, and host--steel load sharing.

After the boundary-kinematics correction above, the same high-fidelity
question was repeated in the promoted \emph{free top rotation} setting for the
two finest profiles.  This is the relevant structural reference for the
current lateral-only continuum top boundary:
\[
\begin{array}{l|r|r|c|c|c}
\text{profile} & n_{\mathrm{fib}} & t_{\mathrm{solve}}[\mathrm{s}] &
\text{status} & |V|_{\max}[\mathrm{kN}] &
\epsilon_{V,\mathrm{rms}}^{\mathrm{vs\ ultra}}\\
\hline
\texttt{fine} & 344 & 156.2 & \text{completed} &
23.532 & 8.40\times10^{-4}\\
\texttt{ultra} & 764 & 161.6 & \text{completed} &
23.546 & 0
\end{array}
\]
The steel area remains \(1.60849544\times10^{-3}\,\mathrm{m^2}\) in both
profiles and the first moments stay at numerical zero.  The fine section is
therefore promoted as the practical free-rotation structural reference: it
matches the ultra section within \(0.084\%\) RMS peak-normalized base shear
while costing slightly less.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_structural_section_fiber_mesh_hysteresis.png}
  \end{minipage}\hfill
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_structural_section_fiber_mesh_convergence.png}
  \end{minipage}
  \caption{Free-rotation structural section-fiber refinement audit.  Left:
  base-shear hysteresis for the \texttt{fine} and \texttt{ultra}
  concrete-patch refinement profiles.  Right: convergence of the base-shear
  history against the \texttt{ultra} profile.  The \texttt{fine} profile is
  already close enough to the ultra reference for the current continuum bridge.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-structural-section-fiber-mesh-audit}
\end{figure}

The promoted free-rotation artifact is
\texttt{data/output/cyclic\_validation/reboot\_structural\_section\_fiber\_mesh\_audit\_200mm\_free\_rotation\_fine\_ultra/structural\_section\_fiber\_mesh\_audit\_summary.json}.

\subsection{Multi-element structural reference before the continuum bridge}

The structural reference has now been reopened in the direction that is most
relevant for the continuum comparison: not only more fibers inside one
section, but several structural elements along the cantilever height.  The
driver exposes this through
\[
  \texttt{--structural-element-count } n_e,
\]
so the same \texttt{TimoshenkoBeamN<N>} interpolation, quadrature family,
fiber section and axial-preload contract can be replicated over a mesh of
beam-column elements.  For a Lobatto rule, adjacent elements intentionally
share end stations; duplicated global \(\xi\) values at element interfaces
are therefore physical station duplicates, not a post-processing error.

The current high-resolution promoted candidate is
\[
  n_e=6,\qquad N=4,\qquad
  \texttt{beam-integration}=\texttt{lobatto},
\]
which gives eighteen section stations along the column and keeps the steel
area fixed at \(1.60849544\times10^{-3}\,\mathrm{m^2}\).  This reference
completed the large cyclic \(\pm 200\,\mathrm{mm}\) internal run in
\(\approx 22.1\,\mathrm{s}\) in an earlier coarse protocol.  A twelve-element
variant was also attempted, but it exposed a genuine continuation/localization
frontier near the large-amplitude reversals rather than an obvious geometry or
fiber-mapping bug; it is therefore kept as a future arc-length/regularization
target and not promoted as the practical reference yet.

The external OpenSeesPy revalidation was rebuilt with the same number of
structural elements and an explicitly declared element family.  Rechecking the
older evidence confirmed an important nuance: the most stable short-cyclic
OpenSees branch is not the Lobatto multi-element force-based route, but the
\(2\mathrm{D}\) \texttt{forceBeamColumn} branch with Gauss--Legendre stations,
\texttt{Concrete01}, and explicit local compatibility iterations
\(\texttt{-iter}\;10\;10^{-12}\).  Under the short cyclic
\(\pm 50\,\mathrm{mm}\) protocol, the station parity is exact to
\(4.6\times10^{-10}\) in global \(\xi\), and the global errors are already in
the engineering-comparison range:
\[
\begin{array}{l|r|r}
\text{observable} & \epsilon_{\max} & \epsilon_{\mathrm{rms}}\\
\hline
V_{\mathrm{base}} & 5.75\times10^{-2} & 3.36\times10^{-2}\\
M_{\mathrm{base}} & 5.75\times10^{-2} & 3.36\times10^{-2}\\
\kappa_{\mathrm{base}} & 3.28\times10^{-2} & 1.76\times10^{-2}
\end{array}
\]
The force-based branch should therefore be kept as the preferred local
equilibrium check in the short cyclic window.  However, it still fails in the
multi-element large-amplitude cycles: Lobatto force-based elements fail on the
\(50\,\mathrm{mm}\) unloading branch, and Legendre force-based elements fail
near the \(100\,\mathrm{mm}\) positive peak even with finer increments and
twenty-five local compatibility iterations.  The larger monotonic envelope is
therefore still best checked with the \(2\mathrm{D}\)
\texttt{dispBeamColumn}--Lobatto branch, which closes to
\(150\,\mathrm{mm}\):
\[
\epsilon_{V,\mathrm{rms}}=4.85\times10^{-2},\qquad
\epsilon_{M,\mathrm{rms}}=4.16\times10^{-2}.
\]
OpenSees reaches the \(200\,\mathrm{mm}\) monotonic target only partially,
failing when stepping from roughly \(158\,\mathrm{mm}\) toward
\(162.5\,\mathrm{mm}\), and the cyclic branch becomes fragile on the first
large unloading step from \(100\,\mathrm{mm}\).  That behavior is now recorded
as part of the benchmark contract: OpenSees remains a valuable independent
sanity check, but the large-amplitude continuum comparison should use the
completed \texttt{fall\_n} multi-element structural branch as its primary
internal reference.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_structural_multielement_hifi_overlay_cyclic_50mm_falln_n4_e6_legendre_ops_force2d_ip3_Concrete01.png}
  \end{minipage}\hfill
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_structural_multielement_hifi_overlay_monotonic_150mm_falln_n4_e6_lobatto_ops_disp2d_ip3_Concrete01.png}
  \end{minipage}
  \caption{Multi-element structural reference against OpenSeesPy.  Left:
  complete short cyclic \(\pm50\,\mathrm{mm}\) comparison using the
  equilibrium-richer force-based Legendre OpenSees branch.  Right: monotonic
  \(150\,\mathrm{mm}\) envelope using the displacement-based Lobatto branch
  before the OpenSees large-displacement singularity encountered near
  \(162.5\,\mathrm{mm}\).}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-structural-multielement-hifi}
\end{figure}

This changes the next continuum gate.  The continuum model no longer needs to
match a single high-order structural element whose plastic hinge is compressed
into one interpolation object.  It must now be compared against the
multi-element \(6\times\texttt{TimoshenkoBeamN<4>}\) reference, which is a
fairer structural envelope for a local solid column with distributed cracking,
embedded bars and fixed-end localization.

The first continuum replay with this new structural reference is deliberately
cheap: \(\texttt{Hex8}\;4\times4\times4\), corotational kinematics, the
orthotropic bimodular proxy host, and eight embedded bars with area exactly
matched to the structural section.  It is not a promoted physical solution,
but it isolates the next source of discrepancy.  Against the free-rotation
multi-element structural reference, the continuum peak base shear reaches
about \(116\,\mathrm{kN}\) while the structural reference reaches only about
\(18\,\mathrm{kN}\); the peak-normalized RMS base-shear error is therefore
about \(3.63\).  Reusing exactly the same continuum solve against a guided
top-rotation structural reference reduces that peak-normalized RMS error to
about \(1.10\).  This is still not a closure, but it is a strong diagnostic:
the local embedded-bar transfer is not the dominant error source, because the
host--bar axial strain gap remains of order \(10^{-9}\).  The remaining gap is
therefore controlled by the continuum host and by the top-boundary kinematic
contract.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_structural_continuum_hysteresis_overlay_corotational_orthotropic_bimodular_proxy_et0p1_4x4x4_bias1p5_struct_free_n4_e6_lobatto_50mm.png}
  \end{minipage}\hfill
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_structural_continuum_hysteresis_overlay_corotational_orthotropic_bimodular_proxy_et0p1_4x4x4_bias1p5_struct_clamped_n4_e6_lobatto_50mm.png}
  \end{minipage}
  \caption{First continuum replay against the multi-element structural
  reference.  Left: free-rotation structural comparison.  Right: guided
  top-rotation structural comparison using the same continuum solution.  The
  error reduction identifies the top-boundary kinematic contract as a primary
  continuum-validation variable.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-structural-continuum-multielement-boundary}
\end{figure}

\paragraph{Cheap degrading-host replay against the multi-element reference.}
The next replay, retained here as a historical diagnostic, replaces the
non-degrading bimodular host by the \texttt{cyclic-crack-band-concrete} branch
while keeping the same corotational continuum kinematics, eight
structural-matched embedded bars, lateral-only top-face displacement control,
and the then-active host-surface axial preload transfer. It is not promoted as
final equivalence evidence below, because the later axial-resultant audit
showed that this load-transfer route contaminates the structural--continuum
comparison.
This is not a return to the expensive Ko--Bathe branch.  It is an intermediate
local concrete law with crack-band regularization and cyclic crack memory,
introduced specifically to test whether the continuum error was being driven
mostly by the absence of host degradation.

The answer is yes, but only up to a point.  On the
\(\texttt{Hex8}\;4\times4\times4\) mesh, the free-rotation structural
comparison improves from a peak-normalized RMS base-shear error of
\(3.63\) with the orthotropic proxy to \(0.684\) with the degrading host.
Comparing the same continuum solution against the guided-top structural
reference reduces the global RMS error further to \(0.246\).  The embedded
transfer audit remains clean in both reads:
\[
  \mathrm{rms}\bigl(\varepsilon_{\mathrm{host}}-
  \varepsilon_{\mathrm{bar}}\bigr)\approx 8.8\times10^{-10},
\]
so the steel is not slipping numerically inside the host.  The local steel
loop is still not closed, however: against the guided-top structural fiber,
the peak-normalized RMS stress error is about \(0.205\), and the continuum
steel-loop work is only \(0.125\) of the structural fiber-loop work.

A blind refinement is therefore not justified.  The
\(\texttt{Hex8}\;6\times6\times8\) replay completed successfully, but it
increased the guided-top global RMS error to \(0.398\) and the steel stress
RMS error to \(0.241\), while the solve time rose from \(36.2\,\mathrm{s}\)
to \(282.2\,\mathrm{s}\).  The first Gauss point on the selected embedded bar
also moved from \(z\approx84.5\,\mathrm{mm}\) to
\(z\approx29.9\,\mathrm{mm}\), which means the local comparison is probing a
different point in the localization front.  The new conclusion is sharper:
the continuum validation should not be advanced by adding elements
indiscriminately.  It needs a controlled crack-localization/hinge-length
contract, or an enriched local formulation such as XFEM/DG, before the
structural fiber loop and the 3D solid loop can be expected to coincide point
by point.

\[
\begin{array}{l|l|r|r|r}
\text{continuum read} & \text{structural top} &
\mathrm{RMS}_{V_b}^{\mathrm{peak}} &
\mathrm{RMS}_{\sigma_s}^{\mathrm{peak}} &
W_s^{\mathrm{cont}}/W_s^{\mathrm{struct}}\\
\hline
\texttt{orthotropic}\;4\times4\times4 & \text{free} & 3.626 & 0.452 & 73.79\\
\texttt{orthotropic}\;4\times4\times4 & \text{guided} & 1.104 & 0.208 & 0.149\\
\texttt{cyclic-crack-band}\;4\times4\times4 & \text{free} & 0.684 & 0.401 & 61.62\\
\texttt{cyclic-crack-band}\;4\times4\times4 & \text{guided} & 0.246 & 0.205 & 0.125\\
\texttt{cyclic-crack-band}\;6\times6\times8 & \text{guided} & 0.398 & 0.241 & 0.160
\end{array}
\]

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_structural_continuum_hysteresis_overlay_corotational_cyclic_crack_band_concrete_ts0p1_4x4x4_bias1p5_cap_lateral_translation_only_struct_clamped_n4_e6_lobatto_50mm.png}
  \end{minipage}\hfill
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_structural_continuum_hysteresis_overlay_corotational_cyclic_crack_band_concrete_ts0p1_6x6x8_bias1p5_cap_lateral_translation_only_struct_clamped_n4_e6_lobatto_50mm.png}
  \end{minipage}
  \caption{Degrading-host structural--continuum replay against the guided-top
  multi-element structural reference.  Left: \(\texttt{Hex8}\;4\times4\times4\)
  with \texttt{cyclic-crack-band-concrete}.  Right:
  \(\texttt{Hex8}\;6\times6\times8\) with the same physical contract.  The
  refined mesh is more expensive and does not monotonically improve the global
  hysteresis, which indicates localization/model-form sensitivity rather than
  a simple spatial-underresolution bug.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-cyclic-crack-band-multielement-50mm}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_structural_continuum_steel_hysteresis_overlay_corotational_cyclic_crack_band_concrete_ts0p1_4x4x4_bias1p5_cap_lateral_translation_only_struct_clamped_n4_e6_lobatto_50mm.png}
  \end{minipage}\hfill
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_structural_continuum_steel_hysteresis_overlay_corotational_cyclic_crack_band_concrete_ts0p1_6x6x8_bias1p5_cap_lateral_translation_only_struct_clamped_n4_e6_lobatto_50mm.png}
  \end{minipage}
  \caption{Matched steel-bar hysteresis for the same degrading-host replay.
  The host--bar kinematic transfer remains numerically tight, but the loop
  energy and local stress path still depend on the continuum localization point.
  This is the next validation target before moving the local model into the
  multiscale exchange path.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-cyclic-crack-band-steel-50mm}
\end{figure}

\paragraph{Plastic-hinge band metric and the \(200\,\mathrm{mm}\) gate.}
The preceding single-Gauss-point steel comparison was intentionally severe,
but it is not mesh-objective once the continuum localizes damage.  The audit
therefore now exports a second local metric: a fixed-end steel band average.
Unless the user declares a different value, the band length is one structural
element,
\[
  L_{\mathrm{band}} = H/n_e = 3.2/6 = 0.5333\,\mathrm{m},
\]
and both the structural fiber histories and the embedded-truss histories are
averaged over that physical interval with one-dimensional Voronoi weights.
This preserves the pointwise trace for debugging, but it prevents a single
mesh-dependent Gauss point from representing the full plastic hinge.

On the \(50\,\mathrm{mm}\) guided-top replay this changes the local read in a
physically useful way.  For \(\texttt{Hex8}\;4\times4\times4\), the pointwise
steel-loop work ratio is \(0.125\), while the hinge-band work ratio is
\(0.406\).  For \(\texttt{Hex8}\;6\times6\times8\), the band stress RMS
improves to \(0.190\), but the work ratio remains only \(0.162\).  The
refined mesh is therefore not rejected because it is unstable; it is rejected
for this validation gate because it is much more expensive and does not
improve the global response or the band energy in a monotone way.

After the axial-resultant correction, the promoted cheap degrading-host branch
was carried again through the full cyclic protocol up to \(200\,\mathrm{mm}\):
\[
\begin{array}{l|r|r|r|r}
\text{read} & t_{\mathrm{solve}}[\mathrm{s}] &
\mathrm{RMS}_{V_b}^{\mathrm{peak}} &
\mathrm{RMS}_{\sigma_s,\mathrm{band}}^{\mathrm{peak}} &
W_{s,\mathrm{band}}^{\mathrm{cont}}/W_{s,\mathrm{band}}^{\mathrm{struct}}\\
\hline
4\times4\times4,\ \text{guided top, axial gate} & 396.4 & 0.659 & 0.107 & 0.909
\end{array}
\]
This is the present closure of the first continuum validation stage.  The
embedded steel chain is now credible at large amplitude: the host--bar strain
gap remains \(\mathcal{O}(10^{-10})\), the local band stress RMS is about
\(0.107\) of the structural peak, and the band loop work is \(0.909\) of the
structural reference.  The corrected axial gate is also tight: the maximum
base-axial deviation from the imposed \(0.02\,\mathrm{MN}\) preload is only
\(2.24\times10^{-8}\,\mathrm{MN}\).  The global continuum column is still too
strong in peak base shear, however; at \(200\,\mathrm{mm}\) the continuum peak
is about \(97.8\,\mathrm{kN}\), compared with about \(44.6\,\mathrm{kN}\) for
the guided structural reference.  The remaining open problem is therefore not
the embedded truss, nor the area/position of the steel, nor the axial load
contract, but the host concrete crack-localization and shear-transfer model.
The crack-state gate makes this explicit: all host Gauss points become cracked
and open by an equivalent imposed drift of about \(23.1\,\mathrm{mm}\), while
the largest recorded crack opening reaches \(23.2\,\mathrm{mm}\).  This is too
diffuse to represent a localized fixed-end plastic hinge and explains why the
steel chain can look physically credible while the global column remains too
stiff in shear.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.78\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/structural_continuum_crack_gate_200mm.png}
  \caption{Crack-localization gate for the corrected \(200\,\mathrm{mm}\)
  structural--continuum replay.  The host crack field saturates almost the
  whole mesh early in the protocol.  The next local-model target is therefore
  objective hinge localization and crack shear transfer, not another embedded
  steel-area or boundary-condition audit.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-structural-continuum-crack-gate-200mm}
\end{figure}

A negative control confirms that this cannot be solved by simply making the
cracks arbitrarily soft.  Setting the residual shear floor to \(0.01\), the
large-opening shear floor to \(0.002\), the decay strain to \(0.02\), and the
open-crack compression transfer to \(0.001\) worsens the \(50\,\mathrm{mm}\)
guided-top global RMS error to \(0.509\) and almost eliminates the steel loop
work \((W_s^{cont}/W_s^{struct}\approx2\times10^{-4})\).  That branch is
therefore retained only as a control.  The next scientifically defensible step
is a richer local crack-transfer model with objective localization length
and/or an enriched XFEM/DG representation, not another scalar softening knob.

\paragraph{Opening-dependent crack-transfer laws.}
That negative control led to a narrower literature gate: keep only crack
transfer laws whose state variables can be compared across a smeared Gauss
point, a future cohesive XFEM interface, and the Ko--Bathe branch. Three
families were retained. First, fixed or rotating smeared-crack models with
opening-dependent shear retention, following the fixed/rotating crack taxonomy
of Rots and Blaauwendraad \cite{RotsBlaauwendraad1989CrackModels}, provide the
cheapest extension of the current crack-band branch. Second, rough-crack or
aggregate-interlock models relate tangential transfer to crack opening,
sliding, and closure/contact \cite{BazantGambarova1980RoughCracks,
Walraven1981AggregateInterlock}; this is the physically relevant mechanism
missing from a constant shear floor. Third, compression-field models such as
MCFT \cite{VecchioCollins1986MCFT} and the Ko--Bathe concrete model
\cite{KoBathe2026} carry richer concrete/reinforcement compatibility and
crack closing/reopening information, but are not scalar local shear-retention
laws and therefore should not be forced into the same interface prematurely.

The two lightweight laws were implemented in
\texttt{src/fracture/CrackShearTransferLaw.hh} and are shared by the smeared
crack-band materials and the XFEM cohesive law. The first law is the promoted
opening-exponential branch:
\[
  \eta_s(w)=\eta_\infty+
  \left(\eta_0-\eta_\infty\right)
  \exp\!\left(-\frac{\langle w\rangle_+}{w_d}\right),
\]
where \(w=l_c\langle\kappa-\kappa_0\rangle_+\) is the objective crack-band
opening, \(\eta_0\) is the residual shear ratio at crack initiation,
\(\eta_\infty\) is the wide-crack floor, and \(w_d\) is the decay opening.
The second law adds a smooth closure gate:
\[
  \eta_s(w,\varepsilon_n)=
  \eta_s(w) + g_c(\varepsilon_n)
  \left(\eta_{\mathrm{closed}}-\eta_s(w)\right),
  \qquad
  g_c=s^2(3-2s),\quad
  s=\operatorname{clamp}\!\left(
    \frac{\langle-\varepsilon_n\rangle_+}{\varepsilon_c},0,1\right).
\]
This is not a full aggregate-interlock model because it does not yet evolve
slip-dependent rough-crack contact stresses. It is a deliberately bounded
proxy: if the crack closes or enters normal compression, the tangential
transfer can recover toward \(\eta_{\mathrm{closed}}\), while an open crack
still degrades with opening. The MCFT family was selected but not implemented
as a scalar law because it needs panel-level average strain compatibility,
reinforcement ratios and crack spacing; forcing it into a Gauss-point shear
floor would hide the actual physics.

The direct \(100\,\mathrm{mm}\) cyclic gate is revealing. The opening-
exponential law with \(\eta_0=0.10\) completes in \(148.8\,\mathrm{s}\), gives
a peak-normalized global base-shear RMS error of \(0.2324\), and keeps the
hinge-band steel work ratio near \(0.961\). The compression-gated proxy
completes in \(125.0\,\mathrm{s}\) with essentially the same global error
\((0.2325)\) and steel work ratio \((0.961)\). This near identity is a useful
diagnostic: the present scalar \texttt{cyclic-crack-band} state does not carry
enough objective crack-normal closure information for the closure gate to
become active. The richer \texttt{fixed-crack-band} branch, which does carry
fixed crack normals, timed out in the comparable \(50\,\mathrm{mm}\) cyclic
gate after \(900\,\mathrm{s}\). Ko--Bathe remains in the audit as the heavier
reference: the existing monotonic \(20\,\mathrm{mm}\) crack runs complete in
\(44.0\,\mathrm{s}\) for \(\texttt{Hex20}\) and \(164.8\,\mathrm{s}\) for
\(\texttt{Hex27}\), with first cracking at \(6.67\,\mathrm{mm}\). The conclusion
is sharper than before: the cheap branch can be benchmarked, but closure-aware
transfer will only matter after the crack state is made directional/objective
or moved to an enriched interface.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.88\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/structural_continuum_crack_transfer_model_audit.png}
  \caption{Crack-transfer model audit for the local RC-column branch.  The two
  lightweight opening-dependent laws now share the same implementation seam;
  the compression-gated proxy is almost inactive in the current scalar
  crack-band state, the richer fixed-crack control is not yet computationally
  viable, and Ko--Bathe remains the heavier crack-opening reference rather than
  a cheap cyclic local model.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-crack-transfer-model-audit}
\end{figure}

\paragraph{Fixed-crack enriched control and first XFEM seam.}
The next attempted intermediate model was deliberately richer than the scalar
cyclic branch, but still cheaper than a full discontinuity method:
\texttt{FixedCrackBandConcrete3D}.  Its state stores up to three fixed crack
normals, evaluates the trial strain in the crack basis, degrades normal and
shear transfer by crack direction, and applies unilateral closure before
rotating the stress back to the global frame.  The first implementation used a
central-difference tangent.  That was physically tempting but algorithmically
wrong for the global Newton iteration: near crack insertion, the \(+h\) and
\(-h\) perturbations can activate different crack sets.  The \(50\,\mathrm{mm}\)
cycle then failed near \(p=0.6875\) after \(2216\,\mathrm{s}\), with all
\(512\) host Gauss points already cracked.

The tangent was therefore replaced by a frozen fixed-crack secant operator.
For the current trial strain, the crack basis \(R_c\), crack damage, and crack
closure state are fixed during linearization:
\[
  \Delta\boldsymbol\sigma =
  \mathcal{T}_{\sigma}^{-1}(R_c)
  \mathbf{D}_{c}^{\mathrm{sec}}
  \mathcal{T}_{\varepsilon}(R_c)\Delta\boldsymbol\varepsilon.
\]
This is not a full consistent tangent, but it is the correct semantic tangent
for this low-cost branch: it linearizes the same fixed-crack surface that is
being solved, rather than differentiating through a discontinuous crack-set
selection.  Unit tests now cover compression recovery, subcritical tension,
rotated principal-crack insertion, shear-transfer degradation, and orthogonal
second-crack memory.

With that correction, the same \(\texttt{Hex8}\;4\times4\times4\) guided-top
cycle completes in \(313.6\,\mathrm{s}\).  The physical result is still not
promoted:
\[
\begin{array}{l|r|r|r|r}
\text{host branch} & t_{\mathrm{solve}}[\mathrm{s}] &
\mathrm{RMS}_{V_b}^{\mathrm{peak}} &
\mathrm{RMS}_{\sigma_s,\mathrm{band}}^{\mathrm{peak}} &
W_{s,\mathrm{band}}^{\mathrm{cont}}/W_{s,\mathrm{band}}^{\mathrm{struct}}\\
\hline
\texttt{cyclic-crack-band} & 36.2 & 0.246 & 0.290 & 0.406\\
\texttt{fixed-crack-band} & 313.6 & 0.390 & 0.228 & 0.131\\
\texttt{fixed-crack-band},\ \eta_s=0.8 & \text{failed at }2777.6 & - & - & -
\end{array}
\]
The default fixed-crack law reduces the hinge-band stress RMS but makes the
global column too soft at \(50\,\mathrm{mm}\): the peak base shear is about
\(23\,\mathrm{kN}\), compared with about \(44\,\mathrm{kN}\) in the guided
multi-element structural reference.  Increasing crack shear retention to
\(\eta_s=0.8\) does not rescue the branch; it stalls during reversal after
many cutbacks.  The conclusion is therefore conservative and useful: the
fixed-crack smeared law is a documented comparison branch and an algorithmic
lesson, not the promoted local model.

Because that result points to discrete/localized fracture rather than another
scalar damage knob, the repository now contains the first concrete XFEM seam:
\texttt{src/xfem/XFEMEnrichment.hh}.  It implements a plane level-set crack
\(\phi(\mathbf{x})\), the shifted Heaviside enrichment
\[
  \psi_I(\mathbf{x}) =
  H(\phi(\mathbf{x})) - H(\phi(\mathbf{x}_I)),
\]
and cut-element node marking.  The shifted form is the standard partition-of-
unity choice because the enrichment vanishes at the enriched node and preserves
the ordinary nodal interpolation.  The eventual enriched displacement field
will therefore enter as
\[
  \mathbf{u}_h(\mathbf{x}) =
  \sum_I N_I(\mathbf{x})\mathbf{u}_I
  + \sum_{J\in\mathcal{E}} N_J(\mathbf{x})\,
    \psi_J(\mathbf{x})\mathbf{a}_J
  + \cdots ,
\]
rather than by modifying the existing embedded-bar or material interfaces.
The test \texttt{xfem\_enrichment} validates side classification, shifted-jump
values and cut-element enrichment masks.  This is intentionally not yet a full
XFEM solid element; it is the first tested preprocessing and interpolation
contract needed to add one without destabilizing the validated continuum and
multiscale surfaces.

The second XFEM step adds the bulk kinematic operator needed by such an
element.  The header \texttt{src/xfem/XFEMEnrichedApproximation.hh} evaluates
the shifted-Heaviside displacement
\[
  \mathbf{u}_h(\mathbf{x}) =
  \sum_I N_I(\mathbf{x})\mathbf{u}_I
  +
  \sum_{J\in\mathcal{E}} N_J(\mathbf{x})
  \left[H(\phi(\mathbf{x}))-H(\phi(\mathbf{x}_J))\right]\mathbf{a}_J ,
\]
the regular bulk gradient away from the crack surface
\[
  \nabla\mathbf{u}_h =
  \sum_I \mathbf{u}_I\otimes\nabla N_I
  +
  \sum_{J\in\mathcal{E}}
  \left[H(\phi(\mathbf{x}))-H(\phi(\mathbf{x}_J))\right]
  \mathbf{a}_J\otimes\nabla N_J ,
\]
and the crack trace jump
\[
  \left[\!\left[ \mathbf{u}_h \right]\!\right] =
  2\sum_{J\in\mathcal{E}} N_J(\mathbf{x}_{\Gamma})\mathbf{a}_J .
\]
The distributional term
\(\delta_{\Gamma}\mathbf{a}_J\otimes\nabla\phi\) is deliberately not folded
into the volumetric strain; it belongs to a future cohesive/interface
integration contribution.  The exported kinematic record therefore separates
the regular engineering strain,
\(\boldsymbol\varepsilon=\operatorname{sym}\nabla\mathbf{u}_h\), from the
normal and tangential crack opening,
\[
  w_n=\mathbf{n}_{\Gamma}\cdot
  \left[\!\left[\mathbf{u}_h\right]\!\right],\qquad
  \mathbf{w}_t=
  \left[\!\left[\mathbf{u}_h\right]\!\right]-w_n\mathbf{n}_{\Gamma}.
\]
This split is important for the reduced RC local model: the concrete bulk law,
the embedded steel transfer and the future cohesive law can remain independent
modules.  It is also the kinematic equivalence bridge needed to compare a
Timoshenko structural hinge, a smeared-crack solid, and a future strong-
discontinuity solid without hiding the discontinuity work inside an ad hoc
material parameter.

The third XFEM step now adds that future interface law as a tested constitutive
contract, still outside the global assembler.  The header
\texttt{src/xfem/CohesiveCrackLaw.hh} implements a bilinear cohesive
traction--separation law in the spirit of the fictitious-crack model for
concrete \cite{HillerborgModeerPetersson1976} and the fracture-energy
regularization logic also used by crack-band models
\cite{BazantOh1983CrackBand}.  With crack jump split into
\((w_n,\mathbf{w}_t)\), the effective separation is
\[
  \kappa =
  \max_{\tau\le t}
  \left(
  \left\langle w_n(\tau)\right\rangle_+^2+
  \beta^2\|\mathbf{w}_t(\tau)\|^2
  \right)^{1/2},
\]
and the bilinear damage variable is
\[
  d(\kappa)=
  \begin{cases}
    0, & \kappa\le \delta_0,\\[2mm]
    \displaystyle
    \frac{\delta_f(\kappa-\delta_0)}
         {\kappa(\delta_f-\delta_0)}, &
    \delta_0 < \kappa < \delta_f,\\[3mm]
    1, & \kappa\ge\delta_f,
  \end{cases}
  \qquad
  \delta_0=\frac{f_t}{K_n},\qquad
  \delta_f=\frac{2G_f}{f_t}.
\]
The normal traction uses degraded opening stiffness in tension and a separate
contact stiffness in compression,
\[
  t_n =
  \begin{cases}
    (1-d)K_n w_n, & w_n\ge 0,\\
    K_c w_n, & w_n < 0,
  \end{cases}
\]
while the tangential branch keeps the same retained-shear law used by the
smeared crack-band branch,
\[
  \mathbf{t}_t =
  \eta_{\mathrm{eff}}(w_n,\mathbf{w}_t,d)\,K_t\mathbf{w}_t,\qquad
  \eta_{\mathrm{eff}}\ge 1-d .
\]
This is deliberately not promoted as a calibrated RC concrete law yet.  Its
role is architectural: the future XFEM/DG local model can consume the same
crack-opening vector exported by \texttt{XFEMEnrichedApproximation.hh}, update
history through \(\kappa\), and return interface tractions without touching the
smeared continuum material or embedded-bar coupling.

The stabilization break added one more guardrail to keep the smeared and XFEM
paths semantically aligned.  The common header
\texttt{src/xfem/CrackKinematics.hh} centralizes the normal/tangential jump
split, the effective mixed-mode separation, and the crack-band conversion
\[
  w_{\mathrm{cb}} = \langle \kappa-\kappa_0\rangle_+\,l_c .
\]
The crack-transfer surface itself was then moved to
\texttt{src/fracture/CrackShearTransferLaw.hh}.  Smeared crack-band concrete
and the XFEM cohesive law now call the same retained-shear function, including
the opening-exponential and compression-gated variants.  This avoids a
dangerous split in semantics where a Gauss point and a cohesive interface would
both report ``retained shear'' but evolve it from different hidden variables.
The cohesive law now exposes a
\texttt{make\_crack\_band\_consistent\_cohesive\_law} factory, mapping the
smeared parameters to \(K_n=E_c/l_c\), \(K_t=G_c/l_c\), \(f_t\), \(G_f\), and
\(\eta_s\).  Conversely, \texttt{CyclicCrackBandConcrete3D} now exposes the
objective equivalent opening and the active retained shear fraction directly
from its committed state.  These are small API changes, but they are important:
the next continuum benchmark can compare a smeared Gauss point and a future
XFEM interface by the same physical variables instead of by unrelated internal
damage scalars.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_structural_continuum_hysteresis_overlay_corotational_cyclic_crack_band_concrete_ts0p1_4x4x4_bias1p5_cap_lateral_translation_only_struct_clamped_n4_e6_lobatto_200mm.png}
  \end{minipage}\hfill
  \begin{minipage}{0.48\textwidth}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_structural_continuum_steel_hinge_band_hysteresis_corotational_cyclic_crack_band_concrete_ts0p1_4x4x4_bias1p5_cap_lateral_translation_only_struct_clamped_n4_e6_lobatto_200mm.png}
  \end{minipage}
  \caption{Full \(200\,\mathrm{mm}\) cyclic gate for the cheap degrading-host
  continuum against the guided-top multi-element structural reference.  Left:
  global base-shear hysteresis.  Right: fixed-end hinge-band steel hysteresis.
  The steel transfer chain is now credible, while the global peak shear remains
  too large and points to the concrete crack/shear-transfer law.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-cyclic-crack-band-200mm-clamped}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Hex20/Hex27 escalation and PETSc linear-solver audit}

The high-order continuum branch is now instrumented by the runtime manifest.
Every run records a \texttt{discretization} block with domain nodes, host and
rebar element counts, embedded-node count, PETSc global degrees of freedom and
the allocated/used nonzeros of the tangent matrix. This is necessary because
changing \(\texttt{Hex8}\) to \(\texttt{Hex20}\) or \(\texttt{Hex27}\) changes
the graph connectivity as much as it changes the interpolation order.

Zero-amplitude setup probes on the same
\(\texttt{6x6x12}\), \(\texttt{cover\_aligned}\),
\(\texttt{longitudinal\_bias\_power}=2\) mesh give:
\[
\begin{array}{l|r|r|r}
\text{host} & n_{\mathrm{dof}} & \mathrm{nnz}(K) &
t_{\mathrm{setup/zero}}[\mathrm{s}]\\
\hline
\texttt{Hex8} & 1987 & 108783 & 23.6\\
\texttt{Hex20}+\text{ three-node bars} & 6979 & 906447 & 112.0\\
\texttt{Hex27}+\text{ three-node bars} & 12559 & 1953579 & 119.2
\end{array}
\]
Thus the high-order switch is available and auditable, but it is not a cheap
drop-in replacement for the promoted \(\texttt{Hex8}\) branch.

The first nonlinear high-order probe used
\(\texttt{Hex20}\;4\times4\times8\), fixed-end longitudinal bias, three-node
bars, the tensile crack-band host proxy and a monotonic
\(50\,\mathrm{mm}\) target. The LU policies completed with identical physical
response:
\[
\begin{array}{l|c|c|c}
\text{policy} & \text{status} & t_{\mathrm{solve}}[\mathrm{s}] &
\text{linear evidence}\\
\hline
\texttt{newton-l2-only} & \text{completed} & 182.6 &
\texttt{KSPPREONLY+PCLU}\\
\texttt{newton-l2-lu-symbolic-reuse-only} & \text{completed} & 184.0 &
\texttt{RCM+reuse ordering/fill}\\
\texttt{newton-l2-gmres-ilu1-only} & \text{failed} & 515.2 &
\texttt{GMRES}~1000~\text{iterations}
\end{array}
\]
The failed iterative case is useful evidence rather than a patching target:
the PETSc reason is \(\texttt{KSP\_DIVERGED\_ITS}\), so the current
penalty-coupled tangent is too ill-conditioned for a simple ILU(1)
preconditioner. Relaxing nonlinear tolerances would hide the problem. The next
scalable route should instead audit penalty scaling, block storage and stronger
preconditioners before promoting \(\texttt{Hex20}\) or \(\texttt{Hex27}\)
\(6\times6\times12\) to the full cyclic validation.

\subsection{Hex8 fixed-end adaptive longitudinal refinement}

The next refinement pass returned to \(\texttt{Hex8}\) and refined only in the
longitudinal direction near the fixed end. This is the physically relevant
direction for the present benchmark because the plastic hinge, crack opening
and largest steel strains are localized near the empotramiento; refining
uniformly far from that region mostly increases the algebraic cost.

The first aggressive candidate,
\(\texttt{Hex8}\;6\times6\times18\) with
\(\texttt{longitudinal\_bias\_power}=2.5\), placed the first steel Gauss
points at approximately \(0.49,1.84,4.62,10.88\,\mathrm{mm}\). It also reduced
the fixed-end crack-band characteristic length to
\(2.33\,\mathrm{mm}\). The full \(200\,\mathrm{mm}\) cyclic run did not finish
within the long-run window. This is useful evidence: concentrating points near
the base is necessary, but over-concentrating them can make the local
softening/penalty problem computationally prohibitive.

Keeping \(\texttt{bias}=2\) and increasing only \(n_z\) was more stable:
\[
\begin{array}{l|c|r|r|r|r}
\text{case} & \text{status} & t_{\mathrm{solve}}[\mathrm{s}] &
|V|_{\max}[\mathrm{kN}] & |\sigma_s|_{\max}[\mathrm{MPa}] &
|\varepsilon_s|_{\max}\\
\hline
\texttt{6x6x12 bias=2} & \text{completed} & 1345.6 & 31.1 & 586.3 & 8.53\times10^{-2}\\
\texttt{6x6x16 bias=2} & \text{completed} & 3053.8 & 29.1 & 558.3 & 7.13\times10^{-2}\\
\texttt{6x6x20 bias=2} & \text{timeout} & >18000 & - & - & -
\end{array}
\]
The \(\texttt{6x6x16 bias=2}\) case is therefore the current completed
high-resolution candidate. It is not declared the final mesh-converged local
model; rather, it is the most refined completed baseline before the present
solver and crack-band regularization chain becomes impractical. The
\(\texttt{6x6x20}\) and \(\texttt{bias}=2.5\) frontiers should be revisited
after the penalty scaling, block storage/preconditioning and characteristic
length policy have been audited further.

The frozen summary is:
\[
\texttt{data/output/cyclic\_validation/reboot\_continuum\_hex8\_fixed\_end\_refinement\_audit/hex8\_fixed\_end\_refinement\_summary.json}.
\]

\subsection{Continuum kinematic policy selection}

The continuum reduced-column executable now exposes the kinematic policy as a
resolved input rather than as an implicit compile-time assumption:
\[
\texttt{--continuum-kinematics }
\{\texttt{small-strain},\texttt{total-lagrangian},
\texttt{updated-lagrangian},\texttt{corotational}\}.
\]
The baseline remains \(\texttt{small-strain}\) for reproducibility with the
previous validation artifacts, but the same benchmark surface can now dispatch
to \(\texttt{total-lagrangian}\), \(\texttt{updated-lagrangian}\), and a
partial \(\texttt{corotational}\) solid path. This is important for both
scientific and software reasons. Scientifically, the
large-drift local column should not make Newton pay for a rigid-body rotation
that the material should never see. For a rigid rotation \(F=R\),
\[
    E = \frac{1}{2}(F^T F-I)
      = \frac{1}{2}(R^T R-I)
      = 0,
\]
so the Total Lagrangian Green--Lagrange carrier removes that spurious
contribution exactly. Architecturally, the choice is now carried by the
\texttt{RunSpec} and serialized in the runtime manifest, so a future
Python/Julia wrapper can select the same policy without depending on ad hoc
driver knowledge.

The implementation keeps the hot path templated by the policy:
\[
\texttt{ContinuumElement<ThreeDimensionalMaterial,3,KinematicPolicy>},
\]
with a small runtime dispatcher at the benchmark boundary. The TL and UL
policies also expose a linearized reference-geometry \(B\)-matrix seed so the
generic model setup path can assemble an initial stiffness without knowing the
nonlinear policy internals. Nonlinear residuals and tangents still come from
the policy-specific \(\texttt{evaluate()}\) route.

The corotational branch follows the classical polar split used in
Crisfield/Felippa-style corotational formulations:
\[
    F = R U,\qquad
    \hat e = \operatorname{sym}(R^T F)-I.
\]
The constitutive model sees \(\hat e\) in the corotated frame, while the stress
and tangent are rotated back to the assembly frame before forming the residual
and stiffness. The present tangent freezes \(R\) during linearization, so this
is intentionally classified as a partial runtime branch rather than as the
reference finite-kinematics solid formulation. The missing
\(\partial R/\partial u\) contribution remains the next audit item.

A minimal elastic \(\texttt{Hex8}\;1\times1\times1\) smoke case confirms that
the selectable policies agree in the small-displacement limit:
\[
\begin{array}{l|c|r|r}
\text{policy} & \text{status} & |V|_{\max}[\mathrm{MN}] &
t_{\mathrm{solve}}[\mathrm{s}]\\
\hline
\texttt{small-strain} & \text{completed} & 0.13247 & 0.145\\
\texttt{total-lagrangian} & \text{completed} & 0.13247 & 0.423\\
\texttt{updated-lagrangian} & \text{completed} & 0.13247 & 0.390\\
\texttt{corotational} & \text{completed, partial} & 0.13247 & 0.492
\end{array}
\]
The \(\texttt{corotational}\) option is therefore executable, but still carries
a finite-kinematics disclaimer in the audit matrix. It is useful for the
validation campaign because it isolates rigid-spin filtering, but the thesis
should continue to treat TL as the reference continuum-solid finite
kinematics path until the consistent tangent and larger-amplitude evidence are
closed.

\subsection{External continuum bridge with OpenSeesPy}

The continuum stage also needs an external computational lens.  The structural
OpenSees bridge already showed that a third implementation can expose mistakes
in signs, units, protocol semantics and material-history projection.  The
same idea is now applied to the local 3D continuum column through
\texttt{scripts/opensees\_reduced\_rc\_column\_continuum\_reference.py}.

The OpenSeesPy model is deliberately conservative.  It uses a conforming solid
mesh with \texttt{stdBrick}, \texttt{bbarBrick}, or \texttt{SSPbrick}; concrete
is selected among \texttt{ElasticIsotropic} and \texttt{ASDConcrete3D}; and
the eight longitudinal bars are OpenSees \texttt{truss} elements that share
nodes with the brick mesh.  A \texttt{Damage2p} route is kept parameterized in
the script because it is physically attractive for degradation studies, but
the locally verified OpenSeesPy~3.8.0 build rejects it at material declaration
time with \texttt{material type Damage2p is unknown}. It is therefore not used
as numerical evidence in this pass. This is not the
same discrete statement as the current \texttt{fall\_n} embedded-bar route.
The bridge is therefore not promoted as numerical truth.  Its role is to ask
whether an independently assembled conforming continuum has the same
macroscopic tendencies as the \texttt{fall\_n} local continuum under the same
declared section, axial load, and lateral displacement protocol.

The top-face displacement is imposed by OpenSees \texttt{sp} constraints driven
by a \texttt{Path TimeSeries}.  This keeps the analysis pseudo-time monotone
even when the prescribed displacement reverses.  The axial compression is
distributed over the top face by tributary surface weights, and the support
resultant is recovered from the summed base reactions.  These choices mirror
the lessons learned from the structural bridge: benchmark time and protocol
time must be explicit metadata, and support resultants must be mechanical
resultants rather than accidental nodal-force readouts.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=bash,caption={Paired continuum \texttt{fall\_n}/OpenSeesPy benchmark used as an external 3D-solid control. The runner can use Python~3.11 for plotting while launching OpenSeesPy with Python~3.12.},label={lst:validation-reboot-continuum-external-paired-benchmark}]
py -3.11 scripts/run_reduced_rc_continuum_external_benchmark.py \
  --output-dir data/output/cyclic_validation/continuum_external_pair_cyclic_10mm \
  --python-launcher "py -3.12" \
  --analysis cyclic \
  --falln-material-mode cyclic-crack-band-concrete \
  --opensees-concrete-model ASDConcrete3D \
  --opensees-steel-model Steel02 \
  --solid-element SSPbrick \
  --nx 4 --ny 4 --nz 4 \
  --longitudinal-bias-power 1.3 \
  --amplitudes-mm 10 \
  --steps-per-segment 1 \
  --reversal-substep-factor 1 \
  --axial-compression-mn 0.02 \
  --axial-preload-steps 2 \
  --max-bisections 6
\end{lstlisting}

The first short cyclic bridge completes on both sides.  After explicitly
aligning the reversal-substep factor between both codes, the
\(4\times4\times4\) slice reports \(26.60\,\mathrm{s}\) in \texttt{fall\_n}
and \(1.16\,\mathrm{s}\) in OpenSeesPy up to \(10\,\mathrm{mm}\).  The same
surface also completes at \(20\,\mathrm{mm}\), with \(40.05\,\mathrm{s}\) in
\texttt{fall\_n} and \(2.31\,\mathrm{s}\) in OpenSeesPy.  This timing gap is
recorded as evidence, not yet as a general efficiency claim: the
\texttt{fall\_n} route includes its embedded-bar/degrading-host machinery, and
the OpenSees route is a conforming comparison control.

The postprocessor aligns histories by actual imposed drift rather than by step
index, because \texttt{fall\_n} persists accepted continuation substeps while
OpenSeesPy stores only the requested protocol points.  With that alignment,
the short cyclic case gives a maximum base-shear difference of about
\(1.11\,\mathrm{kN}\), while the axial reaction closes to roundoff
\((1.15\times10^{-13}\,\mathrm{MN})\).  The remaining open item is the local
steel history: the matched-drift maximum stress-envelope difference is about
\(31.9\,\mathrm{MPa}\). At \(20\,\mathrm{mm}\), the maximum base-shear
difference grows to \(7.56\,\mathrm{kN}\), and the maximum steel-envelope
difference grows to \(99.2\,\mathrm{MPa}\), while the axial reaction remains
closed to roundoff.

The OpenSeesPy \(\texttt{ASDConcrete3D}\) route also needed a continuation
correction.  The \(\texttt{sp-path}\) control now uses a convergence-profile
cascade and adaptive pseudo-time bisection while preserving the prescribed
motion on the whole top face.  With that correction, the external continuum
bridge reaches \(20\,\mathrm{mm}\).  At \(50\,\mathrm{mm}\), \texttt{fall\_n}
completes the full cycle in \(56.43\,\mathrm{s}\) and reaches
\(|\sigma_s|_{\max}\approx 420.5\,\mathrm{MPa}\), while OpenSeesPy fails during
the post-peak negative unloading branch when trying to return toward
\(-40\,\mathrm{mm}\).  The paired runner therefore keeps partial CSV and SVG
artifacts under the explicit status \texttt{failed\_with\_partial\_artifacts}.
This narrows the next audit from a vague ``continuum mismatch'' to the
host--bar coupling, local steel-history projection, and the external
comparator's own unloading frontier.

The plotting workflow is intentionally split across Python interpreters on the
current Windows workstation: Python~3.11 provides \texttt{matplotlib}, whereas
Python~3.12 provides the working OpenSeesPy binary.  The paired runner can
therefore be launched with Python~3.11 while its OpenSees child process is
kept on Python~3.12.  This is not just convenience; it keeps solver
availability and postprocessing availability from becoming the same failure
mode.

The paired runner now also declares the physical model controls explicitly:
host-only versus reinforced models, fall\_n embedded bars versus OpenSees
conforming bars, concrete zoning, and lateral-control mode. This prevents the
matrix from mixing models that are visually similar but physically different.

An elastic host-only \(50\,\mathrm{mm}\) control, with no steel in either code,
clarified the role of the external solid element. On the biased
\(4\times4\times4\) mesh, \(\texttt{stdBrick}\) gives an RMS base-shear
difference of \(9.87\,\mathrm{kN}\) \((9.10\%)\), \(\texttt{bbarBrick}\) gives
\(9.08\,\mathrm{kN}\) \((8.44\%)\), and \(\texttt{SSPbrick}\) gives
\(65.30\,\mathrm{kN}\) \((232.85\%)\). Thus \(\texttt{SSPbrick}\) is much too
soft to serve as an elastic stiffness-parity control. When elastic steel is
activated in both models with \(\texttt{bbarBrick}\), the base-shear error
stays essentially unchanged \((9.08\,\mathrm{kN}, 7.96\%)\) and the RMS
maximum steel-stress difference is only \(0.495\,\mathrm{MPa}\). This confines
the remaining nonlinear gap to the concrete law, crack regularization and
localization compatibility rather than to a trivial steel area, position or
basic coupling error.

The nonlinear \(\texttt{ASDConcrete3D}\) comparison then flips the practical
recommendation: at \(20\,\mathrm{mm}\), \(\texttt{SSPbrick}\) preserves axial
reaction and gives the smallest global error, while \(\texttt{stdBrick}\) and
\(\texttt{bbarBrick}\) produce larger residual unloading offsets and
steel-envelope differences. The external continuum bridge should therefore
keep both controls: one elastic stiffness/kinematics control with
\(\texttt{stdBrick}/\texttt{bbarBrick}\), and one nonlinear robustness/trend
control with \(\texttt{SSPbrick}\) until a locally available, better
degrading 3D concrete material is identified.

The amplitude gate was then extended to \(200\,\mathrm{mm}\). In the
elastic-with-steel control, \(\texttt{bbarBrick}\) completes the full
\(50,100,150,200\,\mathrm{mm}\) cyclic protocol on both solvers.  \texttt{fall\_n}
reports \(137.23\,\mathrm{s}\) on the refined control, OpenSeesPy reports
\(396.87\,\mathrm{s}\), the RMS base-shear difference is
\(13.87\,\mathrm{kN}\) \((7.58\%)\), and the RMS maximum
steel-stress-envelope difference is \(3.50\,\mathrm{MPa}\). This
large-displacement elastic control keeps the host--bar chain and support
resultant convention inside the same tolerance band observed at smaller
amplitudes, now with the refined external mesh promoted below.

The nonlinear external branch is a frontier rather than a closed comparison.
With \(\texttt{SSPbrick}\) and \(\texttt{ASDConcrete3D}\), \texttt{fall\_n}
completes the coarse history up to \(200\,\mathrm{mm}\) in
\(413.73\,\mathrm{s}\), reaches \(|\sigma_s|_{\max}\approx435.0\,\mathrm{MPa}\),
and keeps host--bar kinematic gaps around \(10^{-10}\,\mathrm{m}\). OpenSeesPy
stores only four hysteresis points and fails when targeting \(+100\,\mathrm{mm}\)
after reaching \(50\,\mathrm{mm}\), with a singular tangent and \texttt{NaN}
residuals. Figure~\ref{fig:validation-reboot-continuum-external-200mm-panel}
therefore separates two facts: the elastic external control reaches
\(200\,\mathrm{mm}\) on both solvers, while the nonlinear OpenSees comparator
is not robust enough, in this material/element configuration, to judge the
large-amplitude \texttt{fall\_n} branch.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/continuum_external_hysteresis_200mm_panel.png}
  \caption{External continuum hysteresis comparison at \(200\,\mathrm{mm}\).
  Left: elastic host plus steel control, completed by both solvers. Right:
  nonlinear \(\texttt{ASDConcrete3D}\) frontier; \texttt{fall\_n} completes
  the full protocol while OpenSeesPy stops after the \(50\,\mathrm{mm}\)
  branch.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-continuum-external-200mm-panel}
\end{figure}

A focused elastic-with-steel mesh-refinement control was then run to decide
how much trust to place in the OpenSees continuum reference itself.  The
coarse existing \(\texttt{bbarBrick}\) control at \(50\,\mathrm{mm}\) had an
RMS base-shear gap of \(9.08\,\mathrm{kN}\).  The refined request
\(6\times6\times12\), with the transverse coordinates forced to include the
eight bar locations, becomes an actual OpenSees mesh with \(1053\) nodes,
\(768\) solid elements, and \(96\) conforming truss segments; on that mesh the
same gap falls to \(3.49\,\mathrm{kN}\), while the steel-stress RMS gap remains
small at \(0.76\,\mathrm{MPa}\).  A direct \(\texttt{stdBrick}\) rerun on the
same refined mesh gives \(3.73\,\mathrm{kN}\), so the residual is no longer
dominated by the \(\texttt{stdBrick}\) versus \(\texttt{bbarBrick}\) choice.
At \(200\,\mathrm{mm}\), the refined \(\texttt{bbarBrick}\) control reduces
the RMS base-shear gap from \(25.88\,\mathrm{kN}\) to
\(13.87\,\mathrm{kN}\), and reduces the steel-stress RMS gap from
\(6.99\,\mathrm{MPa}\) to \(3.50\,\mathrm{MPa}\).  An attempted
\(8\times8\times16\) \(\texttt{bbarBrick}\) OpenSees-only run exceeded the
twenty-minute diagnostic budget without producing a manifest, so the
\(6\times6\times12\) control is promoted as the current refined external
reference, not as a final mesh-convergence proof.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.92\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/continuum_external_mesh_refinement_summary.png}
  \caption{Focused OpenSees mesh-refinement control for the elastic
  continuum-with-steel benchmark.  Refinement substantially reduces the
  \texttt{fall\_n}/OpenSees base-shear residual, while the
  \(\texttt{stdBrick}\) versus \(\texttt{bbarBrick}\) difference on the
  refined mesh is much smaller than the remaining cross-code gap.  The
  \(8\times8\times16\) point is recorded as a cost frontier rather than as a
  completed data point.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-continuum-external-mesh-refinement}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Structural--continuum kinematic-equivalence gate}

The next validation pass deliberately separated boundary kinematics from
material tuning.  The continuum top face was already prescribing a uniform
lateral displacement, but the axial displacement field over that same face was
not being measured.  The benchmark now records the top-face lateral range,
axial range, an affine fit \(u_z = a + b_x x + b_y y\), and the corresponding
small-rotation estimates
\[
  \theta_x \simeq \frac{\partial u_z}{\partial y}, \qquad
  \theta_y \simeq -\frac{\partial u_z}{\partial x}.
\]
This made the previous ambiguity visible.  The
\texttt{lateral\_translation\_only} continuum cap is not equivalent to a
strict guided beam end: at \(50\,\mathrm{mm}\) it develops an effective
\(\theta_y\approx 2.03\times 10^{-2}\,\mathrm{rad}\), while the uniform axial
penalty cap drives \(\theta_y\) down to about
\(6.2\times10^{-9}\,\mathrm{rad}\).  The latter is kinematically clean but
much too stiff for the current structural reference, so the binary
\texttt{free}/\texttt{clamped} structural comparison is no longer sufficient.

The structural benchmark therefore gained a third, explicit top-rotation
policy,
\[
  \theta_y = r\,\frac{\Delta}{L},
\]
exposed through
\texttt{--top-bending-rotation-drift-ratio}.  This is not a material
calibration knob; it is a kinematic audit seam that lets the structural slice
match a measured or intended continuum top-section contract.  A cheap
structural-only sweep against the fixed \(50\,\mathrm{mm}\) continuum response
identified \(r=0.5\) as the best first bridge among the tested values:
the peak-normalized RMS base-shear error dropped to about \(0.124\), compared
with \(0.250\) for the strictly guided structural end.  The same full
structural--continuum replay at \(50\,\mathrm{mm}\) gave a global
peak-normalized RMS error of \(0.129\) and a local steel stress RMS of
\(0.099\).

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.92\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_top_rotation_ratio_sweep_lateral_only_50mm.png}
  \caption{Top-rotation kinematic sweep against the fixed continuum response
  at \(50\,\mathrm{mm}\).  The best tested bridge is neither the free nor the
  guided endpoint, but an intermediate section rotation
  \(\theta_y \approx 0.5\,\Delta/L\).}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-top-rotation-ratio-sweep-50mm}
\end{figure}

The \(100\,\mathrm{mm}\) replay remains operational with the same
\(r=0.5\) policy.  It is more nonlinear: the global RMS rises to about
\(0.356\), while the local steel stress RMS remains about \(0.116\) and the
hinge-band steel work ratio is \(0.772\).  This is useful evidence rather than
a failure: the steel transfer and kinematic bridge are now credible, but the
host crack-band response still dissipates too little local steel work and
does not yet reproduce the structural plastic-hinge energy over larger cycles.

A subsequent guardrail made the kinematic issue sharper.  A new continuum
top-cap mode,
\texttt{affine\_bending\_rotation\_penalty\_cap}, ties the axial displacement
of the active top-face nodes to an affine section plane,
\[
  u_z^I-u_z^A =
  \theta_x\,(y_I-y_A)-\theta_y\,(x_I-x_A),
\]
where \(A\) is the selected top-face anchor.  The ratio \(r\) used by the
structural audit can now be imposed directly on the continuum cap through
\[
  \theta_y = r\,\frac{\Delta}{L}.
\]
This test is deliberately not a new calibration path: it is a boundary-condition
truth test.  At \(50\,\mathrm{mm}\), using
\(r=0.5\), the continuum top-face fit reports
\(\theta_y=7.8125\times10^{-3}\,\mathrm{rad}\), as intended, with an axial
plane residual of \(1.6\times10^{-10}\,\mathrm{m}\).  The global comparison
then becomes much worse, however: the peak-normalized RMS base-shear error is
\(0.725\) and the continuum peak base shear is about
\(0.088\,\mathrm{MN}\), roughly twice the corresponding structural envelope.
Therefore the favorable \texttt{lateral\_translation\_only} replay must be read
as a warping-admissible comparison branch, not as a strict plane-section
kinematic closure.  The remaining closure target is now more precise: reconcile
the three-dimensional crack/localization field with a structural plastic-hinge
surrogate under an explicitly declared top-section contract.

The complementary affine test used \(r=1.28\), approximately the effective
rotation ratio measured on the previous \texttt{lateral\_translation\_only}
continuum run.  This reproduces the top rotation
\(\theta_y\approx2.0\times10^{-2}\,\mathrm{rad}\) with a plane residual below
\(2\times10^{-11}\,\mathrm{m}\), and the continuum peak base shear returns to
about \(0.042\,\mathrm{MN}\).  The structural reference with the same imposed
ratio is no longer an energy-equivalent plastic hinge, however: the
peak-normalized RMS base-shear error is still \(0.484\), and the continuum
hinge-band steel work is about \(10.9\) times the structural value.  This
rules out a simple ``choose the right top rotation'' explanation.  The
continuum and structural models must next be reconciled through localization
length, crack-transfer memory, or an enriched/discrete crack representation,
not through boundary-condition tuning alone.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.92\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_structural_continuum_hysteresis_overlay_corot_ccbc_ts0p1_sr0p2_oexp_oct0p05_4x4x4_bias2_both_ends_cap_affrot_r0p5_struct_drift_r0p5_n4_e6_lobatto_50mm.png}
  \caption{Affine top-cap guardrail at \(50\,\mathrm{mm}\).  The continuum cap
  now enforces the same imposed section rotation as the structural benchmark,
  which exposes the residual material/localization gap that had been partly
  hidden by the warping-admissible \texttt{lateral\_translation\_only}
  comparison.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-affine-top-cap-guardrail-50mm}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.92\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/reduced_rc_structural_continuum_hysteresis_overlay_corotational_cyclic_crack_band_concrete_ts0p1_4x4x4_bias1p5_cap_lateral_translation_only_struct_drift-ratio_r0p5_n4_e6_lobatto_100mm.png}
  \caption{Inelastic structural--continuum replay at \(100\,\mathrm{mm}\)
  using the intermediate top-rotation policy
  \(\theta_y=0.5\,\Delta/L\).  The comparison is now a kinematic-equivalence
  test rather than a free/clamped endpoint comparison.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-struct-cont-ratio-100mm}
\end{figure}

The \(200\,\mathrm{mm}\) structural replay exposed the next solver/material
frontier.  With the same ratio and a refined structural protocol, the run
stops around \(-113\,\mathrm{mm}\) during reverse loading.  The imposed drift
and rotation are exactly satisfied, and the last residual is still of order
\(10^{-8}\), but PETSc exits through the maximum-iteration path.  The nonlinear
solve policy now has an auditable small-residual acceptance seam for
\texttt{SNES\_DIVERGED\_MAX\_IT}; nevertheless this particular branch remains
just outside the conservative acceptance band.  The next large-amplitude task
is therefore not another blind material tweak, but a targeted continuation
study for the structural plastic-hinge reversal and the corresponding crack
closure/degradation memory in the continuum host.

\subsection{Stabilization break: wrapper surface and future XFEM/DG seam}

This stabilization pass also exposed a software-design issue that is easy to
miss when the focus stays only on nonlinear response curves. The reduced-column
benchmarks were already scientifically useful, but their \emph{entry surface}
was still too implicit: the real contract lived partly in CLI flags, partly in
Python orchestration scripts, and partly in whatever each
\texttt{runtime\_manifest.json} happened to serialize. That is acceptable for
internal iteration, but it is not the right foundation for either a future
Python/Julia wrapper or a future second local-model family.

The architectural correction is intentionally modest but structural. The code
now carries:
\begin{itemize}
  \item a typed benchmark-surface descriptor in
    \texttt{src/validation/ReducedRCColumnBenchmarkSurface.hh}; and
  \item a lightweight local-model taxonomy in
    \texttt{src/analysis/LocalModelTaxonomy.hh}.
\end{itemize}
The first records that the current material, section, structural and continuum
reduced-column benchmarks expose a \emph{stable manifest contract} and a
resolved input block that a future wrapper can target intentionally. The
second records, in code, which local family is being solved: uniaxial
constitutive point, structural section surrogate, promoted continuum
smeared-crack host with interior embedded bars, explicit comparison controls
such as boundary bars, or only future extension routes such as XFEM/DG-like
local fracture solvers.

This matters scientifically because the local model is not just ``another
implementation detail''. The reduced local continuum is already being prepared
as the future microscale workhorse, and therefore its identity must stay
explicit. For the current promoted branch, the nonlinear local equilibrium may
still be read schematically as
\[
  \mathcal{R}_h(\mathbf{u}_h,\mathbf{q}_h;\lambda)=\mathbf{0},
\]
where \(\mathbf{u}_h\) denotes the host displacement field and
\(\mathbf{q}_h\) the internal constitutive history carried by the smeared
fixed-crack concrete law together with the embedded steel state. A future XFEM
local model would enlarge that approximation space, for example through a
partition-of-unity enrichment of the form \cite{BelytschkoBlack1999,MoesDolbowBelytschko1999}
\[
  \mathbf{u}_h(\mathbf{x}) =
  \sum_{I} N_I(\mathbf{x})\,\mathbf{u}_I
  +
  \sum_{J \in \mathcal{E}} N_J(\mathbf{x})\,H(\phi(\mathbf{x}))\,\mathbf{a}_J
  + \cdots,
\]
where \(H(\phi)\) introduces a strong-discontinuity field driven by a crack
geometry \(\phi\). A DG/HDG path would move the discontinuity representation to
element interfaces or hybrid traces, changing both the primal unknowns and the
effective-operator extraction route instead of merely adding enriched nodal
coefficients \cite{DemkowiczGopalakrishnan2010}.

The XFEM route is now kept alive by a concrete, tested mask contract rather
than by prose alone. The \texttt{xfem\_enrichment} regression checks that a
plane crack near the base of a two-cell 3D column enriches only the cut cell
and does not propagate enriched degrees of freedom into the healthy upper
cell. Figure~\ref{fig:validation-reboot-xfem-base-crack-candidate} records the
same idea as a validation artifact: the future XFEM local model should pay for
extra displacement support only where the crack geometry requires it.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.58\textwidth]{figures/validation_reboot/xfem_column_base_crack_candidate.png}
  \caption{Minimal XFEM enrichment contract for the local RC-column model: a
  base crack cuts only the lower cell, so only its nodes receive enriched
  displacement support while the upper region stays on the standard continuum
  space.}
  \label{fig:validation-reboot-xfem-base-crack-candidate}
\end{figure}

The first quantitative XFEM benchmark has now been added as a deliberately
limited local cohesive-hinge surrogate,
\texttt{fall\_n\_reduced\_rc\_xfem\_reference\_benchmark}.  It uses the
existing shifted-Heaviside crack kinematics and bilinear cohesive law, places
one explicit crack plane at the fixed end, and lets the eight longitudinal
Menegotto--Pinto steel bars cross that plane.  The equivalent base shear is
computed from the crack-plane normal-traction moment plus any declared
crack-plane shear transfer:
\[
  V_b^{\mathrm{XFEM}} =
  \frac{1}{L}
  \left[
    \int_A t_n(\mathbf{x})\,x\,dA
    +
    \sum_b \sigma_{s,b} A_b x_b
  \right]
  +
  \int_A t_s(\mathbf{x})\,dA.
\]
This branch remains a deliberately reduced local model, but it is a real
benchmark artifact because it writes a runtime manifest, \texttt{hysteresis.csv},
\texttt{cohesive\_section\_state.csv}, and \texttt{steel\_history.csv}.  The
\(200\,\mathrm{mm}\) progressive cyclic run with the flexural crack branch
completes in about \(1.3\,\mathrm{s}\) when run without the global trial,
reaches \(|V_b|_{\max}\approx0.039\,\mathrm{MN}\), and drives the cohesive
damage variable to one.  A second sensitivity branch that activates direct
tangential slip across the crack reaches \(|V_b|_{\max}\approx0.414\,\mathrm{MN}\)
because the crack-plane shear cap saturates and dominates the response.  That
branch is intentionally retained as a guardrail rather than promoted as a
physical closure point.

The same benchmark now also carries a stronger PETSc audit for the global XFEM
path.  The model can be built directly with
\texttt{ShiftedHeavisideSolidElement}, whose local interpolation is
\[
  \mathbf{u}^h(\mathbf{x}) =
  \sum_I N_I(\mathbf{x})\,\mathbf{u}_I
  + \sum_{I\in\mathcal{E}_{\mathrm{XFEM}}}
    N_I(\mathbf{x})\,
    \bigl[H(\phi(\mathbf{x}))-H(\phi(\mathbf{x}_I))\bigr]\,
    \mathbf{a}_I .
\]
The regular volumetric residual and tangent use the corresponding enriched
\(B_{\mathrm{XFEM}}\) operator, while the distributional part is assembled on
the crack surface through the cohesive jump
\[
  \llbracket \mathbf{u} \rrbracket =
  2\sum_{I\in\mathcal{E}_{\mathrm{XFEM}}}
  N_I\,\mathbf{a}_I .
\]
In the current two-cell audit solid, the lower cell cut by the base crack
enriches eight host nodes, so those nodes carry
\([u_x,u_y,u_z,a_x,a_y,a_z]\), while the healthy upper nodes remain standard
\([u_x,u_y,u_z]\).  The resulting \texttt{PetscSection} reports six DOFs on an
enriched node and three DOFs on a standard node.  The first cut Hex8 gathers
\(48\) local unknowns, and the trial enriched residual/tangent norms are
nonzero.  This closes the global indexing and element-kernel contract:
\[
  \mathcal{I}_I =
  \{u_x,u_y,u_z\}_I
  \cup
  \begin{cases}
    \{a_x,a_y,a_z\}_I, & I \in \mathcal{E}_{\mathrm{XFEM}},\\
    \emptyset,         & I \notin \mathcal{E}_{\mathrm{XFEM}}.
  \end{cases}
\]
The benchmark now goes one step beyond this audit and writes
\texttt{global\_xfem\_newton\_hysteresis.csv}.  That artifact is a full
PETSc/SNES solve of a shifted-Heaviside reinforced solid column with a cohesive
base crack: a \(2\times2\times4\) Hex8 mesh with fixed-end longitudinal bias,
\(85\) total nodes, \(48\) elements once the reinforcement line elements are
